THE BED PAGE.
“ TVT°* not ' °^ en that I get a chance to go
I to a theatre,” said the Hawker, when
I met him one night sharpening a
match to stick in his coat as a substi-
tute for a button. “ Appears to me galleries don’t
‘ pack ’ like they used years ago. I like to see a
big, jammed, sweaty crowd' that’ll discuss a play.
Don’t mean the young fellows who babble silly
filth about the stage women; or the idiot who
acts as chorus; or the hearty man flushed with
beer and onions who sees a double meaning in
everything, and nudges you in the ribs and fills
your ear with his mouldy whiskers to tell you
that he’ll try and remember that one. By the
way, don’t remember old Swanks, do you ? Before
your time, I suppose. At any rate, they used to
have him at the Victoria. Chap I knew used to
run a ’bus after the play out to Redfern for a
shilling. ‘ Queen’s Own ’ he called her. Only
’bus—oh, yes, Swanks had charge of the gallery.
Big, sooky-looking fellow he was, with ears like
little turn-over tarts. Well, one night a lucky
digger style of chap, in lavender pants and velvet
collar, comes in during the performance. Farce
it was. ‘ Turn Him Out,’ with Charlie Burford in
it. You see, in those days they gave a show and
no mistake. A big drama or a grand opera, a
farce, and a ballet with a name lots longer than
the skirts, —with a'change of bill four or five times
week.
“ I think I ’ll have beer ! Don’t suppose this
salicylic acid they talk about will hurt a man
much. Pretty well all this sunshiney-looking
bottled honey is doctored with it, and you can’t
lick it to brighten up sick wine. Do you know in
the old days it was a regular thing at harvest
time—about December or so—for the farmers to
■supply the hands with neat rum. At least, it was
Called rum. Old chap used to work in a spirit
store near the Haymarket told me they had the
devil’s own trouble making up the stuff. Men
wouldn’t look at the real West Indy. When all
the ingredients were put in the cask, and you
started to rock it to mix ’em, long tongues of
blue flame would dart out of the bung hole. By
the time reaping was finished most of the men
would be tearing round with their arms above their
head thinking they were ’wild bullocks. Then
they’d crawl to creeks and drink ’em dry, and——
“Oh, yes, this fellow comes in and lights up a
cigar. There were no firemen in those times, so
when Swanks gets a whiff of the smoke and sees the
gleam, he flung himself straight at it. But the
smoker runs his fingers through his long, straggly
beard, and shoos Swanks off like you would a dog.
Swanks gripped the beard with his left hand,
drives the cigar into the fellow’s mouth like a nail
into a plank with the right, and while the chap
spluttered and belched smoke and steam through
the nose and eyes, Swanks kept his mouth shut
tight. But what I can’t stand in theatres nowa-
days is tli© eating. Like to see people eat hearty.
Don’t think there’s anything so pleasant or so
soothing as to lie and hear a horse munching his
feed. A sound-toothed horse, mind you ; not a
parrot-mouthed brute that gobbles and slushes
his hard tack, and gets poor as a rake on good grass,
and leaves a man with his cart
* * •* • *
“ Yes, but when everybody around you in
the theatre is guzzling and scruffling in a
covert, stealthy way with lollies and things,
the goose-pimples get that hard and sharp that
they tear your shirt. Chap alongside me to-night
—face like leather, hands waxy—fellmonger, I
suppose, had a bag of those glue-pats they patch
windows.Vith. Says to the girl with him, ‘ ’eve a
joo-joob,’ and she scuffles down in the bag and
has one. He keeps taking them and flicking
them off the palm of his hand, to be packed into
his cheek till he had a glistening lump like a blind
boil. Fruit! I missed lots of the play just
noticing in what a lot of ways people eat oranges.
Some feel a fruit all over till they come to what
seems to be the right place. Then, chawing out a
bit of peel the size of a threepenny-bit, they
absorb the juices by hideously-prolonged pro-
cesses of suction. Others just rip ’em asunder
and bury their face to the eyebrows in the pulp.
Some strip the fruit naked and eat the segments
one by one, straining the pips through their
teeth and drippling them down the coat-collars of
people ip front. Just behind me a big chap, with
a mass of hair and whiskers that made his eyes
look like those of a native cat peering out of a
log, was gulping dates. By the slushing and row
he made affyone would have thought he was
getting satisfaction and to spare out of them. And
when I looked round, all a-creep, I saw him stow-
ing the stones in his waistcoat pocket—to be
picked over on the way home, I suppose. But the
peanuts ! when a man who is ashamed or some-
thing to be seen eating them openly takes a few
in his hand the moment the lights go down, and
perspires in the effort to husk them noiselessly.
It’s worse than sleeping on chopped horsehair.
The play ? Just one of those tinted operas made
up pretty well on the lines of the patent medicines
I hawk around sometimes. Crowded so full of
diversions that what with trying to listen to the
music that reminds you of everything, and watch-
ing the gilded tommyrot, and wondering why
everybody on every stage listens for loud sounds
in the same strained way, you scarcely remember
what you ’ve seen and heard.
“ Oh, I don’t mind—the same. People who go
out to enjoy themselves are curious. The other
Saturday I took a trip to the Hawkesbury River in
an excursion boat. Magnificent day for it. Big
crowd of all sorts of people. One old man I
liked the look of was seated on a box near
the bows. Very bloodshot eyes and livery, in-
flamed appearance all over. Hair stood straight
out from his head, with a tendency to hook at the
ends. Eyebrows like prawns’ feelers. I remarked
that the sea was nice and calm. He jumped off
the box and yelled that it was just his luck.
‘ Here’s a man wearing his pancreas out and
straining his heartstrings till the strands are start-
ing Saturday after Saturday, and I’m if I can
be sick! Best thing in the world to be sick!
Why, if I could only be half as ill as that young
fellow ’ —he pointed to a youth leaning over the
rail with his face in an expression for all the
world like that of a tired ’bus horse being led from
his manger to do an extra run— 1 I’d be happy as a
sandboy ! ’ He glanced so vindictively that I re-
tired unsteadily and swiftly to the shelter of a
funnel. There I found a quartermaster, or some
sort of an adult in an infant’s cap, and explained
to him the troubles of the fiery man. ‘Tell you
what,’ said the mariner in a friendly, confidential
way, ‘ if you ever want to be sick and can’t, why a
clout aside the head with a sheep’s-pluck ’ —but just
then I noticed that all the bandsmen were blind.
Name of some institution on their caDS. Charity
or economy ? Couldn’t tell which.
“ When we got into the river, the day had spread
its tail like a peacock, and nearly everybody went
into a stuffy cabin near the keel to eat a faded
meal. Those who remained on deck clustered
densely about two men who were arguing politics
with vehement incoherence. The leader of the
govt, was a man in an imitation sealskin cap,
with a long face as amateurishly-fashioned as
that of a heathen god. His long arm clad in
alpaca wavered unsteadily under the weight of a
hand like a grab-winch. He maintained, in sup-
port of his policy, whatever it was, that he was
the father of eleven children, of which this one
present—a rather pleasant - looking girl of 20
or so—was a sample. The other man—old,
puckered, with a solitary tooth like a weed-
clad pile, a quivering upper ljp shaped like
a turtle’s beak, and with a world of shrewd-
ness in a small blinking eye, also wore
a skull cap and carried a helmet in his hand.
He impatiently waved aside the domestic achieve-
ments of the wooden-faced man, and asked if
boots cost ss. a pair and a man could not earn ss.
where was the ss. to come from? And then a
weak, underdone sort of a man overwhelmed
with whiskers tried to explain something in a ner-
vous shriek, but the old man butted at him like a
goat. The wooden-faced man meanwhile bat-
tened on to a man who was in difficulties with the
clogged stem of his pipe, bailed him into a comer,
and raved and pranced about the benefits of
eleven children and decimal coinage.
* » » *
“On the voyage back, while all the people not
otherwise engaged tripped the wobbling deck in
kitchen lancers, I found a cosy nook in the stern.
Stretched at full length on his back, and with his
eye—large, dark, and glistening like a seal’s—
fixed on a ring-bolt, a man was entertaining a
very drunken friend with an entirely new and
most picturesquely blasphemous version of, the
scriptural history of King David and the man he
put in the front of the battle. When the friend
rolled off the grating and lost interest in the story
in attending to a bruised elbow, the seal-eyed
man, without removing his gaze from the ringbolt,
commenced to talk of the geological formation of
Australia with such a swing of technical detail
that I asked if he were a geologist, or whatever
you call them. He laughed and said I No fear—
been at sea since I was a kiddy.’ But it just
shows, doesn’t' it, that you never can tell what a
man gets interested in. Look at Shandy, for
instance ; have never seen anybody with such an
appetite for watercress as Shandy had—regular
passion for it. They say that there’s a lot of iron
and stuff in it, and people get a craving for Stuff
like that just the same as horses will rush the
trough in which blacksmiths cool iron. Anyhow,
he was a long, narrow sort of chap that seemed to
have shot up into the air too far, and looked as if
he was bending right over to do it again and
better. Had an eye like an opal, and such a tract
of crinkled chin that every barber who saw it must
have thanked his lucky star Shandy did not
patronise him. And when you’d see him in one
of those little eating-houses swooping down on the
watercress like a plague locust, you could not eat for
wondering how such a mass of chin could be moved
so quickly without shaking all his joints apart.
Of course, there’s nothing much in eating water-
cress, but Shandy had a way of spinning it in the
bowl, and the herbage would pass into his mouth
in a sort of everlasting thread. I used to meet
him round about the warehouses, with a little
striped bag of studs on cards, and combs, and
soap and odds and ends. Had periodical attacks
of some wild, raging sort of religion. Last time
I saw him was in a bank, writing something on a
deposit slip. His striped bag, filled to bursting
with cheap razors, lay alongside him. When I
touched him on the shoulder he yelled like a Red
Indian. From the corner of his mouth hung a
shred of watercress. On the deposit slip he had
formally deposited in the custody of the bank his
soul, or some tommyrot like that. While I was
trying to make it out he dashed off an advertise-
ment about a gentleman of quiet tastes wanting
board in a Christian family, and signed it Quietus.
Heard last trip that he got a place, smashed all
the furniture, and cut his throat the night he
moved-in. Lot of the young chaps where I get
my stock were chaffing me to-day about the lone-
liness of a game like mine. Why, damn me, they
train them in those big places to be nothing better
than sheep-dogs. Why, look at that young Blobbs !
‘ ‘ What, did n’t I tell you ? The big ‘ house ’ was
fairly on the totter in the backwash of improvi-
dent speculation, so they naturally commenced to
Riink about retrenchment. Of course, these big
firms can’t rush a thing like that—have their
creditors round ’em like sharks in no time. In
the case of many of the young fellows, things were
pretty simple. Easy to have a lot of rules made,
so as ahy ordinary mortal can’t help breaking
them —and there you are. But this Blobbs was a
Earagon or an archangel, or something—anyhow,
e steered inside the rules so gingerly that there
was no getting him over the edge by ordinary
means. At last they packed him out here to work
off a lot of sluggish, out-of-date brushware on
commission. He arrived with a couple of port-
manteaus, bulging with resplendent clothes and
.high collars,_ and top-boots, and his heart thump-
ing with pride. He practised elocution and atti-
tudes and deportment all the way out. He felt
how he would revolutionise commercial methods.
He’d show them how to move lines; he’d get
through so swiftly that the cable would grow red
in the fervour of his indents. And it was not
until the price asked for the quarter-acre
brass-plate he had designed staggered him
that Blobbs realised that the firm had
made no provision for rent or anything of that
sort. So at last he rented a sample-room within
hailing distance of the planets. Then he hired
one of those travellers’ chignon sort of traps—
commence like a buggy and end up as a box-
waggon. It was drawn by an unnaturally tall
horse in a shrunken hide that left a lot of the car-
case exposed, and with chronically gurgling in-
sides. A boy in an unpleasantly advanced state
of neglect drove it.
“ Day by day Blobbs in his significant ap-
parel was driven from ‘house’ to ‘house,’
and day by day the heads waved him off.
At the end of a fortnight, the boy said things
in unmeasured terms, and the owner of the trap
summoned him for its hire. Then he set out on
foot to do the retails. He talked brushware and
displayed samples for weeks without turning a
hair. It seemed as if the whole city had entered
into a conspiracy against him, and that the com-
munity had firmly resolved to polish its boots
with banana-peel and send out its clothes to be
beaten like carpets, and to let its hair stand on
end for ever. It was a strict condition of Blobbs’
tenancy that no one should have fires in thebuildi
ing or remain after 6 p.m. There was nothing
else for it but to camp in the sample-room,
and to do so Blobbs had to lock himself in at
6 o’clock in the evening, and remain there quietly
without a light, while the watchman, with some
indefinable suspicion, Went his rounds with an
erratic frequency that played the very devil with
Blobbs’ heart. No' Italian regicide, jailed in an
observation cell, underwent more torture while it
lasted. If Blobbs made the least movement, even
to turn in the packing-case, in which his bed was
cunningly devised, the sound would seem to
reverberate through the building, and the watch-
man’s step would seem to hesitate near his door.
So he would lie there until his nerves stood up like
the bristles on his brushes, and through the dreamy
hours, in which he was afraid to sleep lest a snor9
should betray him, Blobbs would weave wild
schemes for moving his hairy incubus ‘into the
stream of commerce. After a few minutes’ in-
voluntary doze he would start up, his heart still,
to listen for what seemed countless hours, in fear
that his dreadful secret had been revealed. It
never occurred to him to abandon the brushware.
Everything was against that —training, instincts,
and all the rest of it. His only thought was the
interests of the firm. It would be so humiliating
to the firm if news went round that their trusted
representative slept in a packing-case in a sample-
room. *
* * * *
“ After many nights Blobbs revolted—to an ex-
tent. He went out and split his last shilling in a
sixpenny hangout at the theatre. When he came
out the streets were gleaming wet. In the door-
way of the building the watchman stood yarning
to a policeman. Robert was explaining something
that called for exuberant exposition and stepped
out into the roadway. The watchman, fascinated,
followed. This was Blobbs’ chance, and he flew
through the doorway like a wet streak. In the
dark Tie stumbled over the watchman’s tea-can,
and at the rattle both the watchman and police-
man rushed in. There was a lift in the building.
The architect, foreseeing that no one but the
watchman, who had nothing else to do, could ever
dream of using the stairs, had made that means of
ascent in precipitous zigzag flights. In a con-
vulsion of fear Blobbs tore up and up. Sometimes
his heart thumped like a steam-pump. Then it
stood still, and there were whirring and bunging
noises in his ears. The sweat poured into his eyes
and sparkled on his hands.. But he could not stop,
and he blundered to the top several flights ahead
of his pursuers, and jobbed at his keyhole. At
last he was in, the door was closed, and he
lay sobbing with his face buried in the long hair
of a hearth brush. On the landing the watchman
and the policeman altercated, puffed and gasped
broken, angry words. Blobbs heard them, growl-
ing and panting, try the doors. After a while
the policeman had misgivings or something—
thought that perhaps the thing (whatever it was)
had not come so far up. So he hastily ran down
some stairs, and fell down the remainder to the
ground-floor. , Meanwhile, the watchman lingered
on the topmost landing. Three times he com-
menced to descend the stairs. Three times he
changed his mind, and came back with a perturbed
scuffle towards the door of Blobbs, who felt as if
he were being alternately dipped in a limpid
spring and in vats of boiling fat. When, for the
fourth and what seemed from the heavy impact of
heels to be the last time, the watchman’s footsteps
retreated, Blobbs in a spasm of relief sat upright,
banging his head against a case.
“Then everything was clear to the watchman.
Heknew and felt rage—seething rage thatreddened
his vision, and urged him to tear Blobbs’ flesh. He
had been imposed upon, tricked by a miserable
creature whose ill-fortune drove him to immure
himself for 14 hours in a dreary attic where there
were not even the noises of the street to break the
monotony. He roared threats and tugged and
kicked at the door, wrenching off the knob like
ripe fruit. When the trembling Blobbs opened
the door and commenced to explain in a voice
like variations of ‘Home Sweet Home,’ the
watchman fell upon him with a whirlwind of in-
coherent reproaches. ‘Mighter goter man the
sack, yer dorg yer ! Moren a man’s billet’s worth,
yer cow yer—l ’ll chuck yer out, neck and crop ’ —
and he did. On the edge of the stairs Blobbs
made a frantic effort to maintain his dignity—for
the sake of the firm. The watchman aimed masses
of brushware at him, knocking him down the
stairs. The tumult resounded through the build-
ing. Robert heard, and dashed up the stairs. •
Shapeless things flew past him. Some struck him
with an angular aggressiveness that bruised and
blinded him. Other great masses met him with
the force of cannon balls, caressed him as lightly
as fluff, and fell to the floor with resounding
whacks. Robert became frenzied, and when a
mass of something definite rolled against him, he
fell upon it and scratched and tore, and kneaded
it with hands and knees, and head, until a huge
packing case, hurled by the watchman after the
person and chattels of Blobbs, bunted him off his
victim and carried him down several flights of
stairs.
“When his hopes were at their zenith Blobbs
had constructed with wires a sort of sample trophy
of brushware. This had stood in a corner of the
sample-room, and it was not until the watchman
commenced to sweep the place with his feet that
he discovered it. Then he dragged it to the top
of the stairs and hurled the hairy thing with
terrific violence after the rest. Blobbs pushed it
past him with the adroitness of a tradesman.
Robert, much damaged by the case, was reascend-
ing the stairs to tear the soul out of the person
unknown who had resisted arrest, when the weird
trophy bumped into him. And he was still trying
to stem it with his baton when Blobbs, stimulated
by the approach of the watchman, leaped
wildly over him and escaped to the street.
What became of Blobbs ? Oh, after days mouch-
ing round, trying to rescue the brushware, and
trying to figure out whether drowning in fresh-
water was more dignified than suicide in salt-
water, he disappeared. But I met a chap the
other day in the park. He was waiting for his
Old Age Pension. Apparently, he had about 15
years to wait, but he did n’t seem to mind that a
bit. His eyes had muddy shores, but the whites
were clear as health could make ’em. The parts
of his face not sparsely covered with a growth like
whitethorn in bloom were greasily ruddy. The
dome of his hat was mangy, and the tide of
natural pearl around the rim was at the spring.
Had a suit of mixed tweeds, rendered quite water-
proof by his own oil. He blew a cloud of ashes-
from his pipe, by way of pointing my duty.
“ ‘ Did he ever get a job ? ’ ‘No; couldn’t say
he’d ever tried. Plenty of people to do all the
work in a bit of a place like this.’ ‘He was not a
native, then?’ ‘Yes, country-bred and bom.’
Where ? ‘ Oh, jest over there ’ —and he waved his-
hand towards North Shore. But did n’t he ever
get run-in by the police ? ‘ Sometimes, not
often. Y’see, I chucks me whole weight on a
copman, and, when wonst he’s took me single-
handed, he’s had enough, see ! ’ It was not hard
to realise that the constable who would undertake
a second journey under the weight of that un-
savoury parcel of blubber must either be a zealot
or an ex-carcase-delivery-man.
* * * *
“Beginning to feel fowl-housy, I suggested
that he should go and get a drink. He took the
coin, and lolled back on the seat. Wasn’t he
going ? ‘ P’raps, but it was a long way up to a
pub.’ I pointed out that it no doubt was, up that
path, but could n’t he go down there (to Woolloo-
mooloo). Yes, he did n’t mind that so much ;it
Bloped a lot. And he languidly arose and moved
off, taking the fullest possible advantage of the cir-
cumstance that his boots were heelless. Did it
ever strike you that a chap like that is
a veritable masterpiece of nature. He is con-
stitutionally a reservoir of animal heat. He
wastes (in the sense of energy) nothing. Every-
thing he absorbs is converted into fat. Disease
germs that would riot in the blood and tissue of a
man less generously endowed are drowned in
boiling oil when they attempt to attack him.
The grease that oozes from every pore of his skin
repels predatory insects. He is impervious to
moisture. His joints can never stiffen and his
marrow defies rheumatism. Even the cold that
he catches in a draughty doorway must leave him
in despair because he is too indolent to cough.
The only thing that might wreck him would be
the total stoppage of food and drink. But that is
scarcely likely to happen, since his huge bulk puts
him to so great advantage in the scrimmage for
sustenance which other people unconsciously
carry on for his benefit.
“Just contrast him with the ordinary work-a-day
sort of man, under what may be regarded as
pretty fair conditions of these times. After
45 years of family raising and hard graft he
has- nothing but rheumatism —several sizes too
large for the shrivelled husk it wracks. In
the asylum he is a felon, or rather a pawn
in the game disciplinarians are playing for
promotion. So the old-age pension of 10s.
a-week and freedom are a foretaste of
Elysium. In the full tide of opulence he resolves
himself into a committee of ways and means.
How will he spend it all ? And this is
the proposal of one battered veteran after
goodness only knows how many delicious
hours’ computation: A room, 3s. a week ; one
square meal a day, fid. —3s. 6d.; leaving 3s.
fid. for 14 meals, tobacco, beer and sundries.
To any ordinary person acquainted with the
generous proportion of the average 6d. meal,
the idea of that one square feed a day in the
case of a system that actually does not require
a great amount of aliment seems all right. But the
person acquainted with the sixpenny meal is also,
doubtless, aware of the fact that in nine cases out
of ten it is the spoils for the victors. When the
burly navvy or wharf-labourer comes in and
energetically fills the intervals of waiting by
drinking all the sauce, scoffing all the greenstuff,
butter, pepper, and sundries, the proprietor grins.
Certainly, his smile is sickly, but that is the end
of it. He must live, however; and since his
profits are usually in direct ratio to the leavings,
the chances are that when once he becomes aware
of the identity of a pensioner, upon that feeble
old man he will seize as legitimate prey for ex-
perimentation in culinary economics. In the
contest the elderly person of no assertion and less
digestion will, of course, go to the wall.
“ Ruin this time, if you don’t mind. It’s what
my father drank out on the Oxboro’, and he was a
' snug old party. Never less than a pint a day,
and a quart when he could get it. You can see
his tombstone in old Windsor cemetery —aged 93.
Well, I believe a man inherits his father’s drink,
same as his constitution, and his diseases, and his
little tricks of holding his hands, and so on.—
Not bad ! —Must you be going—sorry—you’re
splendid company, young chap, and not too d d
talkative. So long ! You’ll see me round again
about Christmas.”
BILL SPUDS.
Singularly enough, it turns out that almost at
the Very time when the Rev. Dill Macky was
engaged in controversy with Father Masterson,
S.J., over the body of the naughty Anna, a
decision which precisely settled for loyal Catholics
the guilt of Anna was given by the Sacred College
at Rome. To this venerable body was submitted
the case, -not of Anna, but of Caia, also a naughty
lady. Caia was unchaste before her marriage :
she repented and was absolved at confession ; and
she subsequently declared upon oath to her bride-
groom that she had never been unchaste. De-
manded of the Collegium, sitting under the
presidency of a Consultor of the Index-Congrega-
tion, “ whether Caia had acted rightly. ” Decided:
“ She acted rightly at the first. For it is not
certain whether she then knew that Titus
(the husband) would require bodily chastity as
a condition of marriage. And, as she had received
from another source the forgiveness of her sin, she
was justified in availing herself of a reservatio
mentalis in her reply to his enquiry.” This
decision was given on March 11, 1901; was pub-
lished in Analecta Ecclesiastica, a theological-poli-
tical monthly, published at Rome, and edited by
Felix Cadene, a domestic prelate of Leo XIII;
and is quoted by Eng. Church Times, which
declares it is based '“upon the authority which a
loyal Yaticanist Catholic is bound to accept,” and
is “ presented to the world stamped as living and
modem, and as the utterance of the Ecclesia.
docens.”

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Three-Bag Crop.
We’re cleaning up a three-bag crop,
And “ Good enough ! ” says we ;
Just fork the stack and watch it drop,
The grain’s a sight to see—
A sight to cure the fly-bung eye
I’ve had this week or two,
Just to stand and watch the cups go by,
And see the wheat slide through.
For it’s togs for me and the boys and the missus—
It’s white-man’s food to eat;
And the graft comes willing
When the bags are filling—
Five bushels to the bag of wheat! wheat! wheat!
Thirteen shillings to the bag of wheat!
You pick your season—wet or dry—
Take “early” against “late,”
Then plough and sow accordingly—
It’s just a gamble straight!
Of course, a bit of graft and pluck
Won’t do you any harm ;
But, after all, it’s mostly luck
That runs a mallee farm.
Ho ! see the mortgage all a-row—
A hundred bags of best!
And, Lord ! it gives a man a glow
To get that off his chest.
So shake her up, you handleman !
Hi ! chaffy, clear away,
And dish the tally, if you can,
You put up yesterday !
For it’s togs for me and the boys and the missus—
It’s white man’s food to eat —
And the graft comes willing
When the bags are filling—
Five bushels to the bag of wheat! wheat! wheat!
Thirteen shillings to the bag of wheat.
VIC.
E.R.P.
The Old Reaping’ Band.
There was a good graft, and we followed it, too,
So confident, careless and free,
Up country and down where the waving grain
grew,
And sure of a welcome were we !
But good-bye to the rioketty reapers of yore,
And the sheaves that were bound by the hand ;
Good-bye to the rattle we’ll follow no more,
So-long to the double lock-band !
It was tie the stuff well, and ’twas gather it clean,
And sling the sheaves far enough back ;
And ’twas “ Nobody stick up the bloomin’
machine
If he isn’t in want of the sack ! ”
So farewell to the lunches and soda-drawn tea,
And the sugar that mostly was sand ;
Good-bye to the junk that was salt as the sea,
So-long to the old single-band !
We sweated and sweltered, and cursed at the graft,
For harvesting wasn’t a joke ;
But we stood the sheaves up for a sun-shade and
laughed
When a cog or a driving-bar broke.
But farewell to the clatter of stones on the roof,
And the dog-curse maliciously planned ;
And the sigh for the maids that were never aloof:
So-long to the old “ lightning” band !
Scarce is the graft that is worth a regret,
No matter how early it dies,
If soienoe but kill it; for vigorous yet
From its ashes shall others arise.
So good-bye to the freedom we revelled in then,
And the wages that we could demand ;
Good-bye to the bosses that welcomed the men,
So-long to the double lock-band !
V.
BEN SUN.
The Irony of Fate.
Paddy Rooke was the boss o’ the shearin’ shed ;
’E was sinewy, straight and tall,
While I was employed as the shearers’ cook,
And was skinny and plain and small.
It wasn’t my fault that the meat was tough,
An’ the tar got inter the stoo ;
Yet ’e kicked me the length o’ the shearin’ shed,
An’ ’e walloped me black and blue.
An’ I brooded on my wrong,
An’ I oursed both load an’ strong,
An’ I felt that Life was nothing but a sham,
An’ I wished that I was ’e,
Or that Paddy Rooke was me.
An’ I railed at Fate, wot made me wot I am.
Tilly Brady, she lived at the ’Arf-way ’Ouse,
An’ a plum of a gal was she ;
An’ I seen ’er tip Paddy a hartful wink
As she sugared an’ milked ’is tea.
Then ’e jined the “ Horstralian Mounted ’Orse,”
With a uniform like a toff,
An’ e’ went to the wars to fight the Boers,
And she went to see ’im off.
An’ I watched ’er pipe ’er eye
As they kissed an’ said, “ Good-bye,”
An’ I felt that all the world was only sham.
An’ I wished that I was ’e,
Or that Paddy Rooke was me,
An’ I moaned the Fate wot made me wot I am.
But Paddy got ’it with a cannon-ball,
And it scrunched up ’is bloomin’ bones;
An’ they laid ’im out on the starry veldt,
An’ they covered ’im up with stones,
While I got spliced to the Brady gal;
But she turned out a blinded sell,
For she’s always a-wishin’ that I was ’e,
Till I wishes ’em both in—well —
In a place both ’ot an’ deep ;
For I sit an’ groan an’ weep,
And I still think all the world’s a rotten sham :
An’ I wishes I was ’e,
An’ that Paddy Rooke was me,
An’ I cuss ray Fate, wot made me wot I am.
N.S.W.
THOMAS E. SPENCER.

The wail of a neglected wife at Christ-
church (M.L.) was that her husband didn’t own a
humpy of his own, so lie took her to his mother s,
where they gave her only bread and bad butter
for breakfast because she wouldn’t assimilate the
maternal parritch. Abuse was always on tap,
and when harangued for wasting the washing-
blue, and for not being able to account for a little
bit of soap that the dog bad chewed, the tan-
bride grew tired of wedded bliss. On baturdays
hubby used to give her a whole saxpence to spend,
and then went to the theatre with his sister.
Marriage on these hilarious terms was voted a
failure by the court, and the wife got a separation
order.

MR. REID’S ANXIETY ABORT THE POOR MAN
"If you come down on the great struggling masses in this or any other country, the poorest country as country as well as the richest; if you down come down on the great breathing workingman.
whose only espital is his flooting strength that is renewed by sleep; if you come down on the poor to bear the burden of the taken of the State, the poor, who make up the wealth and
strength of grery nation-(loud and prodonged cheers)-I say, ladies and gentlemen, that if you come down," etc.
G.H.R. (to the "Poor Man"): "They've shut me out, but if you will make a back for me Im think I could get through the window or over the fanlight somehow."
POOR MAN: "But you remember I gave you a legup six or seven years ago when you promisel to make me Jolly and Fat; and look at me now!"

Hotice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia.,
Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
€l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of\\d. postage, the subscription
to Tiie Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage—lid. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition of The Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to Tub Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal l\ote,
One Pound Note, of stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The. whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
"Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amateurs in Made anti White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions : under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 2, 1901.
The New Boom.
That the only possible result of a con-
tinuance of the present financial career of
Australia is national insolvency, and a stop-
page of payment of the interest on the public
liabilities, and a great commercial cataclysm,
is almost too obvious to be worth arguing
about. The questions of real interest are :
(1) What chance is there that the present
financial drunk will be checked in time 1 and
(2) if it isn’t checked, how long, in the ordi-
nary course of things, can the disaster be
postponed ? The six Commonwealth States
have added about £44,000,000 to their
liabilities since the year of the great collapse
—-the year when Australia decided that it
had received a lesson which it would never,
never, by any chance forget. Despite the
great increase in population, it owes a good
deal more per inhabitant than it did then. It
had a year or two of comparative sobriety cer-
tainly, but it was only a year or two. It is
now .spending money that isn’t its.own faster
that in 1892, before it received its solemn
warning, and far more than twice as fast as
in 1896. Not that Australia was more than
partially sober in 1896, but all the same the
increase is monumental. This is how the
two years compare :

This huge increase, too, had nothing what-
ever to do with the cost of the Sydney land
resumptions. They only came into the
account at a later date, and it is partly—though
only in part —owing to them that the
Commonwealth States have borrowed
£7,500,000 in the past four months, and are
out on a wilder orgy than ever.
* * * *
But not only has borrowing increased ;
the whole system has deteriorated. At one
time loans were issued because the country
required money to pay for reproductive
works. Now this country has so many
works in proportion to population that it is
hard to find anything reproductive to build.
But it owes so much publicly and privately
that unless it continues borrowing it must
send away goods to the value of about
£16,000,000 a year more than it imports,
to pay interest. This is an awful responsi-
bility for a small community to face— most of
all a community which believes in high
wages and short hours, and which has little
to export except the produce of the worst-
paid Gibeonite on earth—-the person who
goes on the land. Britain is our chief
creditor. To pay in goods means that Aus-
tralia, with its barren soil and its long sea-
distances and costly freights, must put its
goods on that distant market so cheaply that
they can sell in competition with those of
more favored and more adjacent countries,
where there is no short hour or minimum
wage movement. Australia can’t pay in
raw material unless its people are willing to
work 10 or 12 hours a day for 20s. a
week, or somewhere thereabouts—and as a
matter of fact the man on the land, who
supplies the bulk of this country’s exports,
generally does that. But if it borrowed no
more, and paid all its interest-bill in produce,
a far larger proportion of its population
would have to work for 12 hours a day, and
20s. or so per week. The country floats
loans nowadays largely to postpone this
problem, and ingenious politicians invent
public works to set against these loans.
And this is the result:—

Up till 1892, only about one-sixih of the
loan-money was applied to works which
yielded no revenue. In the period between
1892 and 1901 more than one-third was
thus applied. And as these are loans the
interest on which has to be .paid wholly out
of taxation, the tax burden is steadily in-
creasing. And as more of the revenue goes
in interest less of it goes in public works,
and to counterbalance this more of the
public works expenditure is charged to
ioans.
* • * •
Going back only six years it is remarkable
how the craze has grown for propping up
the battered credit of Australia by virtually
putting loan moneys into revenue. That is
really what it amounts to, though the object
is threefold : the revenue, burdened by the
increasing weight of interest, is relieved by
the transfer to loan expenditure of under-
takings which used to be paid out of taxa-
tion. Work is found for the unemployed.
And the borrowed cash helps to solve the
ever-aching problem—how to send a surplus
of about £16,000,000 worth of produce to
England every year, and sell it in that over-
burdened market against our cheap com-
petitors, so as to pay the annual interest
bill, and yet keep up the fiction that Aus-
tralia, though bound by its debt obligations
to be a huge supplier of cheap produce, can
still pay the highest wages in the world's
market. This is how the great swindle has
grown and expanded since 1895 :

It is the fashion to meet all warnings of
possible danger with the assurance that
Australia, so tar, has met all its obligations,
and that the past is a guarantee for the
future. But, so far as concerns meeting the
drain of interest out of our own resources,
Australia has hardly even begun to encoun-
ter its obligations yet. Some of the States
haven’t begun at all. Taking the last ten
years, this is the extent to which the States
have liquidated the liability for interest on
their public debts out of their own re-
sources, so far as the figures are supplied by
Coghlan’s “ Seven Colonies,” his “Statistics
of the Seven Colonies,” and his “Statistics
of the Six States of the Commonwealth :

Taking all things into account, the result is
that the six Governments of the Com-
mouwealth had to provide, in 10 years,
£69,619,449 as interest on their public
liabilities. Almost all of this had to be
remitted to England. The only way most
of it could be remitted was in the shape of
produce—largely produce in the sale of which
Australia has to compete with nations that
have better soil and lower freights. There-
fore, in order that the produce might sell in
the British market under these drawbacks,
it was necessary for Australia, if it paid this
bill in its own goods, to become a cheap-
labor country in an aggravated form. Aus-
tralia’s legislators shelved four-fifths of the
difficulty by borrowing £57,914,059 of the
money, and only faced the other fifth of the
problem. Wherefore it will be a much larger
problem a little later on.
* * * *
Australia has had two booms within a very
few years—the one which broke in 1893 and
the one which will break presently. Until the
country began to be struck, in the premoni-
tory days of 1891, with a difficulty in the
British loan market, it carried its debt-
burden easily for the sufficient reason
that it borrowed every year enough
to pay the interest, and something more
to go on booming with. Then came a
series of lean years—years of sackcloth and
repentance—when the country borrowed
only enough to pay the largest part of its
interest bill, and liquidated the rest out of
its own resources. They were cold and
bitter years, but if Australia had paid the
whole of its interest bill out of its own re-
sources—as it will have to do some day—
they would have been a vast amount worse,
and very few shillings a week would prob-
ably have been regarded as a liberal wage for
a week of 64 hours, with other things in
proportion. And then there came the
period of revival when, in the words of the
blatant politician, “the soundness of the
country asserted itself,” and “ its solvency
was triumphantly vindicated,” and other
things of the kind happened. That was the
period when Australia began to borrow
more vigorously again, and gave up the
attempt to pay even a reasonable section of
its interest bill out of its own means. Here
are some extracts from Coghlan’s statistics,
which illustrate the position :

* * * •
So the dog has gone back in a general way
to the unpleasant substance mentioned in
the Scriptures, and the sow that was not
washed, but only sponged over, has resumed
business in the old mudhole. Last year
Victoria and South Australia reduced their
loan expenditure a little—only a little—but
the ‘other four States made increases that
more than compensated for this. The usual
signs and ear-marks of the good old boom
again begin to be apparent. Comparing the
year just ended with the one immediately
preceding, the exports of the Commonwealth
decreased about £4,000,000 and imports in-
creased nearly £6,000,000. In 1899 the six
States paid £13,500,000 of their foreign
interest liabilities by excess of exports ; in
1900 only £3,500,000. In two, if not three
States, the area under crop has begun to
diminish slightly, and the old tendency to
throw up the heavy and comparatively
profitless job of agriculture, and take to
Government works, again manifests itself.
And, as usual, the new boom is described as
the emergence of the country, by its own
efforts, from a period of gloom and de-
pression. In which respect it is so like the
old boom that the difference between the two
isn’t worth mentioning.
An Agricultural Minimum Wage.
Under the heading of “ Labor Legislation,” this
item was wired over from Maoriland last week
Labor legislation has had considerable attention during
the past week. On the one hand, the Industrial Concilia-
tion and Arbitration Act has had a set-back ; on the
other, the Factories Act has been pushed forward. The
latter measure was the subject of an all-night sitting on
Thursday, on which occasion the House worked with
exhaustive diligence, and was led by the Minister for
Labor (the Premier) with tactful generalship. The result
is the first appearance on any statute-book of a legal
eight hours’ day. That is a great triumph for organised
labor. At the same time, by exempting all country avo-
cations from the operation of the law, all interests are
consulted. This single piece of legislation, which has
drawn the legislative line hitherto regarded as beyond
the wit of man, is sufficient to make the session of 1901
memorable.
Australasia now possesses one eight-hour law from
which all purely country industries (agriculture,
dairying &c., most especially) are omitted ; one
minimum wage law from which country industries
are omitted ; and a whole multitude of laws for the
benefit of the worker, from almost all of which
country industries are omitted with a persistence
Worthy of a better cause. And nobody, or hardly
anybody, dreams of not omitting them. They are
left out instinctively and as a matter of course.
Yet the country worker, whose hours are long in
inverse ratio to his wages, which are short, calls
for legislative assistance quite as much as the town
worker. Why doesn’t he get it?
* * • *
Leaving Maoriland out of the reckoning, Aus-
tralia has to export, chiefly to England, not only
enough to pay for its imports (which are absurdly
large because it hasn’t yet learned how to manu-
facture for itself hundreds of things which it
should manufacture), but £8,000,000 worth per
annum to pay interest on its private, and almost
as much more on its public, borrowings. Even
now it has to be, in proportion to population,
almost the largest exporter in the world, and if it
ceased foreign borrowing it would have to further
increase its exports by several millions a year
—or else correspondingly diminish its imports, it
doesn’t export largely of manufactured goods like
the United States, but almost wholly raw pro-
duce, and as the most ignorant of races can tear
raw produce out of the ground in some sort
of fashion, there is plenty of competition in
that line. The Australian has to plough and
sow and harrow nearly four of his barren acres to
get the same amount of produce that the American
gets off two, and the German off little more than
one, therefore he needs to be a remarkably cheap
laborer if he is to hold his own. He has also to
pay about twice the sea-freight that they do before
he gets his goods on the British market, so again
he needs to be an extra-cheap laborer. He needs,
or will need before long, to be about the cheapest
toiler on the land among the white races of this
earth. Practically the whole recent history of this
country is the history of a continuous struggle to
get lots of goods on the English market at such a
low price that they will sell in competition with
all comers, so as to raise the money to pay the
interest with and to pay for imports.
• * * *
If Australia, under present conditions, gave its
country workers a decent minimum wage and
decent working hours it would doubtless involve
an increase in local prices, but that would
only be a small item in the consequences. The
country would also cease to produce cheaply
enough to hold its own in the British market
against long-hour and low-wage competitors, and
the sudden cutting off of the resource that pays the
interest bill would bring about the promptest kind
of national bankruptcy. A little might be done by
cutting down rent, but a very large proportion of
the agricultural community has bought its land
cheaply off the State, and pays no rent. Some-
thing might be done by cutting down railway
freights, but a great deal of what was taken off in
freights would have to be put on again in taxes,
and they couldn’t nearly all belaid on the wealthy
classes, for in these times the wealthy classes of
Australia have no very large amount of money—
their wealth consists more in schemes for getting
money. The Australian can’t hope to start
paying his interest bill in manufactured goods
for many years to come; America is nearer
to the English market, and the American has
a vigor and ingenuity in this kind of business
which the Australian shows no present sign of
emulating. And, under these circumstances,
what are the prospects ?
• • * *
Apparently, the first thing is to learn to manu-
facture for ourselves—by “artificially coddling
exotic industries ” with a Protective tariff or by
any possible means—so that there will be less im-
ported manufactured goods to pay for witli the
products of the ill-paid country worker. The
second thing is to discourage the absentee of
every kind by every possible means—the absentee
landlord, money-lender, squatter, bank, insurance
co., and all other varieties —so that there will be
less interest on private loans to pay for out of the
products of the same sweated producer. Thirdly,
it is essential to put an end to all foreign borrovv-
ing, so that there will be no increase in the public
interest-bill, that has to be liquidated in
the products of the worst - paid class of in-
dustry on earth. Otherwise, there is nothing in
view to prevent Australia becoming almost the
lowest-wage white man’s country on this globe.
It is at present in the amazing position of having
to send abroad about tivo-thirds of its entire pro-
duce to pay interest and to pay' for imports ! It
lias to send away about one-seventh to pay
interest alone. It has to sell most of this
in a market which is open to all the cheap-
labor countries on earth, and most of its
competitors are nearer to their market, and
are blessed with a more productive soil. A coun-
try like that can’t pay decent wages to its primary
producers, and it doesn’t. The energy of its re-
formers is directed almost wholly to providing
decent wages and decent conditions for the com-
parative handful of industrial workers in its city
factories, and the much larger population which is
“on the land” gets what wages and hours and
accommodation Providence sends it. The joke in
The Bulletin’s “ Aboriginalities ” column about
the Australian farmer who pays 10s. a week for
15 hours’ labor per diem is partly joke and
a good deal tragic earnest. A debtor country,
which has to pay its way by supplying Britain
with produce dragged out of a soil which is
inflated with pride when it averages 8 bushels to
the acre, and with the cost of 16,000 miles of
freight deducted from the proceeds, can’t be a
land of high wages. And a debtor country which
has to grow two-thirds of its produce under these
conditions is likely to be a country of miserably
low wages, so far as the majority of its
population is concerned. Will some reckless
reformer propose an eight-hours’ day and a mini-
mum wage of even 30s. a week for the agricultural
employee, and listen to hear something drop ?
The Interesting Consumptive.
Until a few years ago the sufferer from phthisis
was merely an “ interesting” invalid. For many
persons he is an attractive invalid still. His
disease does not usually confine him to the house.
He can walk about and chat; he can teacli in
Sunday-school; he can play ping pong; and all
the time look pale and interesting. His hectic
cheek-spots are not usually regarded as danger-
signals. His disease is a Respectable d isease, located
inaplacewhich can be mentioned without blushing,
and his hacking cough is a melancholy appeal to
public sympathy. When at the last he comes to
spit up half-a-pound of blood and lung in a single
paroxysm, he does it in comparative privacy ; and
his funeral is numerously attended by those who
have seen in him only an unfortunate victim of
malignant Fate.
• • • *
The notion that consumption is “catching”
begins to take root, however; and when the
public really wakes up to the fact that, in most
communities, phthisis is far more to be dreaded
than leprosy, “the poor consumptive” will probably
have a much less pleasant time during his lingering
journey to the grave. The residents of Roma, in
Queensland, for example, commence to realise that
there are some risks attached to living in a town-
ship renowned as a consumptives’ sanatorium.
Roma, where Petek Jackson, the pugilist,
lately died of phthisis, is reputed to have
a climate eminently suited for the treatment
of the disease. The consumptive sojourners
in Roma at present number over 50, and, as
far as is known, none of the local residents
are any the worse. The Govt, is supporting as
many as 20 patients on farms, and the farm people
have apparently not suffered, though they breathe
the same air, use the same eating and drinking
utensils, and, generally speaking, take no precau-
tions whatever. The patients walk about the town,
and expectorate in the streets, hotel-bars—any-
where. There is not one hotel in which some con-
sumptive person has not slept. Indeed, it appears
that the hotel proprietors at present cannot refuse
to accept consumptives as boarders. But the ap-
proach of phthisis is so insidious that one never
knows when the germ has taken root; and even.


Roma grows uneasy. A building which had been
used as a sanatorium was sold some time ago tor
50 per cent, less than its original value.
Jackson’s fate, by-the-way, was only that of nearly
all the consumptives who go to Roma. I hey either
die there, orreturntotheir homes just in time to cue.
In New South Wales the suburbs oil the Muson s
Point line are favored as residences by consump-
tives ; and at Hornsby, the terminus, so many
phthisical persons reside that the suburb is
shunned by others on that account. In the trains,
morning and evening, you may see numerous con-
sumptive people more or less obviously ailing ;
and some of them are not particular where they
expectorate. When the carriage-windows are
kept closed, as in winter, the risk to the healthy
is certainly grave. * *
It is time that people generally recognised that
the consumptive, as a source of disease infection, is
practically more dangerous in Australia than
either the leper or the smallpox-patient; for the
simple reason that smallpox and leper patients
are quarantined, while phthisis patients go
everywhere unfettered ; and phthisis is still
the most fatal disease in this country. Con-
sumptives should be put under surveillance
for the good of the community, however we
may pity them. Distinct cases of phthisis should
be reported to the Board of Health ; the patients
should be warned against transmitting infection
per sputum-, and hotels and public conveyances
should only be open to them under definite restric-
tions designed to safeguard healthy travellers. It
will be rough on the consumptive; but the public
health comes first. Which fact, to do many
intelligent phthisical patients justice, is already
quite recognised by themselves.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Man Who Writes to the Papers.
Nowadays the Australian papers are inundated
with letters like the following (which appeared in
a Sydney daily) from people who evidently fancy
that the Federal tariff, with its great mass of
revenue duties in addition to its Protective im-
posts, was got up for Kingston’s amusement: —
Sir, — The Federal Government require two millions of
money, and consequently have to raise eight millions,
thanks to the “ Braddon Blot.” Would it not be possible
to raise one million by a tax of 10s. per bale on wool
exported (a trifle over a farthing per pound), and another
million by raising four millions through excise and
Customs duties ? If above could be carried out, it would
mean the reduction of the proposed duties by one-half. —
Yours, etc., G.A.F.
It is a solemn fact that nine Australians out of
ten appear to believe, like the writer of
the above, that the tariff was devised be-
cause the Federal Government wanted money
for its own expenses, and even the tenth one is
only vaguely aware that it was the great
necessities of the States, and of New South Wales
as much as any other, that made the Kingston
tariff necessary. A customs tariff that yielded
£5,000,000 would give "the Federal Government,
under the Braddon “Blot” proviso, £1,250,000
for its own expenses, and that, with its share of the
excise, would be all it wants. It could almost pull
through if it applied the old Reid tariff of N.S*. W.
to the whole of the Commonwealth. But it has to
raise heaps of money to hand back to the terribly
embarrassed State Governments—and N.S.W.,
which makes the most row, is not the least em-
barrassed of the lot. N.S.W. cheerfully granted
old age pensions (to start at the beginning
of the financial year during which the Federal
tariff came in force), and trusted to that tariff
to supply the money. Certainly the State
legislators didn’t provide a penny of it. It
raised the wages of many State employees,
and O’Sullivan’s minimum wage on State
contracts is regarded with justifiable pride.
It borrowed so liberally that this year’s
interest bill is fully £lOO,OOO more than last
year’s. It had the second largest deficit in Aus-
tralasia, and it resolved that it wasn’t worth while
providing for it, or the extra interest, or the extra
wages, for the Federal Parliament would find the
cash. It struck noble attitudes over its Old Age
Pensions and increased wages, and said (while
leaving the Federal Parliament to find the money)
that these things showed how strong, solvent, and
progressive the State had become under an en-
lightened policy, &c., &c. And Kingston and
Turner are doing their best, amid great obloquy,
to find the money—yet N.S.W. has heaped up
obligations at such a rate, without providing for
them, that N.S.W.’s share of the tariff-revenue
promises even now to be £300,000 less than the
State Treasurer reckons that he requires, and
both Kingston and Turner have despairingly
given up the attempt to wholly fill the gap.
So the State Government gets the credit
and glory of granting Old Age Pensions and
other concessions, and the Federal Government
has mud thrown at it liberally as a brutal extor-
tioner for trying to find the means to pay them
with, and the people who voted the Old Age Pen-
sions, and left the Federal Government to find the
money, write to the papers about it.
* .
A Proposed Pise 1 Referendum.
W. M. Hughes, who represents some of the
Sydney suburbs in the Federal House of Repre-
sentatives, suggests that it would be well to submit
the Barton Government’s tariff to a popular vote.
The idea certairdy has much to recommend it,
though a good deal depends on how it is proposed
to lay the mattter before the people. The
Referendum differs materially from an ordinary
election. The latter is a function where the
people choose between (say) Smith and Brown,
and the fact that the alternative of Smith is
Brown, and the alternative of Brown is Smith,
is obvious to any ordinary voter. The Referen-
dum, on the other hand, may be a proposition
without any alternative. So far, the Foreign-trade
party has only proposed that the people of Aus-
tralia should be called on to declare by a national
vote whether they will have Barton’s tariff
or not. Apparently, if Barton’s tariff
is rejected the Calico Jimmy party will
profess that that means that the Reid tariff,
which wasn’t placed before the people at all, is
accepted. If the question was put in one line:
“Will you have Barton’s heavy tariff or
not ? ” a very large number of people would vote
“ No” with enthusiasm. It would read like the
question : “Do you prefer to be heavily taxed, or
not to be taxed ? ” and on that subject the great
multitude has -no doubts whatsoever. But it is
admitted, even by George Reid and M'Millan,
and all the Foreign-trade leaders, that a tariff
which will yield between £8,000,000 and £9,000,000
is absolutely necessary, unless this country is
going to default in its interest-payments. There-
fore, it is necessary for the Reid party to prepare
its tariff also, and then it would be a fair thing to
hold a Referendum on these four questions—the
matter can’t be put into less :
(1) Are you in favor of the Barton Government’s tariff?
It is framed to yield about £8,500,000 the State*
can’t get through with less. Its framers allege that it
will create new industries and supply employment and
wages ; and its enemies, without wholly denying this,
declare that it will raise prices so greatly as to more than
counterbalance any advantage thus gained. You can
form your own opinion on these points.
(2) Or are you in favor of the Reid tariff, also calcu-
lated to raise about £8,500,000 ? Its framers guarantee
that it won’t give assistance in the way of creating any
industry or finding anybody employment and wages ;
and its enemies cordiallv agree with them. Its enemies
say that it will'also raise prices. You can form your
own opinion on these points. . ...
(3) Or do you reject both proposals, with a view to the
drafting of some new scheme quite different from either,
but also calculated to raise £8,500,000 ?
(4) Or do you simply propose to reject them both and
have no alternative scheme at all ? Australia, if it re-
pudiates its public debt, lock, stock and barrel, can do
without any tariff. Do you vote for Repudiation ?
That is the case to be put before the public by
Referendum, if there is any real demand for a
Referendum. At the same time it is highly im-
probable that it is the case which the Calico
Jimmy party wants to put before the public. The
community would reject almost any taxation pro-
posal if it was put before it in the form of the
question: “Do you prefer to be taxed or not?”
and if the Barton tariff was sent to the people by
itself that would be the question. “Do you pre-
fer to be taxed this way, or to raise an equal
amount the other way ?” is a different question.
The average prisoner, when requested to say
whether he prefers to be fined £5 or not, would
answer enthusiastically in the negative. If asked
whether he would prefer to be fined £5 rather
than do 14 days, he might answer quite as cor-
dially in the affirmative. So far, the importers’
proposals for a Referendum include no mention of
the 14 days.
The Voice of the Freacher.
An English clergyman in S’Africa writes a long
letter to Manchester Guardian on the subject of
the war. He fully believes in the justice of it
from the British standpoint, but makes scathing
remarks after this fashion on the muddlement of
Salisbury, Chamberlain and Co., and the brutal
and incompetent Ketchener, whose reputation,
built up against ill-armed savages, seems likely to
be buried with many more in an African grave :
There are just two points on which I feel impelled to
write to you, and they both relate to an essential mistake
which we are making about the Boer people. They are
not a people to be cowed, nor are they a people to forget.
This bears, for one thing, very relevantly on our conduct
of the war. Every piece of terrorism, every “ severe
measure,” has so far recoiled on ourselves.' The burning
and pillaging committed by our troops, and to which I
can testify (having ridden hundreds of miles in their
track), has merely put the very devil into the Boers. . . .
From what I have personally seen of the ravages inflicted
by our troops on the Free State by tire and pilfering,
there are revelations yet to be made to the British public
which will fulfil Mr. Kruger’s forecast and indeed
“ stagger human itj'.”
I know nothing personally about “ concentration ”-
camps, having never visited one. However, I have, when
riding on the veldt, met convoys of women and children,
and have also seen them huddled by scores in open
trucks which stood by the hour at Springfontein railway-
station until the line could be cleared for forwarding
them to the Bethulie Camp. The last-mentioned con-
tingent had come in the same day from Fauresmith owing
to “ clearing ” operations in that district. . . . What will
never die from the memory of survivors is the horrors
which we have allowed to fall upon them, not, of course,
intentionally, but through sheer muddlement. The
Government returns as to the mortality in these
camps furnish only too convincing proof, and I
venture to predict that Miss Hobjiouse’s findings
will be virtually endorsed by the Ladies’ Commis-
sion,' which has now sailed. It is the same kind
of neglect which caused the hospital scandals, and which
I have with my own eyes witnessed going on in the fight-
ing districts and in the near presence of the enemy. A
General sets out to “clear” a given stretch of country 7.
He is going to scoop human lives wholesale into his net,
but has no notion of what he will do with them when
caught. The capture effected, he dumps the unfortunates
down on the nearest camp, whose officer is at his wit’s
end to provide for them. Then the troubles begin ;
children die for lack of milk, women are untended in
child-birth, and the Government have to compile a set of
“ returns ” which make an Englishman’s ears tingle as he
reads.
We have now the blended stupidity and cruelty of
these “rebel” executions. I am not surprised to see
that some dozen leading farmers were afterwards'
banished from the Cradock district. Probably, as the
South African News suggests, they refused to attend
when ordered. I hear that the Murraysburg district is
seething with fury. Yesterday’s papers report a specially
fierce and successful attack on a military train in that
neighborhood. It will yet be found that the putting up
of the scaffolds for Coetzee and Marais was equivalent to
building recruiting offices for the Boer commandants.
May God inspire our rulers with humaner and wiser
counsels, but the average British officer exercising mili-
tary law appears, from what I have seen of him, to regard
himself as a viceregent of the Almighty without His
responsibility. . . .
It is the demand for “ unconditional surrender,” the
brutal announcement that we mean to be “ top dog,” to
“ fight to a finish,” and not leave them a “ shred of inde-
pendence” which is nerving the Boers with the courage of
despair. We shall find them on these lines as intractable as
Philip and Alva found their forefathers. If we were not
wholly in the right when the war began—-I speak as an
Outlander— we had, at least, a great deal of it on our
side—l think we had the bulk of it. We are now trans-
ferring that quantity to the side of the Boers, and history
may come to curse us as it has long cursed Spain.
The “ Tied” Hotel Question Again.
N. S. Wales Upper House threw out the Bill for
the abolition of “tied” hotels by a majority of
two. The majority also made the mistake of
giving reasons for its conduct, and denounced the
measure as a scheme for the repudiation of agree-
ments and covenants, for the confiscation of
securities, and for the standing of vested interests
wrong end up. Of course, all this was the merest
imbecility, but the Upper House says things like
that in its sleep. It is an automatic drivelling-
machine, and it is only necessary to put a liberal
idea in the slot and the machine throws out a
foolish remark on the instant. There is no
question of depriving the brewer of his
security; the trouble is that the brewer
won’t give back the security, even when
the loan is fully cleared off. He has so fixed
things that when he gets his money back he still
keeps his publican enslaved, and the object of the
Bill was to make him surrender the security like
any honest money-lender when the loan is paid.
New 7 South Wales has now to consider, for the
hundredth time, what it will do with its
Upper House. That body isn’t entrenched behind
a property franchise, like the Upper Houses
of Victoria, Tasmania and S. A. It can be
abolished any day ; when it rejects the Bill for its
own abolition it is only necessary to have a
Parliamentary recess of a few weeks, appoint say
80 new members pledged to vote for abolition, and
then let a great Upper House of about 150
M’s.L.G. hold its first and last session, and com-
mit an ungraceful suicide. It can’t be too often
reiterated that the existence of an Upper House
in the Parliament of a federated State is contrary
to the weight of imperial precedent, and a gross
outrage on British Imperial traditions. The only
other Federation in the British empire is Canada,
and six out of the eight divisions of Canada get
along with one House of Legislature apiece. The
flat refusal of the Australian State Upper Houses
to fall in line with the precedents of the British
empire in this matter is a lamentable and dis-
loyal and revolutionary circumstance. Nobody
professes that the Council originates any
valuable work, and when it amends or re-
jects the Assembly’s measures the country always
demands that it should be coerced into agree-
ment. A House which excites public contempt
when it does nothing and public indignation
when it does anything, is a ridiculous anomaly.
Yet, if the Council isn’t abolished, it must either
be allowed to block legislation urgently de-
manded by the people, or else it must be enlarged
to about 1000 members with a prospect of
further indefinite increase. Democrats petrify
so rapidly in its motionless atmosphere that
it requires to be swamped afresh after every
election to keep it in touch with the popular sen-
timent. Washington, or some other ancient
personage, is credited with having said that an
Upper House is a necessary complement to a
Lower one just as a saucer is of a cup—“it is the
place into which we pour our legislation to cool.”
Nowadays, however, the public drinks its tea out
of the cup, which makes all the difference.

The Blight of Phthisis.
That Premier See recognises to some extent the
horror of the facts set forth in The Bulletin last
week with regard to the Liverpool concentration
camp for the consumptive poor was shown by the
reply which he gave to a medical deputation on
the subject the day following. Facts in regard to
the ravages of phthisis, and the cruel treatment of
poor phthisical patients, were put before the
N.S.W. Premier by several medical men, and
Mr. See promptly promised to establish a
temporary hospital on the sea coast, and
to bring before the Public Works Committee
the question of setting up a permanent building.
Dr. Camac Wilkinson very clearly set out the
importance, from an economic point of view, of
the State doing something to save its consumptive
citizens ; and he had the satisfaction of finding
his medical colleagues agree with him as to the pro-
priety of a coast site for a hospital for phthisis.
When, some little while ago, Dr. Wilkinson con-
tended that proximity to the sea coast would not
necessarily unsuit a locality for the treatment of
consumptives, his view was opposed with some heat
by others, and something of a split in the medical
ranks was threatened. Fortunately that was
averted. In the light of most modern experience
Dr. Wilkinson appears to be right, as practical
experiments in England have shown that con-
sumption can be successfully coped with
under what used to be regarded as un-
favorable climatic conditions, if the food
and fresh air supplies are sufficiently generous.
Indeed, in cases of lung trouble, no arbitrary rule
a; to the effect of situation on the health can be
set down. Some patients will flourish in the
humid air of the sea coast; others pine except in a
dry, inland atmosphere. Writer can adduce from
personal observation the cases of two people who,
in the dry climate of Bathurst, suffered from
respiratory troubles, which a visit to the coast at
once alleviated; and (in the same town, at the
same time) of two others who had been obliged to
take refuge inland from chest troubles which
made life in Sydney, to them, intolerable.
The first State consumptive hospital of N.S.W., if
John See keeps his promise, will, at any rate, be
on the sea coast, and experience of its working
should give fresh valuable data as regards
the suitability of a moist atmosphere to phthisical
patients. The temporary hospital, or even the
permanent one, within the metropolitan area, will,
however, not by any means solve the problem
completely. That central station should be aided
by branch sanatoria, under as varied climatic
conditions as possible for sufferers ; these sanatoria
should embrace, to be properly effective, the range
of climate afforded by Sydney (sea level, moist,
equable weather); Pennant Hills (000 ft. altitude);
Katoomba (3300 ft.); Kiandra (5000 ft.); Bathurst
(2000 ft.); and Bourke (sea level, with very hot,
dry conditions). N.S.W. would thus lay a worthy
foundation for future Federal action to deal with
the consumptive as a national problem. Ulti-
mately, the Commonwealth, if true to itself, must
undertake the campaign against the most deadly
enemy of the Australian race, which yearly sends
5000 victims to the grave, yearly keeps at least
25,000 citizens as prisoners* of disease.

Wow! Yah! Yow!
Now is the time when the sojourner or peregrina-
tor in Queensland pauses nightly on his verandah,
or at the threshold of his tent, to listen to the dogs
of greed and monopoly baying the newly-risen
moon of White Australia. Listen to the black
dog howling ! That is the Q.N. Bank, up to its
ears in mortgages, and ready to send all Australia
and the Australian race to perdition if only its
shareholders may rake a few more shekels
out of its reconstructed ruin by the aid
of Kanaka semi-slavery. That spotted dog
is the Colonial Sugar Company, which advocates
white labor in N.S.W., supports black labor in
Queensland, and is supported by coolie labor in
Fiji—a leopard-dog which changes its spots to suit
the chance of dividends (averaging £160,000 to
£lBO,OOO per annum) Yonder striped dog is the
Philp Ministry, bound body and soul to
the banks and syndicates which smother Banana-
land, and reiterating “ White men cannot,
cannot, cannot cut cane ” —though white
men do cut it continually, and even lately-
deceased Minister Chataway, when associate
editor of Mackay Sugar Journal, admitted, in a
letter dated 27th December, 1893, and published
in Brisbane Courier :
If anyone made the statement that white men cannot
work in the fields anywhere south of Townsville it is
obviously untrue. White men can work in fair comfort,
except during three or four months of the year. In proof
of this, I may say that besides whites cutting several
thousand tons of cane this year, white farmers grew,
cut, and delivered at the mills at least 15,000 tons of cane
which were never touched by a colored laborer. The
trouble is that white men will not accept such wages as
an agricultural industry can afford to pay. Men who are
accustomed to eight hours a day and a shilling an hour
will not work 12 hours a day at cane-cutting and earn
only 30s. a week and their rations.
Out of their own mouths the White-Kanakas are
condemned. Yet all the moribund “ Chambers
of Commerce,” all the Govt.-fed “National
Associations” and “Progress Committees” and
other pretentiously-named organisations behind
which the Tooley-street Tailors lie wire-pulling
in ambush, lift up agonising voices in a prolonged
and melancholy chorus of “ Wow ! yah ! yow ! ”

The anti-tariff ravings of the Melbourne Argus
must put a strain on the credulity of its woolliest-
headed readers. Take some extracts from the
leading article of October 15 :
We repeat the assertion that the tariff will not raise the
price of any one article which our country workers pro-
duce. On the contrary, the price of hops, of eggs and
cheese and other articles is likely to come down ; there
will be a great struggle in the Melbourne fruit markets
with imports from Tasmania and South Australia. The
tariff does not help the mine-owner nor the miner in one
solitary particular, but it penalises the enterprise of the
investor and reduces the spending power of the worker’s
wages. It darkens every home by curtailing the pur-
chasing possibilities of every housewife’s shilling. Is this
the way to advance Australia?
To the Argus’s solemn inquiry The Bulletin
would reply that if the farmer is going to sell his
produce at lower rates than before, what time the
value of the housewife’s shilling suffers a ruinous
reduction through things selling at higher rates
than before, Australia is obviously going to the
devil. The Argus case can be crowded into these
three propositions :
The Commonwealth supplies itself sufficiently with
farm produce.
When an extra-heavy tariff is put on farm produce it
will become cheaper than it was without the tariff.
Therefore when, by the operation of prohibitive duties
on boots, etc., Australia learns to supply itself sufficiently
with them also, the inference is that they will also become
cheaper.
There is no extra charge for this inference. The
Bulletin is a generous-minded paper, and would
share its last inference any day with a mentally-
impoverished brother publication.

The third chapter of the Vic. fellmongers’ protest
against the minimum wage ended the other
day in Melbourne. The first act was the
closing up of three large fellmongeries, all
accompanied by many painful wails about their
inability to pay the minimum wage fixed by law ;
the second was when the law expressed its utter
lack of sympathy with the employers on strike ;
and last week the third stage was reached when
one of the protesting firms resumed operations,
paying the legal wage without any apparent
difficulty—and giving a Saturday half-holiday
into the bargain, which is more than the Act
stipulates for. The other two would also have
resumed if a shortage in the supply of skins didn’t
create a difficulty. It seems that during the
temporary hitch the butchers began to do their
own fellmongering, and found they could do it as
well as the professionals, and that they saved
Is. 6d. on each skin. So, though the same amount
of work is done, it has largely passed into new
hands. That protest against the minimum wage
is likely to prove a costly experiment for the
protesting parties. They probably never really
meant to close up for good, but they acted their
part so well, and the eternal farewell which they
uttered to the smellful sheepskin was so pathetic
and lifelike, that people believed it was all over,
and made new arrangements. And the people
that all this realism was intended to impress—the
Wages Board—weren’t impressed in the slightest.
