Since the appearance of the Federal Tariff Melb.
Argus has gone completely off its head. From
its issue of October 17 :
It is frequently said a high duty must be placed on
doors to prevent the importation of prison-made doors
from America. ... As a matter of fact, America’s cheap
dc.ors are not the result of prison labor but of cheap
material, first-class machinery, good tools, skilled work-
men and large' consumption. Australia may impose a
duty of 100 per cent-., and the American doors can under-
sell'those made locally. A protective tariff can only
increase the cost of doors and buildings and make rents
higher.
So it appears that* America, by reserving to itself
its home maiket under a prohibitive tariff, and
thus guaranteeing steady employment to skilled
mechanics, enabled it to turn out, while paying
high wages, about the cheapest door on earth :
and the logical Argus concludes therefrom that
“a protective tariff can only increase the cost of
doors.” Also the Fatman’s paper girds at Allan
M'Lean for saying that trade adds nothing to
the wealth of the world. “Does it not? Why,
the wool might as well be left to rot on the
sheep’s back.” The Argus evidently doesn’t
understand that wool is first shorn, then washed,
combed, woven, made up into flannels or cloth,
and that all these processes are manufactures, not
trade. They increase the value of the wool, while
the carting of that staple, or of the finished cloth,
to and frq, increases its value nothing, though it
raises the cost very considerably. Isn’t it about
time the Argus applied for an old-age pension ?
A humorist, signing himself “ George White,”
actually got a letter into Sydney Telegraph lately
denouncing Protection after this fashion :
At the present time we have an object-lesson as to the
result of highlv-protective tariffs in two countries which
are highly protected—the United States and France.
The U.S. are themselves producers of gold ; their output
is about £18,000,000 per annum, and yet, as a result of
their protective policy, they are driven to import gold,
which they do not want. We ourselves have sent them
from Sydney by the. last three successive steamers to San
Francisco £1,500,000 of gold. The result is that the
Treasury at Washington is overburdened with gold, of
which it has accumulated about £100,000,000 worth, and
the U.S. Secretary of the Treasury is anxiously devising
means to rid himself of at least some portion of this
enormous mass of unproductive gold by buying up U.S.
bonds not yet due, and anticipating interest on the U.S.
debt. But it is clear that so long as the U.S. policy of
Protection is continued the imports of gold must continue
and the abnormal position become more and more acute.
In France, which is also a highly protected country, the
Bank of France is overloaded with £98,000,000 of gold,
and is making Strenuous efforts to unload some portion
of it by making it compulsory for its customers, to whom
it had hitherto' paid in bank-notes, to take part payment
in gold. France also is stemming the tide by lending
largely its surplus capital to Russia.
When a protected country grows so rich that it
really doesn’t know what to do with its pile of
gold, it is truly a horrible example of the woes of
Protection. These two States had better adopt
Foreign-trade principles with all speed, lest their
pyramid of cash overbalances at the top, and falls
on them, and their backs are broken by t’ e shock.

“The Tariff Kills a Large Industry” was the
heading which appeared in big letters in a recent
issue of Sydney Telegraph. The observations
underneath it went on to explain that certain
customs regulations (which certainly seem rather
foolish and cumbersome) re empties and cases will
ruin the export trade in aerated vvaters, and
thereby “ destroy a large industry ” which employs
“over 1000 hands.” But in 1900 N.S.W. only
exported £2191 worth of locally manufactured
aerated waters (£l7B worth less than in 1899), and
it imported £0640 worth (£43 worth more than
in 1899). If the tariff stops imports, then it cer-
tainly appears that the industry will be a few
thousand pounds better off, even if the whole
export trade falls down as dead as Ananias
in the presence of Peter. Anyhow, the pretence
that an industry employing 1123 hands depends
on the miserable little fragment of export trade
that exists in Sydney, and will perish without it,
is a painfully silly fish-narrative.

B. MOUNTCASTLE & SONS, Brisbane, Q'laad
estates, but these amount to practically
nothing, as the close proximity of these districts
to non-prohibitive areas offers abundant facilities
for the purchase of liquor. To take the oft-
quoted instance of successful local prohibition
in the town of Pullman (U.S.) : by walking across
the street into the town of Kensington the inhabi-
tants of Pullman can buy all the liquor they want.
Similar circumstances would render the trial in
the Rocks valueless. Though countless temperance
reformers during the last century have tried to
find a solution of the great problem, up to now no
satisfactory scheme has been evolved. No doubt
the Gothenburg system has its advantages;
nevertheless, even temperance reformers admit
that it is not complete. As for prohibition ideas,
they are exploded. The new Government
spirit monopoly in Russia ' sheds a light
oil the problem, and it is worth while to
use that light in seeking for the solution.
The most significant feature of the experiment is
the fresh indication it affords of a growing sense
on the part of civilised states “ that the liquor-
traffic is of so dangerous a character that it cannot
safely be entrusted to the private trade, and also
that it is irrational to let the profits of this
monopoly pass into private hands.” The great
principle of the elimination of private profits from
the sale of drink is applied to the full extent.
This is the most promising idea up to date. It is
a national policy, and a national policy is wanted.
To reform two. or three streets and let all the
old evils rage in a hundred adjacent streets is
merely playing with the problem.

Wilson Barrett ntade his re-appearance in
Sydney under heavy circumstances on Saturday
night. The “Christian King” ia a play that is
full of armour and scripture and drawbacks.
Alfred the Great, as pourtrayed by Mr. Barrett,
is an ancient Saxon copy-book full of oppressive
maxims and weighty instances. His conversation
is that of an armed tract walking about in a
troubled land, or a moral pamphlet that has been
called to the throne in evil times. The play is
principally a procession of events, each of which
reflects a separate and disconnected dazzle on the
leading character, but it isn’t a play of any definite
human interest. All through it there is a large,
attitudinising Alfred with the limelight on him—
Alfred musing on his own unworthiness—Alfred
resisting the Danish siren who wants to be his
best girl—Alfred in the red sunset reflecting on
his unfitness to wear the crown of distracted
“ Engle-land ” —Alfred smiting the table a
severe biff as he directs all his private
wealth to be used to feed the poor
Alfred vowing to give half his riches to the poor
when he hasn’t any—Alfred calling on God 16
times—Alfred in a picturesque but useless atti-
tude on foot on the field of battle—Alfred on a
rampant horse on the field of battle (the public
gets, a glimpse later of an artificial horse ramping
by itself after the armv has departed)—Alfred
pardoning everybody—Alfred on deck on the
field of battle—Alfred seeing visions of the future
greatness of his country—Alfred on the field
of battle generally—a great, stilted, elocution-
ary Alfred doing everything except forgetting
his own greatness. Almost every time Alfred
finished speaking, the writer mechanically got out
threepence to put in the plate.
* * * *
Wilson Barrett is not a brilliant elocu-
tionist at any time, and he is not at his best in
the “Christian King.” He is a clean-shaved
monarch m a hairy land ; not because Alfred was
olean-shaved, but apparently because Wilson Bar-
rett is. Alfred goes without any serious attempt at
disguise into the Danish camp, and though he is
there as a spy, he sings a song of Saxon victory,
which naturally causes the Danes to rush at him
One might as well sing the Boer national anthem
to Fitzgibbon, of Melbourne Board of Shirks by
way of averting suspicion. He is saved, not by
his presence of mind, for he has very little, nor
by his faith, but because the girl who loved him
and who had tried to murder his wife, kills the
Dane who is about to give him away, and lies
about his identity to King Guthrum, and
finally buys his liberty by agreeing to give
up her body to that massive Pagan. She
delivers the body in a dead condition. This is
seemingly, intended to show the futility of faith’
and the advantage of having a prompt and self-
sacrificing girl who can do the perjury while the
man with the limelight on him keeps his soul
clean.
• ♦ • *
Fictorially, the “Christian King” is a
notable event. It is decorated with three tableaux
that have a great deal to say for themselves, and
the company, as a rule, does its best with its few
acting opportunities. Miss Lillah M’Carthy, the
passionate Zebuda with the dagger and the
poison-bottle, is an oasis in the great holy desert
of Alfred’s morality. Miss M’Carthy was a good
actress when Australia last saw her; she appears
to be even better now. Miss Edyth Latimer
plays the Queen brightly and naturally. James
Carter Edwards, Basil Gill, Ambrose Manning
George Majeroni, and several others represent
certain large and medium potentates of those
rough times in a capable fashion. They look in
keeping with the picture, while Alfred doesn’t.
Perhaps the recollection that Alfred was aged
about 22 at his accession, combined with Mr,
Barrett’s persistence in looking about 50, may
partly account for his want of perspective, so to
speak.
** * *
Horace Stanley’s drama, “ The 10.30
Down Express,” now blowing-off steam at Sydney
Lyceum, is not a play on railway life. The paste-
board engine appears only in one scene, and then
it appears mildly. The first act is brightened by
Ivy Gorrick as a Lost Woman in the nice, red
cloak tha,t signifies a hardened soul and unrepen-
tant spirit. She is killed straight off by her hus-
band, while good cousin Jack, whose name has
somehow or other got into tl:e marriage certificate
as that of the husband, is arrested for the crime.
Jack has previously putin a claim to marry heiress
Bessie, because he “is one of England’s bulwarks ”
(the dustman is also a bulwark, because he keeps
the nation from getting typhoid, but he doesn’t
aspire to marry heiresses on that account). How-
ever, the bulwark is taken to gaol, and Bert
Bailey, who saw the crime through being hidden
in a boat, is arrested as an accomplice. There is
no evidence against him, so, according to stage
law, he is judged guilty. There is no great evi-
dence against the hero either, except that he is
supposed to have married Ivy Gorrick ; it is pre-
sumed that anyone married to Ivy would want to
kill her. Later, the bulwark escapes and goes on
his knees to the villain to save him, after which
this paper lost all liking for the bulwark, and
didn’t trouble to wonder why the Queen decided
at this moment to send him a free pardon. The
stage sovereign always acts erratically in these
respects. Anyhow, in the next act he is married to
Bessie who has given up her billet as an heiress. He
also becomes a signalman, so that the villain may
deposit him on the rails, while the train that bears
the incriminating photographs which show the
villain to have been Ivy Gorrick’s real husband,
jerks along in the distance, and then emerges
gently in a larger edition from the side
scenes. The heroine having waved a red rag
then humps her bulwark along the lines a little
way, and the train noses at them till it finally
stops “just in time.” Later, the bulwark fills in
time by going to sea, when, with his usual luck,
his ship is posted as overdue. The heroine starves
gently among the washing which Ethel Buckley
takes in for a living. She is also lured to the hut
on the hill, and has a knife flourished at her, and
is tied up before an explosive substance and
rescued just in time, while the gunpowder goes off
harmlessly behind a rock. As soon as the bang
has subsided, the good characters all maintain
that their troubles are now over.
* • * *
Ivy Gorrick, though she appears only in
the first act, has more opportunity to show
her ability than in any previous play.
Also, she does show it. Bert Bailey
and Ethel Buckley act the usual comic
lovers, and Ethel looks very graceful when she
disguises herself as a sailor-boy in order to tell her
fiance that she is in love with herself. Temple
Harrison acts with considerable force as the
broken-down gamekeeper with the lost child.
Harrison is a wrecked personage in almost every
drama, and he invariably has a strayed child on
his gloomy soul. Helen Fergus is cruelly placed
as an ancient damsel on the look-out for a hus-
band, and engaged, when not occupied in the
search, in writing novelettes. When she becomes
a dissipated lady in a bicycle suit and a wheel-
barrow, the humor is dreadful.
# -* * *
“Said Pasha” continues to mildly amble
along at Sydney Criterion. Miss Josephine Stan-
ton has a, shapely voice and a pleasant figure, and
the public is waiting with some eagerness to see
, ( e D in a piece not quite so feebly impossible as
Said Pasha.” If “Said ” would even get up and
be vigorously impossible, he might take ; but his
harmlessness is hopeless. “Dorcas” is still said
tp be in preparation. The Bulletin urges
Dorcas ” to come without any more preparation,
even if she has to arrive with her hair in papers.
It doesn’t know “Dorcas” at all, but she is an
earnestly-required lady all the same.

** * *
Something new under the moon (the
Australian moon) has occurred—an open-air per-
formance of Shakespeare’s pastoral, “As You
Like It.” “Mona,” a private house at Darling
Point Road, Sydney, lent its grounds for two
nights and afternoons last week to that arch-flirt
Rosalind, divers banished noblemen, a fool (who
is wiser than many a cabinet minister), a pessi-
mist, some shepherdesses, lords, pages, and
foresters. They flirted and plotted and got bushed
in the name of charity—the Sick Children’s Hos-
pital Charity. And though so experienced and
capable an actor as Diver was Orlando and the
elocutionists Holman and Campbell were Touch-
stone and Jacques respectively, and though
the Palace Theatre managers dominated the ar-
rangements, the open-airing of the play can’t
be pronounced quite the success that Shakespeare
made the play itself. The weather will always
make its own arrangements. A chill night of
clouds and broken moonlight for the first produc-
tion, a pleasant one for the second, and for the
matinee a screamingly hot wind, a thunderstorm
and hailstones. In a little hollow between two
gigantic Norfolk pines, against a background of
shrub and palm, the stage was set. Stage furni-
ture consisted of prone tree-trunks and stumps,
and on either side of the pines the thick forest of
Mona gave natural dressing-rooms to the players
and cover for the musicians. The play ground
was well lighted, electric wires being run
overhead between the pines, but the audience
sat in depressing darkness, its units peer-
ing at their neighbors during the intervals
to find their identity, and Shivering in the chill
night. Orlando Diver wrestled well, and marred
the trees with writing love-songs on them, and let
himself be wooed right gracefully. Amiens (Mr.
J. S. Edwards), who has a pleasant tenor, did his
songs justice. Mrs. Ernest Coombe, as Rosalind,
displayed a Junoesque blonde presence, and the
highest-class legs ever displayed by any Rosalind,
professional or amateur. The other lady
amateurs were pretty and clear-voiced. Audrey
munched a turnip. Miss Curr, as Phebe, showed
promise of bigger things.
** * *
A great crowd at Melbourne Princess’s on
Saturday to welcome the Broughs, and a general
feeling of thankfulness for what was received.
“Lady Huntworth’s Experiment,” as The Bul-
letin stated not long since, is just a three-act
comedy by R. C. Carton, a particularly clever
playwriter, in whose work there is no bile, nor
any note of serious interrogation. This is a
comedy of character sketches, farcical arrange-
ments, and kitchen interest. Lady Huntworth’s
former husband threatens fora moment to become
a painful blackguard, but immediately thereafter
he gets overcome by drink, and is laid aside under
the sink in the scullery. After which re-assuring
episode Cecil Ward gathers up the limp
bundle of sin and humps him out into
the darkness, where he is said to repose
on the dry bed of a ditch until the
third act. The man in the ditch was Lady Hunt-
worth’s first Experiment, and she found him a
loathsome failure. For 10 long years he only
staggered home at night in order to be helped
upstairs by her ladyship. She nursed him through
many attacks of jim-jams, and kept a stiff upper
lip, whilst he spent all her money. Finally he
began to mingle his alcoholic anathemas with acts
of violence, whereat she started forth on her own
and took a situation as cook at Rev. Robert
Brough’s vicarage, under the name of Caroline
Somebody. In the comedy she is not so much a
woman with a past as a female with a tray, and,
when the plot thickens, she is mostly engaged in
washing-up.
• • * •
The cook-lady (Mrs. Brough) is free to
marry again, because her entertaining husband
had invented a divorce-case, which she didn’t
defend. And the four principal males in the
comedy are anxious to woo and win her as she
washes up. Even the soddened peer of the realm
seeks a re-union on the ground that she has come
into another fortune. A funereal man-servant, to
whom she has revealed new meanings in cutlets
and fritters and taught the difference between
’aricot and ’ash, makes gloomy overtures to
the cook. Also Rev. Brough desires her to
be his own little eggs-and-bacon till death
do them part. Likewise, Cecil Ward, the nephew
of Brough, offers the cook a warm personal friend-
ship with no ulterior motive in it, which is prac-
tically an assurance of a proposal in store for any
woman, of Caroline’s very keen intelligence.
“ Lady Huntworth’s Experiment ” has no special
results, except laughter in front of the house.
Miss Emma Temple elopes casually with a curate,
and Miss Susie Vaughan makes a favorable first
appearance in Melbourne as a terrible maiden
aunt, who discovers Cecil Ward hiding in the
broom cupboard. Leslie Victor re-appears in an
improved form as the lord who dosses in the ditch,
and Miss Gillies Brown is an amusingly exag-
gerated Keziah. The other people from Sydney
repeat their share in the successful little piece.
** * «
.‘‘Hearts are Trumps, at Melb. Royal,
strikes one as being a drama of such exceptional
vastness and complicated mechanism that it will
need half-a-dozen performances to get it thoroughly
fit, in a horsey way of speaking. “The White
Heather ” was probably less expensive than the
show which Bland Holt staged for the first time
On Saturday. Spectacle is piled on spectacle,
until the gorgeousness of the first act is
squashed out of recollection, and the
events' that took place between 9 and
10 p.m. are as dreams of long ago. At
11.30 on Saturday night Miss Harrie Ireland
was hanging over an Alpine chasm with Bland
Holt tied to the other end of the rope, and just as
the managerial schemer was about to cut the rope
and drop Harrie into eternity for the sake of her
insurance money, a curate came flying over the
abyss from an adjacent Alp to rescue the girl.
After which Bland was swept away by a land-
slip, and Providence erected the proceeds of an
avalanche to his memory. ,
** * *
“ Hearts Are Trumps,” by Cecil Raleigh,
has bright dialogue and a panoramic story to
recommend it. The sensations are comparatively
rational, and the company get some inducement
to make the most of their characters. Miss Ross,
as the titled mother of Miss Ireland, and a mar-
tyr to the baccarat habit, loses her money with
great expression. Also, she betrays considerable
motherly feeling when her fine, tall child appears
in brown tights at the Frivolity music-hall, with
a view to sacrificing the esteem of her family
connections for a salary of £4O a week. Mrs.
Bland Holt is effective in a music-hall star
capacity, and Holt (of the avalanche) is
a deeper and more serious Jew money-
lender than usual. Walter Baker on this
occasion hides a large portion of his identity
in the character of a battered old Australian mil-
lionaire, who turned out to be a long-lost rela-
tive. Miss Florence Seymour, Styan, Albert
Norman, and 30 other people, appear in the cast.
About 20 female gamblers wear gorgeous apparel
without getting their bare names or necks into
the programme. Brunton’s scenery is admirable
in many different stagings, and when the sunrise
on the Swiss Alps is a week older, this journal
will endeavour to say which particular section of
“Hearts are Trumps” is the prime part of the
joint.

At Her Majesty’s (Melb.) a revival of
“The Gondoliers,” advertised for Saturday, was
postponed on account of something the matte,r
with Kenningham’s foot, and “Florodora” broke
out again in the same old place. At time of
writing “The Gondoliers” is under orders to
transpire next Saturday (2nd Nov.), but mean-
while the people of the gay-tinted island where
the bottled perfume comes from are having
their bumps felt by Tweedlepunch. A new
item in the festivities of Cyrus Gilfain is
a song and dance by the hunky kid who revealed
her remarkable young voice in “The Casino Girl.”
Otherwise “Florodora” is mueh the same as it
was before, when Kenningham and Brownlow
happened to be ou 1 ; of the bill. Brownlow left
Melbourne some days ago to take the place of
Bathurst, on tour—Bathurst being violently ill, it
was said. But, inasmuch as the last-named bari-
tone is now filling Brownlow’s place at Her
Majesty’s, it would seem that the other baritone
has changed sicknesses with him.

“Tom, Dick, and Harry,” and its system
of doggerel rhyme advt. appear to be flourishing
with a great flourish at the Bijou, and no change
of programme will cast a shadow before until next
Saturday at the earliest. Wednesday of this week
was to be a soldier and sailor night, when mem-
bers of the local Scottish Regiment could get
admitted at half-price if they came in full evening
dress as regards their legs.
* iff * *
Fitzgerald’s circus building, which holds
5600 mortals, when they are all there, was crowded
every night last week. Melbourne, although a
fickle show-town in some respects, remains ever
faithful to the entertainment that radiates from
Dan Fitz. and his veteran piebald pupil, Commo-
dore. The circus, these times, is not overloaded
with horse, yet it begins and ends with remark-
able displays of that noble animal. The gymnasts
are indispensable, the girl-juggler grows on
acquaintance, the elephant and lion supply a want,
and the greyest jokes of the arena would be missed
if they were gone, but the gentleman that pays
the rent and provides the expensive fittings is a
party by the name of Gee-gee. Take away the
horse and the equestrian performers have nothing
left to stand on.

At Poverty Point.
Tyrone Power’s unfortunate venture at Melb. Princess’s
should be one more object-lesson to managers in the use-
lessness of preliminary puffs and photographs where there
is nothing startling to ski-te about. Power’s first piece
was about the most advertised thing that Melbourne has
known. It started with a £260 house on the Saturday,
and the Monday’s advts. in the dailies were a column
long. Yet you canit always make the public believe
that a duck is a goose.
Tyrone Power’s schedule is a bit of a revelation.
Insolvent, who opened Melb. Princess’s Sept. 7th, and
is said to have had £260 in the house on his first
night, states that he “ became unable to pay his debts
about 25th Sept.” He sets down his professional expenses
at £l2OO per month, his house-expenses at £6O per
month, and his debts on Oct. 23rd at £1623. The sole
assets are scenery and theatrical properties, valued at
£250, and held as security for £385 of rent due to G. Mus-
grove. So Tyrone Power’s expenses during his six weeks’
term of management were £lB9O, of which £1373, in-
cluding £2OO borrowed money, remains unpaid after
reckoning the £250 of scenery and props, as cash security
There were six weeks’ receipts at the theatre (beginning
with the £260 first night house) to pay the £617, or, say
£Bl7, difference. How much capital) then, did Tyrone
Power put into his business at the outset? Tyrone
Power’s managerial expenses appear to have begun at the
photographers. He owes the photo, man £B9. Two
florists want £l7, which suggests that the tributes of
floral esteem were large and frequent (a M.L. paper, by the
way, foolishly remarked that there were three eartioads
of them one night). Drapery firms figure on the schedule
for £2OO ; the unpaid liquor bill is 16guineas, and so on.
Orchestras don’t always strike—they sometimes do
worse. Writer remembers a night at the old Alexandra
when the unpaid musicians, instead of “going out,” re-
mained in, blaring hideous sarcasms and cacophonous
derision through their trumpets and things at the actor-
manager every time he tried to get something pathetic
romantic, or noble off his swelling chest.
People in Melbourne have lost confidence in Dan Fitz-
gerald’s tame she elephant. When the circus was making
itself at home on the new pitch, the lady elephant who
was disgusted with all the worry and racket attendant
upon furnishing the new home, gave way to her feelings
and, catching up a casual laborer, held him high in the
air as if to fling him out of the electorate. When Lizzie
was persuaded to let go, the man had to leave for the
hospital.
Stage hands at a recent Melb. charity matinee saw one
of their jokes go woefully astray. A budding actor-author
was just completing the performance when, amid cries of
“ Get work ! ” and other mild pleasantries of a like nature
from the gallery, a “property” bouquet, composed of
oolored rags and other materials, was handed up from the
front. The posy was plainly labelled for the aspiring
author, but the gentleman was so flustered that he
failed to recognise the fake, and presented it with
a profound bow to a lady performer. She, thinking- that
an affront was intended, indignantly refused the proffered
gift, and afterwards rushed from the theatre to go
straight home and cry her eyes out at the insult put upon
Lauri’s disregard for his own skin, when he throws
himself about in “ The Casino Girl,” is said to be due to
rivalry with Ward. The business is not in the original
version, and was introduced by Lauri to “ build up” his
part. The eight or nine stalwart people, who have to
stand round and catch the tubbv George feel their
responsibility mightily, for he trusts them so absolutely
that any one man’s failure to get a grip at the right
moment would probably mean serious injury to the fat
little funny man.

Wilson Barrett went through the latter part of his
Adelaide season suffering great pain from an obscure
growth near the optic nerve. He was finally obliged to
telegraph to Melbourne for the surgeon who had <dven
him relief there, and who joined him in Adelaide in’dine
to travel back with him.

What Luck ! In ’B5, when sinking for coal at
Ballimore, near Dubbo, boring was stopped through the
outflow of water, which turned out to be the finest
natural mineral water in the market, called Zetz Spa.*
• • • *
A very successful trial of the Gardner Electric
Drill took place at the works of the Austral Otis Com-
pany, South Melbourne, a few days ago. The drill,
although only temporarily fixed, bored 8A inches in three
minutes through hard basalt. A number of prominent
mining men witnessed the test, and expressed themselves
as greatly pleased with the machine. The Austral Otis
Company have been appointed Sole Agents in Australasia
for the Gardner Electric Drill.*

» * * *
If further proof is needed that Luhrig Tanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Yanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempriere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

Mr. Mephan Ferguson, Engineer, Ironfounder,
&c., of Melbourne, Adelaide, and Perth, reports that the
great demand for spiral riveted pipes has necessitated the
increase of manufacturing facilities at his Pootscray
works, and he is now in a position to supply orders at
very short notice, for all sizes of pipes from six to twenty
inches in diameter, with either patent cast-iron bolted
joints, flanged joints or the ordinary spigot and faucet
lead joints. Mr. Ferguson also manufactures boilers,
pumps, fluming, patent Nelyambo earth-scoops or tank-
cleaners, rabbit exterminators, refrigerators, ice-making
machinery, bolts, nuts, forgings, and all sorts of iron-work.*

The Wild Cat Column.
Queensland proposes Issuing a little local loan
of £100,(XXI at 3J per cent. This country lias been having a
great time in the loan market since 1st July, 19J1. Here
are the loans floated or announced during the four
months, barring certain small local sales in Adelaide and
elsewhere, of which'ho exact record is available :—
N.S. Wales ..£4,307,760 S. Australia .. £850,000
Westralia .. 1,500,000 Federal loan
Queensland .. 1,474,213 (proposed).. 1,000,000
Tasmania .. 650,000
This makes a total of £9,781,973 either actually borrowed
or announced—about £7,500,000 has already been bor-
rowed—in four months. The actual borrowings of July,
August, September, and October break the record for the
whole of last year, and last year broke the record of every
previous year since 1891. And it is understood that both
Victoria (for probably £500,000) and Maoriland (for pro-
bably £1,000,000) will shortly be holding out the cus-
tomary hat in the usual quarter—just to round things
off, as it were. A shade over a million of Australian loan
moneys has already gone in discounts and expenses this
year, for this country’s credit is low just now, and its
3 per cent, bond for £100 fetches about £90 net.

The profits represent £4 Is. 9d. per cent, on the share-
holders’ funds" as at the beginning of the half-year (capital
£1,498,220, and reserves £69,654 ; total, £1,567,874)—a
very material advance on the £3 12s. 8d. per cent, earned
in the previous half-year. The National Bank, having
completely emerged from the reconstruction period, is
now fast regaining its old position. It is a substantial
concern, with over £6,000,000 of deposits and £2,920,814
of liquid assets, including £1,502,943 cash and £763,640 of
State and municipal securities. At the same time, its
future depends, like that of most of the reconstructs, on
the rebuilding of the reserves. They stood at £680,488
when the unpleasant thing happened in ’93, and, if they
are to assume anything like solid dimensions again
within a reasonable time, it is necessary to treat the ordi-
nary shareholders in a grudging spirit, and not to be too
friendly even with the preference crowd.
It is a notable fact, by the way, and one that
looks none too well for business generally, that the
National Bank’s figures show a decline in a number of
directions. Compared with six months ago, note-circula-
tion, Government and private deposits, liquid securities
and advances have all diminished somewhat. This
doesn’t necessarily mean anything of great importance,
but a similar tendency is noticeable just now with
many institutions.

The earnings are equivalent to £6 12s. 7d. per cent, on

menced. This is a very handsome rate, and the Colonial
Bank could have paid a 5 per cent, dividend on both pre-
ference and ordinary capital (preference capital, £304,044;
ordinary ditto, £133,213; total, £437,257; 5 per cent,
dividend thereon, £10,931) and still had £4138 to add to
its reserves. But it was undoubtedly the wiser course to
let the ordinary shareholders wait a little longer. The
last of the reconstructed deposits (£111,358) are being
released at the end of this year, and it is unwise to dissi-
pate cash needlessly till this liability is met. Moreover,
so much money disappeared in the bad days that the
total shareholders’ funds (capital and reserves) are even
now less than £469,000. This isn’t much for a bank of
the Colonial’s age and reputation to inspire confidence
with, and it is highly advisable to give the reserve every
possible chance to grow up again.
Further, despite the increasing prosperity of the
Colonial Bank and the fact that its new deposits
have increased by a little over £15,000 during the
half-year, cash and liquid securities (£497,834) are
£133 663 less than they were six months ago. This
is, doubtless, partly owing to the repayment of re-
constructed deposits. It is also, no doubt, partly
owing to the fact that the cash resources of Australia
have shown a diminishing tendency of late. The Colonial
Bank holds £374,266 cash and £11,845 of State and
municipal securities, and this second item would stand a
good deal of strengthening with advantage.

The County of Bourke Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Society has a paid-up capital of £98,150, and as
its deposits are less than £10,000 (they were about
£160,000 when the bank panic struck it, and it was
forced to suspend), it now trades almost entirely with
its own funds, and can’t be again rushed by a scared
public. The only drawback about its apparent splendid
prosperity is that the £6770 added to reserves was almost
wholly derived from recoveries from capital previously
written off as lost, and the earnings of the current busi-
ness, until the present year, didn’t leave any great margin
over and above the dividend. Still they leave a very
handsome margin this year, and the improvement seems
likely to be permanent.

“A Protectionist” (Wedge Bay, Tasmania):
The reason why N.S.YV. has, in the last 29 years, had
more loan money coming in over and above the interest
that went out than Victoria was simply that N.S.W. s
heaviest borrowing has been in recent years, while Vic-
toria’s heaviest borrowing happened a long time ago. If
£10,000,000 was borrowed last year at 4 per cent, only
£400,000 has gone back in interest; if it was borrowed 20
years ago, then £8,000,000 has gone back in interest. If
Victoria borrowed another £10,000,000 now, the in-
flux of money over and above the outflow of interest
would rise at once from £1,983,000 to £llg>B3,ooo.

The Colonial Permanent Building and Invest-
ment Society (Melbourne):
Loss on Written off Total
Business. Properties. Deficit.
September, 1897 £1397
„ 1898 £284 £9827 11,508
„ 1899 559 12,067
„ 1900 548 12,615
„ 1901 519 1005 14,169
The paid-up capital is £48,000, of which £33,831 is still
intact—on paper anyhow. A certain amount of progress
is being made at reducing the liabilities to depositors and
mortgagees, though it can hardly be described as rapid
progress. These are the figures :
Owing to Creditors other than Shareholders.
September, 1898 .. £57,911 September, 1900 .. £52,796
„ 1899 .. 54,949 „ 1901 .. 51,670
Two matters which stand out conspicuously here are
that the loss on the actual business of the society is a
fraction more this year than it was in 1900, and the
amount paid off the liabilities is a good deal less. In 1899
the liabilities were reduced by £2962 ; in 1900 by £2153 ;
in 1901 by only £1126. This is the natural result of the
state of suspended animation which the institution (like
many other institutions) has drifted into. Australia is
full of building societies that are doing no new business
and getting no new deposits, but merely realising their
foreclosed properties (their assets are almost all foreclosed
properties), and gradually paying off their old depositors.
As the institution grows smaller in this way the earnings
grow less, even though the remaining properties may be
increasing in value, and as the office, manager, board of
directors, gas-bill, and many other charges remain just
the same they swallow up the profits. Australia
is full of the tail-ends of once great building
societies —fragments whose assets now, in many
cases, yield a very respectable return per cent.
But it would take four or five of them to make a respect-
able institution, and the earnings can’t stand the cost of
four or five managements when there should only be one.
If such a concern as the Colonial Permanent B. and I.
Society could persuade its shareholders to take up
£50,000 worth of preference shares, pay off all the old
liabilities, keep the foreclosed properties till better times
arrive, and go into active business again on a basis
that would enable it to get a moderate amount of new de-
posits, it is quite possible that it could pay moderate
dividends on both its old and new capital. Half the
Australian building societies nowadays are comatose con-
cerns that have to hire a manager and office merely to
look at the foreclosed assets.

The Australian Mutual Investment and Build-
ing Co. (Sydney) was one of the landmarks of the boom,
and 10 years ago its 25 per cent, dividends were the pride
and glory of that noble profession which consisted mostly
in A buying land from B on bills, and selling it to C on
bills, and discounting the bills with D, who generally had
no money of his own, but did the discounting with F’s
deposits. Whereupon A was enabled to declare a big
dividend, and as the chief shareholders in A were
fenerally B, C, and D, things were comfortable for every-
ody except F. The Australian Mutual Investment and
Building Co., having made almost the record boom of the
business, has also made almost the record burst. These
are its doings in recent years :
Loss on Loss on Accumulated
Business. Property. Deficit.
1894 £99,562
1895 £5,946 £93,176 198,684
1896 174 94 198,952
1897 1,161 7,740 207,853
1898 736 2,381 210,970
1899 467 92 211,529
1900 255 211,784
The co. has a paid-up capital of £57,058, so the prospective
loss to the creditors, even if the remaining assets realise
their book value (which is not invariably the case with
assets), is about £155,000. The co. owes £183,941 to de-
positors and others, and the paper value of its assets—
mostly unsold lands—is £29,215. No interest, of course,
has been paid to depositors for many years. The losses
are comparatively small nowadays, mainly because almost
everything is already lost. The depositors received 6d. in
the £ in 1897 ; Id. in the £ in 1898 ; 3d. in the £ in 1899 ;
and Id. in the £in 1900. Altogether an historical concern.

* » * »
Lead got down to £ll ss. in mid-October, a fall
of 10s. in a week, and of £6 7s. 6d. in 12 months. Nobody
seems to have the smallest idea what the future is to be.
Meantime the Pinnacles, which was only kept going
by the unexpected discovery of a good run of easily-
dressed stuff, has sent all hands off ; and Block 10, which
must have been losing from £2OOO to £3OOO a month,
is only keeping open because the metal buyers will not
cry off contracts, which run a little past the end of the
year. The British has proved a very good body of ore at
the 500 ft. —hitherto not u ached —and was considering
whether it could not start milling again when the slump
to £ll ss. drove those thoughts out of its head. The
prospecting in this mine, away from this one point—
Howell’s 500 ft. working—is still unsatisfactory. The
Junction is in the middle of a reconstruction, whose de-
tails have already been discussed, but there is still no
move towards the amalgamation of the mines of the
northern group, without which cheap enough work is
impossible. Not one of the co’s, for one thing, can
afford to engage the first-class manager that is
necessary. For some time past there has been a
shocking waste of money. The North mill and re-treat-
ing plant (last remark doesn’t apply to this) are nearly
completed ; but the lead market must improve before it
can make divs. B.H. Prop, is now trying the Hunting-
ton-Heberlein desulphurising process at Port Pirie ; it is
stated that it improves the lead-extraction (it is a mid-
process between concentration and smelting), but no
figures are available. For the present the co. is not
doing anything with the ridiculously-boomed Carmichael
process of the same character. The co. has not got
through its trouble with the local Miners Association
yet. The men want a fairer contract form, and they
are thoroughly justified in seeking it, for the present one
is grotesquely lop-sided. The complaint as to poor pay
is less sound ; the average is much above the standard
miner’s pay of 9s. The men who really deserve pity are
the companies seven-and-six a day laborers ; how they
exist on the money in a place where living is so dear is a
mystery. The A.M.A. contains some members who
threaten ; but its numbers are now weak, and its influ-
ence has been greatly reduced by its acceptance of log-
rolling outsiders as members. The Central (Sulphide
Corporation ) still does well ; it is stated to be also satis-
fied with the new zinc-extraction process (Metternich)
which it is using. In fact, about the most satisfactory
feature in the present gloomy situation is the firmness of
the zinc market. The South is ready at last to use its
extended ore-dressing plant ; but it is significant that
here, too, development-work has been much reduced.
♦ * * •
The Overflow, N.S.W. : Sanguine anticipations
have not been realised. They seldom are anywhere ; with
a N.S.W. mine, never. The reconstruction of the co.,
arrangements for new plant, erection of same, and starting
of smelting operations were all duly chronicled, also that
the smelter had been blown-in successfully, and that the
first matte had been tapped. Then came an ominous
silence, so strange to the amateur mining juggins, and so
familiar to men who have been there before. But the
truth has to come out at last, and the best possible polish
is always put on failures. “ Some little difficulty was ex-
perienced through an insufficiency of experienced furnace-
men ; the furnace had to be blown-out after three days
owing to the failure in the delivery of ironstone flux, and
it will take another week to arrange a satisfactory de-
livery from the co.’s own leases.” The man who supplied
the ironstone flux defends himself and wants to know
why they have gone so many miles further away for it,
and what about the eating-out of the bricks of the furnace
before it had been running three days? Something of
this sort happened before at the mine, and apparently
it has occurred again. “ The matte and bullion tapped
were of a high-grade quality, but the quantity produced
could not be estimated, owing to a large absorption by
the crucible.” It is always something. Often the metal
is in the tailings, sometimes in the slag, at others in the
concentrates ; occasionally it is escaping in flue-
dust, but alas ! too often it is not in the ore to start
with. The Bulletin does not say this of the Overflow, of
which it has always held a comparatively good opinion,
but that it is a mismanaged concern, and always has
been, is beyond the shadow of a doubt, and, good as the
mine may be, it is not good enough to work it as a
smelting proposition so far away from a railway. It
wants a better mine than the Overflow has yet proved
itself to stand a long road-haulage for fluxes and a much
longer one for coke.
* * * *
Grassy Gully, N.S.W., still keeps up its repu-
tation as a mine of humor. “It is officially reported that
Mr. Kavanagh, one of the directors, has just returned
from a visit to the mine ; he confirms a recent good report
by the manager, and explains that the richest stone occurs
alongside the richest portion previously mined, only being
separated by a few inches of rock. The newly-discovered
make of gold is in three veins aggregating 12in., which
are comprised in 2ft. 6in. of stone ; this makes the reef
double the width it was previously thought to be.”
Apart from the official report of Mr. Kavanagh’s
return, the rest of the information is as clear as
a Chinese puzzle. Then there is Mr. W. H. O.
Lovely’s report:—“ Five men at work on rich
ore and keeping the battery going two shifts ; battery
yielding one ounce gold per ton, and the tailing slimes
run 12dwt. and 1 to 4 ozs. per ton respectively, making a
yield of above 2oz. per ton. It would be much higher if
it were possible to break the ore separate from the mul-
lock adjoining it.” Wages are estimated at £25 per week,
and the value of yield from battery and cyanide plant
£lOO per week. This is the mine of which, fully a month
ago, the manager undertook to astonish the share-
holders with its “ astounding richness.” Every report,
no matter from whom, gives itself away. For instance, take
this present one. The battery is going two shifts on rich
ore, and the yield from it and the cyanide plant, from ore
worth over 2oz. per ton, is only £lOO per week. Fifteen
tons of 2oz. ore would be worth more than £lOO, and a
10-head battery, as this mine has, should crush that in
24 hours. “ Owing to the limited amount of development
done, it is advisable to keep a couple of men in each of
the bottom drives, so as to open out the good stuff below.”
Well, if this does not show the insignificance of the mine,
it certainly does of the management.

Peak Hill Proprietary, N.S.W. : Chairman to
shareholders : “ Negotiations for the disposal of the co.’s
property, or the formation of a large co., in London, are
still proceeding; meantime operations at the mine are
being confined to the crushing of ore obtained by
tributors and the cyaniding of tailings. Should negotia-
tions now pending in London not succeed, active opera-
tions will be resumed at the mine.” They have been
pending for a mighty time. How long have the
public wearied of reading of what a magnificent div.
would be declared if only baronet Samuel succeeded in
placing the mine in London, whither, it was given out,
he had gone for that special purpose, and of how cheap
the shares were with this result in view ? Hope deferred
in a London flotation of a N.S.W. mine makes share-
holders’ hearts grow sick, and the flotation of this one
there is beyond even hope. While the war is on, even a
cork-mine would not float in London.

Great Boulder : Main lode intersected at 1400 ft.
level, value 28dwt. and pierced by a diamond bore at
1500 ft., value 15dwt. 00. now testing the lease which
runs around the northern boundary of the Ivanhoe, the
ore-shoots of which dip into the deeper ground of the
lease but do not exist, or, if they do, have little or no
value in the upper ground. Stated that some consterna-
tion was recently caused amongst London shareholders
by a rumor that the main lode of the Boul-
der was rapidly dipping west into the Golden
Horseshoe ground,- and this despite the fact
that in the deeper levels the lode is nearer the
Boulder main shaft than in the levels above. The lodes
in the Boulder, as well as in other mines, have a tendency
to dip to the east; the notable exception is the Golden
Horseshoe lode which came in from the Boulder, but
there are evidences, of course not admitted by the G.H.
crowd, that, sooner or later, the lode will resume its
original course and get back into the mines from which it
came. Six additional furnaces are being put up on the
Boulder. This probably does not mean an increased ton-
nage, but that it is intended to discard the old 30-head
stamp-mill, now almost worn out, treat the whole of the
ore from the mine per sulphide process—and get ready
for the time when the tailings, which have for the last
two years been such a magnificent asset, become ex-
hausted.
■ * * »
Lake View Consols, W. A.: “ The management,
it is reported, is in conflict over the present policy of
working the mine. The consulting engineer, Mr.
M’Kinnon, has officially reported that the mine cannot
keep up the present output. This the manager, Mr.
Hartman, stoutly denies, and he challenges the directors
to have an independent examination made of the pro-
perty. Intense interest is manifested here over the
controversy. It is reported that the Board has requested
Mr. Hewitson and Mr. Hamilton to inspect and report.
When interviewed to-day, Mr. Hartman declined to
communicate any information for publication. Mr.
Hamilton was also seen, but he stated that he has not
yet received any official request to act.” And this is
published in the eastern papers, which for months,
no doubt from the same source, have been booming
the great capabilities of this mine—that the plant
was being so enlarged as to treat one-half more tonnage
per month ; that the gold-production would be oorre-
spondingly greater ; that while it was now turning ovef
14,0000 z. per month from 10,000 tons the gold production
when the larger plant got to work would be over 18,000 oz.
per month ; that the yield, great as it was, could, with-
out doubt, be maintained for long to come without any
perceptible strain on the apparently inexhaustible ore-
reserves in the mine; and that development in
different directions and various depths warranted
the policy of the management. That M'Kinnon is
right and Hartman wrong is well known throughout
the field ; probably to none better than the newspaper
mine-boomers who sent along the wires to the opposite
effect. The same remark will apply to other mines there,
particularly the Horseshoe and the Ivanhoe, whose plants
will soon be much bigger than the mines. The game of
making a market for shares by putting up big plants has
been played all over the world, but nowhere probably to
such an extent as at Kalgoorlie. Big as the mines are,
there is not one of them, except the Boulder, sufficiently
developed to justify an increase of plant. The Boulder
is the only mine that has done its development first, and
although it is more than double any other mine on the
field in size, it does not want any bigger plant to
deal with its ore reserves. In all other cases the cart has
been put before the horse, involving an expenditure of
hundreds of thousands of pounds long before it was
wanted, and if, as likely, these plants are never wanted,
the money will be thrown away. Of course the object
usually is to work the market and not the mines.
« • • *
Bucldand River dredge (Vic.): Well as this
dredge has been doing, the shareholders are told it will
do much better when it reaches the richer ground ahead.
Never a dredge started but that it had better ground
ahead, and the public have long tired of the wheeze, par-
ticularly in view of the cold-blooded fact that, in quite a
number of cases, dredges which started with an average
yield of 30 and 40 ozs. per week, and were going to reach
better ground ahead, have now to put up with less than
half their former yields.
* * * •
Things have not been too rosy in Zeehan (Tas.)
district for the last few months, owing to low lead and
silver prices. The local galena still keeps up its high
average of silver and lead. There are but two mines now
working on wages, others being under tributors,
whose presence enables lessees to comply with labor
conditions when shareholders refuse to pay calls. The
tonnage raised is well maintained, but the net proceeds
are woefully small. Storekeepers, publicans and middle-
men generally are all in the dumps. To keep
their spirits up the Tas. press has hit upon the happy
idea of valuing the ore raised at the total London value
of the metals it contains. For instance, the British
Zeehan, a Bull-owned mine, exported for the
quarter ended Sept. 30th ore valued by the press
at £20,782, for which the co., after deducting all
smelting charges, freight, etc., received only £10,575.
The export for Aug. in that quarter was 379 tons, contain-
ing 33,1980 z. silver and 172 tons lead, which the press
valued at £73Bu, and for which the co. got £4390. The
Mines Department returns are only a trifle more reliable.
Instead of obtaining from the lessees the net price
received by them for their ore, and adding to it the rail-
way freight to the port, and publishing the same as the
actual value to the country of the ore exported, the
department takes the gross total value and tonnage as
published by the press and deducts only £4 perton from it.
Yet the average deductions for the ore of the whole dis-
trict amount to about £6 per ton. The average monthly
output is 1500 tons, the gross value whereof the press
usually states at £30,000, but the net value is really about
£20,000. This sum has to support a population of 5000,
and pans out at £1 per week a head, which explains,the
present pallid looks of local business people, not to men-
tion the abnormal increase of compulsory teetotalism.

SOCIETY
Softgoods Sargood has been presented
with the blessed flag which was hoisted at Melb.
Exhibition building last May when the Duchess
of York gave the signal for the general flutter of
Union Jacks over the State schools of Victoria.
This, in consideration of said Sargood’s splendid
loyalty in organising the universal flutter, and
providing a considerable quantity of the “Grand
Old Flag ” material from his warehouse stock.
Probably it isn’t all the reward that Sargood ex-
pected for his trouble, but he must make the best
of it. The Bulletin would suggest that he have
it made up into a vest and a pair of pants at his
factory.
* * » •
Probably the selection of Lord Dundonald
to boss the Australian forces would be a fair
success as military appointments go. Dundonald
was the cavalry commander with Gen. Buller’s
army in Natal, and his confession of contempt for
the old cavalry methods in which he had been
trained brought him under favorable notice some
12 months ago. But then the lord who shows
symptoms of ordinary intelligence is very apt to
be credited with more brains than he really has.
Seen through an atmosphere of astonishment, his
“broad views” appear much broader than they
are. He gets over-praised to the same extent as a
parson who talks common-sense concerning Jonah
and the whale.

A returned M.L. cohentingenter cele-
brated the receipt of his back-pay by running off
with the wife of an ultra-Jingo magnate. The
husband feels his injury so keenly that he is
becoming quite a pro-Boer. But lie should re-
cognise that a little allowance must be made for
habits acquired in extending the Empiah.

The method adopted for collecting money
for the national war tombstone in S.A. is for two
r eturned troopers to travel round the country with
a lecture, a bioscope, a horse, and a dog—who has
himself been to the war—with a money-box tied
to his neck. Thus the provincial penny is raked
ia. What a squalid monument it will be!

In a young Sydney municipal!,,; a royal
row raged the other week over the disfigure-
ment of its new Council Chambers by the per-
petuation on the wall m huge letters of the
Mayor’s name. The Mayor maintained his right
to do as he pleased, and supported his action by
remarking that when Edward VII. and Julius
<'aesar entered London their names were em-
blazoned on the walls ! !
The King has pardoned the Australians
in S’Africa found guilty of mutiny ; the Empire
just now can’t afford to lose its Antipodean supply
of farm-burning patriotism. There has been, so
far, however, no revision of the court martial
sentences on Boer prisoners. In their case Jack
Ketchener does his work in peace.

A returned cohentingenter, charged with
two thefts at Mt. Morgan (Q.), confessed guilt,
and, in extenuation, pleaded “ the life I’ve been
leading the last 18 months.” Precisely : the
curses we sent against the Boers are coming home
to roost.

More Pink-hill ! Colonel Porter, of the
Maoriland 7th Contingent, wires Seddon :
Engaged Botha’s force 6th inst. Battle lasted eight
hours. Enemy, 2000 strong, defeated at all points. Maori-
landers charged and captured first position, important
hill on enemy’s right flank, held it under exceedingly
heavy fire concentrated from front and flanks. Maori-
landers were first in action ; their courage and relia-
ance being highly estimated. They are consequently
always employed to carry out most hazardous duties.
Contingent’s casualties, two killed and seven wounded.
Battle, eight hours ! Enemy, 2000. Maori-
landers—?? Exceedingly heavy fire. Two killed,
seven wounded—nine effective shots ! Ye gods !
It recalls a battle which present writer once saw
between two large parties of Chinese on the
Etheridge river. The fight lasted 24 hours, and
in the end one Chinaman was so badly hurt that
he had to stay in his bunk for over two days with
slices of fowl’s gizzard tied over his sore knee.
* * * *
Mr. O’Neill, Bathurst (N.S.W.) Police
Magistrate, rose up with a loud begorra, the other
day, to condemn the State education system as
Godless ; to remark that, whilst Roman Catholics
went to visit cemeteries out of piety, the Pro-
testant tribes only frequented them from levity ;
and generally to orate as a pulpiteering Boanerges.
Coming from a man in a’judicial position this
language naturally caused comment, and Attorney-
General B. R. Wise had to rebuke the flamboyant
sectarian. The rebuke was a model of mild,
fatherly reproof. Addressed to the right sort of
man, it should have more effect than the most
savage of censures ; but it is very doubtful whether
Mr. O’Neill is that sort of man.

Decided in two cases at Port Adelaide
recently that a foreign seaman has no redress in
the Courts here against his captain for brutal
treatment, where he has signed (as, of course, he
always has) the ship’s articles under his own
nation’s maritime law. Wherefore, Captain
Czilnlski can continue to hammer the head of
Antonio Yvtzkoff with a belaying pin indefinitely,
even in Australian waters, so long as he is careful
that the A. B. does not live to get back home to
complain there.

A well-known Sydney physician is said to
have bought a large property on King’s Tableland,
between Lawson and Wentworth Falls, for the
purpose of establishing a consumptive home.
Dr. Ross, member for Molong, N.S.W.,
talks nonsense when he says that smoking is “at
the root of all the cancer that exists.” Women, who
don’t smoke, are just as subject to cancer as men.
Occasional cases of lip and throat can-
cer can be attributed to abuse of smok-
ing causing local
irritation ; and
that is all.

Money-lender Cohen, an ex-mayor and an
ex-M.P., is the Snow tramway-grab candidate for
Adelaide mayoralty, and Alderman Wells is the
champion of munieipalisation. The fight, which
will be bitter and strenuous, takes place on
December 1. Every ratepayer has a vote,
while some with property in each of the six wards
have half-a-dozen votes.

Claimed by Cardinal Moran that of 1000
supposed “incurable” patients treated at St.
Vincent’s “Hospice for the Dying” at Sydney,
440 had been discharged as cured. Verily the age
of miracles is not past.
A movement is again afoot to start
a Woman’s Club in Sydney. Entrance
fee, five shillings. That ought to provide
powder-puffs and looking-glasses anyhow,
and what more does woman want in her
club ?
* * * *
North Sydney’s Mayor, in a vain
attempt to justify the decision of the coun-
cil to defile the harbor foreshores under
its control with hoardings, alleges that the
N.S.W. Railway Commissioners and the
Harbor Trust are also sinners. As a
matter of fact, the Harbor Trust has set
its face against vandalism : and the Rail-
way Commissioners will probably be
taught their duty by Parliament. Mean-
while, North Sydney still reaches out
eagerly for the £264 a year which is the
price of its degradation.
“Gadfly”: lie consumptive remedies. In
De Quincy’s “ Suspiria de profundis ” he says he
was given up as a hopeless consumptive by two of
the most eminent men of that time —his weak
chest and a hereditary tendency to phthisis
seemingly bearing them out. His cure he ascribes
to his liberal use of opium, and he certainly lived
to a comparatively old age, using opium to the
last. Has such a treatment ever been otherwise
recommended ?
• * * *
The N.S.W. “Crown” took a point
against itself in court the other day that it had no
jurisdiction to try an alleged post-office offence,
as the Federal Government possessed exclusive
rights of control in postal matters. So far the
Federal Government has set Up no judicial sys-
tem ; if, then, the contention is correct, it would
be possible for a letter-sorter, until the Federal
court is created, to embezzle as much as he
pleased. Yet N.S.W. Supreme Court is now
seriously considering the matter on its legal merits.
AN OBVIOUS UNTRUTH.
VOICES FROM THE WATER : “ Come in, Sonnie,
it’s fine and Warm.”
SONNIE (from Drought-land): “’Taint, or you
girls wouldn’t have them clothes on.”
How can members of N.S. VY. Leg. Assem-
bly, who are really responsible for the govern-
ment of Norfolk Island, spout night after
night about the rights of free Australian citizens,
while by their silence condoning the form
of authority existing on the island ? The magis-
trate possesses and exercises the power to try all
civil cases without a jury, and a fundamental part
of the unfortunate Constitution of the place is
that there can be no appeal from his decision !
• * % *
“ Monowai ” : Re Sydney harbor adver-
tisements. It is amusing to find the Herald and
Telegraph posing as virtuous defenders of the
aesthetic, when they are themselves the greatest
offenders. Between them they have made Syd-
ney Agricultural Ground the biggest eyesore in
Australia, and their hideous posters defile many a
beautiful country road. Comtnonsense is wanted
in the matter. To abolish advertisements
on railway-stations is doubtful policy. But
all harbor foreshores must be absolutely
clear of them, all country roads, and places
of any pretension to natural beauty.
What it needed is an Advertisement Board (say a
member of each art society—three strong bruisers
to keep the peace between them), with power to
prohibit all advertisements (1) in unsuitable places
(2) that do not reach a certain artistic level.
* » * *
Labor member Roberts states that “ a foul
moral murder ” was committed in connection with
the loss of the barge Gwydir in Spencer's Gulf
and the drowning of three men, and he threatens
to stonewall all Government measures in S.A.
Assembly until an independent enquiry has been
held. The Marine Board blamed the captain of
the tug-boat, but the Supreme Court exonerated
him, the Judge advising the Board to fly for
higher game.
* * * *
Latest Sydney jokeling is to tell a friend
that a telephone message has come while he was
out. Will he ring up 39 Paddington and ask for
Miss Jessie ? 39 turns out to be the Zoo, and the
only Jessie on the premises is the elephant. The
inanity spread to such an extent that the Zoo
people were almost driven out of their senses by
repeated requests for “ Miss Jessie,” and had to
arrange with Paddington Exchange not to put
anyone on to 39 without an understanding.
Whereupon irascible persons, whose best girl was
named “ Jessie,” or who were confident of having
charmed some Jessie or other by their manly
beauty and deportment, loudly demanded the
“ manager,” and in one instance a city doctor who
was asked by three different persons to ring up
“ 39 ” made a scene.
* * * *
The young English semi-millionaire aris-
tocrat Cecil, still a- minor, who, it is cabled,
has been guilty of contempt of court by
marrying, in defiance of his mother’s wishes, Miss
Jessie Bain, daughter of a Scottish sharebroker,
has made a good start in life. No man ever went
far wrong who married a Scotchwoman named
Jessie.

“Liverpudlian”: Extract from S. M.
Herald interview with Mr. Lever (Lever Bros.,
soap-makers, Port Sunlight, Liverpool):—
If Parliament attempts to legislate on the rate of wages
to be paid, it can only be disastrous for every working-
man other than the limited number Who are just in their
prime of life.
Parliament doesn’t interfere with the rate of
wages in England, and at Port Sunlight the
Lever firm won’t take on a man who is over
40, not even for a week’s casual employment.
SONNET.
Earth is so fair, and life (all said) so sweet
That the mere joy of living’s infinite—
Our very tears are pleasures, viewed aright;
Our direst pains but pause on wingfed feet,
And so away. Each hour comes on replete
With opportunity of new delight.
Bright day draws gently on to quiet night,
And sleep woos forth the sting of each defeat.
Oh, I have laughed in flow’ry Spring, and known
The crowning charm of deep autumnal days,
The glad companionship of sea and sky !
Dearest! one hope remains now—one alone :
Bid me to live, e’er all my life decays,
Or in your pitiless mercy bid me die !
TAS.
FRANK MORTON.


A Great Literary Undertaking'.
The “Library of Famous Literature,” which
had such a large sale in England last year through
the London Standard, is now available to
residents of New South Wales. The work is in
20 volumes, and has been recently brought out
under the editorial direction of Dr. Richard
Garnett, C. 8., late Keeper of Printed Books in
the British Museum; with him have been
associated prominent men of learning representing
Germany, France and America. The work con-
tains selections from the world’s great writers,
ancient, mediaeval, and modern, with biographical
and explanatory notes and critical essays by
many eminent writers, and ranks as a very
encyclopaedia of literature. Our parents thought
much of the volumes in which Charles Knight
gave "his “Half-hours with the Best Authors ; ”
but his latest development of that work, ad-
mirable as that was, is as important by com-
parison as, say, the “Dictionary of Natural
Biography ” is to the tentative biographical
dictionaries which preceeded it. The generous
manner in which the idea of the “Library of
Famous Literature ” has been received by authors
may best be indicated by the fact that no fewer
than 300 copyright works are “sampled” in its
pages—for the editor’s aim has been to represent
the most recent as well as the most ancient in
literature. The great anthology will prove a
useful supplement to many private libraries. A
number of sets were reserved in London for
distribution in Australia, and these are now
available. The supply is very limited, and only
those who apply promptly will be able to take
advantage of the remarkably low prices and easy
terms that now prevail. The work is handsomely
illustrated witli colored and other engravings,
and should appeal to all who love good books.*

* * * •
The Sydney woman imprisoned for a time-
payment debt, the other day, will have conferred
a benefit on society if her case prompts some
M.L.A. to call for a return of the number
of persons now in Darlingburst Gaol confined
for debts—maintenance, alimony, costs and other
accounts. In some cases the amounts are trivial,
and the State loses more by the prisoner’s keep
than if it paid the amount owing.

DEFICIT.
“Say, uncle, how
much does it if
take to keep a wife?"
“ Oh, you can’t specify any particular sum—it

What seems to be the Real Tired Girl of
Australia was presented recently in Molong
(N.S.W.) police-court. She was a servant girl
named Mary Ann, and had left several places
under the same exhausted circumstances. On
entering a billet she toiled arduously at first, but
then sagged into a sleepy state and dozed off and
on for days—apparently till her engagement ter-
minated. ' In her last billet she felt the tired feel-
ing coming on so badly that she implored
her mistress to send for the police and
give her into custody for being the tiredest
girl in these parts. She was accordingly
charged with being too drowsy to adequately
maintain herself. There was a difficu'ty about
knowing what to do with the Genuine Australian
Tired Girl, but the Salvation Army people at
Orange offered to take temporary charge of her.
The Bulletin has an idea that there is a consider-
able awakening in front of the Tired Girl. For
getting an enormous amount of work gratis, and a
subscription as well, out of a tired sinner, and
keeping her awake by shouts of “Hallelujah,”
and by passing the hat in the middle of the night,
the Army has no equal.
* * * *
In a community which checks the baby-
supply. on the plea that the little germ of a sinner
is a nuisance in its early stages, it is interesting to
read, set forth solemnly in a Sydney daily paper,
instructions as to the treatment of pet dogs : —-
“ The least possible trace of vaseline may be applied to
their jackets, and polished off with a clean handkerchief.
. . . Brushing and hand-rubbing get up a beautiful gloss
on their coats. . . . Eyes should Vie w ished, and if noses
are dry a little vaseline well rubbed in with the finger.
Long-coated dogs need more attention. They must have
extra combing and brushing. The soap used should be
the best toilet, as common soaps are unsuitable. As to
Maltese spaniels, the hair so carefully kept down the
centre of the back must be sponged downwards from the
parting, while hot towels and warmed soft brushes
should be used for drying. . . . Great pains should be
taken to dry the inside of the ear thoroughly. . . . The
shaving of poodles is, of course, an ever-recurring task,”
etc., etc.
On the score of trouble, it would appear that the
woman who keeps a pet dog should be only too
glad to swap it for twins.

One provision of Peacock’s new old-age-
pension scheme is to empower magistrates to hand
the money over to parsons or J’s.P. on behalf of
the pensioners “as a guard against the wasteful
expenditure of the money.” Which calls for

pensioner’s compulsory contribution to plate or
mission-box. Also another sub-clause to make it
reasonably certain that a certain class of J.P.
won’t be entrusted with any pensioner’s money.

A W.A. chemist has been
fined for treating with a lotion
the eyes of a young customer,
on the ground that this was
surgical treatment. “If defen-
dant had dropped water into
the eyes, that would be a sur-
gical act,” said one doctor, in
the witness-box. Protecting the
public against imposition is one
thing, buttressing up a medical
monopoly another thing. The
• campaign against quackery seems to
be degenerating into the other thing.
■» * * *
“ M. 8.” writes re Sydney medical Mayor
Graham’s threat to enforce the law against mem-
bers of his own profession who fail to report
infectious cases to the Board of Health :
Has it ever occurred to the Mayor to prosecute the
Health Board for neglect by its own officers in these
cases ? The occupier of the infected premises goes to the
trouble (per Act) of making a statement as to what school
his sick child goes to ; whence he gets his milk-supply,
and the doctor fills this in, and mentions any other likely
source of infection in the neighborhood. But the subur-
ban health officials are usually council-clerks or corpora-
tion-laborers, who, upon receiving a report of an infec-
tious case from the local medico, are. supposed to rush off
to the infected premises with a bucketful of carbolic
wherewith to sprinkle the first drain that takes their
fancy. That’s about all they do, and it’s called “ disin-
fection.” Indeed, it doesn’t always get that length. I
know of four cases of scarlet fever, in separate houses,
reported some months ago, yet up to date no local health
official has visited the premises—not even the ir.an with
the squirt. I also know of many cases wherein, though
in accordance with the Act, the children have to stay
away from school because one in the family has scarlet
fever, the father of the family fondles his sick little one,
and yet goes to work as usual to mix with fellow-
employees ! Present scribe, in fact, utterly fails to see
what practical good has resulted from the new Health
Act beyond notification for the purposes of statistics.

The first step towards putting into effect
the N.S.W. Inebriates Act was made last week,
when a visiting committee for licensed inebriates’
homes was appointed. So far, however, no place
has been gazetted as a home, and the victim of
the drink-mania has still to gibber in the streets
and police-cells, unless he is fortunate enough to
have friends with “sand’’enough to put him for
a‘spell into a lunatic-asylum.
* * * *
The protest of the music warehouses against
a 15 per cent, tariff on trombones and trumpets,
and other brazen instruments, will hardly meet
with public sympathy, despite the plea that a
baud (when it gets away from a theatre) develops
a rich vein of human sympathy, and is always
blowing the debt off a hospital, or the mortgage
off a School of Arts. A little less brass music in
the streets would be a welcome result of the tariff.

Part of the price Queensland pays for its
Kanaka is a high murder-rate. Of seven homicides
recently executed in the State, six were colored
men.
* * • ♦
A curious case in a Sydney suburban hos-
pital. A girl of ten (disease, tubercular menin-
gitis) relapsed into unconsciousness last May, and
only recovered her senses the first. week in
October. She was kept alive by artificial feeding
for over four months. The 1 doctors hope for re-
covery now, but, as in all tubercular diseases, a
recurrence is always possible.
A growl from “ Civil Servant ” :
Re N.S.W. Lands Minister Crick’s recent remark that
the Premier would sack him if he struck for an 8-hour
day. The “ House ” laughed at the joke, and the House
patronised the 8-hour day demonstration ; yet I, a
Government employee, in a responsible position, have to
work from 9 a.m. to 10 p.m., minus an hour each for
lunch and tea. When these hours were first required of
me I was informed that they were necessitated by a
temporary pressure of work, blit that was 15 months ago !
Some months ago I received £2O odd extra, with an in-
timation that no more overtime would be paid. This
payment was nominally 2.) months’ overtime, and was
considered fair compensation for nine months’ extra
work of three hours a day, all Saturday's and holidays,
and many a Sunday. Certainly Is. 6d. tea-money is
allowed, but 4Jd. an hour for four hours’ work is farcical.
The work shows no signs of diminishing ; yet there is talk
of reduction of staff, but none of reduction of the £6OO to
£2OOO salaries enjoyed by a dozen favored ones in the
Department. Casual labor at Bs. per day commands Is.
6d. an hour for overtime, yet skilled labor and responsi-
bility must meekly accept Is. 6d. for four and often five
hours of overtime.
• * * *
The 900 odd appeals arising from the
re-grading of N.S.W. Public Service are about to
be heard by the Board. The appellants have
been granted the right of being represented
by counsel, and are deeply exercised as to
whether to take advantage of the permission.
They find there is much to be said on both sides.
To employ a lawyer may look like a confession of
distrust in the Board, or of personal incapacity to
argue the matter, or of consciousness of a weak
case; on the other hand a lawyer man may
gracefully and unblushingly put the merits of
Brown to the Board in a fashion which the
average Brown himself would not have the face to
adopt.
* * it *
Sydney tailoresses have at last succeeded
in enforcing their demand for better terms from
the manufacturing clothiers of the city. It means
an advance of from 324 to 100 per cent, on the
cruelly sweating rates in force in August last, the
date when the women began to organise. Since
the Federal tariff will keep out the foreign cheap-
labor product, there should be no difficulty in
maintaining the rates.

As a variant on the small-girl outrage lie a
Brisbane youth, aged six, came home with a
solemn yarn of how he and Billy Blank were
playing near a water-hole in Victoria Park, and
Billy fell in. Two constables vainly dived in

Bill’s mate has a future as a war-correspondent

before the Victorian select committee on railway
management that, when he visited Europe, he ob-

in the precautions they took to ensure the safety
of passengers by scientific examination of the
sight and hearing of employees. Yet, his bitterest
foe could hardly accuse tlie medico of an undue
political leaning towards State socialism.

Same old fair-sex argument: Who scores
the greater nowadays—the pretty or the attractive
woman? Meanwhile, the sensible woman “gets
there” by visiting the Toilet Salon of Mr. H.
Westall Guest, Bourke-street—thereby proving
that real charm is in a good complexion, luxuriant
hair and well-modelled features. With a little
expert assistance the attractive woman preserves
her charm, and the pretty woman becomes attrac-
tive.*
’Tis her alluring style, at present, to so
gown herself as to display as much of her shapeli-
ness as possible. Given a fine day for the Cup it
will be found that Woman, arrayed in soft,
clinging dress creations, with her undulating
form perfected by the P.D. Corset, will dazzle the
male person with the frankness of her contour.
The P.D. at once transforms any reasonably
shapely wearer into something like a goddess.
The P.D. figure on Cup-day will be the prettiest
part of the show.*
Cablegram from London
A big struggle has commenced for the control of the
tobacco trade. All the wholesale tobacco merchants in
London reduced their prices to-day, in order to fight the
new American combination.
It is added that the Americans are willing to drop
a million pounds in cutting prices in order to cap-
ture the English tobacco trade. The invasion of
England by American capital is growing to very
large dimensions these days, and Britain will take
to Protection again before long in sheer self-
defence.
CHARLES
