A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, October 29, 1901,
Mr Dear Arini,—
Everyone who has more than a quarter-inoh of
forehead must sometimes meditate curiously upon
the eagerness with which we (we are Australia
facing the dawn) seize upon trifles and let our-
selves be tickled with straws. A few days ago
the cable told us that the wife of President
Roosevelt had announced that she can dress on
£6O per year. The announcement is probably one
of Reuter’s little foxes—“little foxes that gnaw
the vines ” —or probably it is a half-truth. Mrs.
Roosevelt might have said that, if necessary—say,
if hubby had only a salary of £l2O a year—she could
manage to dress on half of it, or, peradventure, she
mentioned to a dress chronicler that she managed
on £6OO per year for dress, and a nought fell out
of the reporter’s note-book. But all over Australia
newspapers have fairly flung themselves upon the
straw (or fox) and are commenting upon it from a
dozen points of view, all in perfect good faith.
Ponderous editors of mill-stone dailies give a
snuffle of authority and order a leaderette in which
oocur the words “management”—“economy”—
“good example.” Their wives read it disgustedly
and say, “Why, she’d give £6O for just one very
middling evening dress ! ”
> Roosevelt, since he became President, doesn’t
seem to have done anything worth mentioning,
except ask a negro to dinner. I came across men-
tion of this negro, Booker Washington by name,
some months ago, in an article on the color pro-
blem in the States. Booker Washington is an
ex-slave who educated himself and founded a
Normal School for young negroes at Tuskeegee
(doesn’t that word call up a picture of Indians
chasing bison ?). “A prominent Southerner,” so
goes the mention, “ was asked how he had
addressed the principal and faculty of Tuskeegee
when he met him on the street. He was most
enthusiastic in describing what Washington had
done, and to what good use his scholars put their
knowledge. He contrasted them favorably with
white boys, and yet this man said : ‘ Now, when
I meet the man who has done all this I can’t call
him “ Booker ” like I would an ordinary nigger,
but, by thunder, I can’t call a nigger “ Mr.,” so I
just say “ Professor.” ’ ”
Society of Artists’ Annual Exhibition opened
this week, oils, water-colors, pastels, black and
white, and sculpture—2l2 exhibits in all. As you
enter the main room, Longstaff’s portrait of
Marjorie—Mr. J. C. Williamson’s little daughter
—confronts you, and you think, “ Isn’t Fogtown
lucky to get Longstaff ! ” The child is seated in
a straight-backed chair, her little hands lie
easily on her lap, the head is inclined
towards the right ; the soft, rose-like
face and golden hair are framed below a big
stand-up, pink bonnet. The draperies are of old,
rose-pink silk, and a wisp of white lace crosses the
bare arms. This picture is flanked by the dusky-
green “Fairy Tales” of Mrs. Peters on one side
and a brilliant canvas, “ The Grate,” —deep blue
sea, a man and a woman, she in a crimson robe—
on the other. Then in order come Sid. Long’s
floor-to-ceiling canvas, a portrait of Mayor
Graham in his official robes. Then Julian
Ashton’s laterally-long, principal exhibit, “The
Wave,’ a greenish-blue sea-scape with warm
boulders in the foreground. The “ wave ” dashes
upon a sunken rock, and its long, level, incurving
line is broken laterally, and seethes in a great burst
of white foam and spray. The Gallery will be
open to the public to-day (Thursday). Mrs.
Barton, Mrs. Wise, Mayoress Graham, Mrs. J. 0.
Williamson, and other ladies will give “At
Homes” during the course of the show. The
committee intends to take a plebiscite as to .the
best five pictures.
Mrs. Ernest Coomb'S, who was the Rosalind in
the under-the-Norfolk-pines production of “As
You Like It,” is the wife of a Sydney bank-
manager. Physically, she is an ideal Rosalind,
and might well cry, “ Alack, for my doublet
and hose ; why can’t I wear ’em every day ? ”
“ Mona,” where the play was produced, is in-
habited by Mrs. Rowe, the widow of Major Rowe.
Once upon a time it held a fine collection of pic-
tures—at least they were considered fine in those
days. The grounds glimmered with thousands of
fairy lights. Acetylene gas lighted the stage,
which was just mother earth, and scores of moths
and a scared bird or two blundered above the
players’ heads. The poor support given by the
public to the production is not likely to make
Messrs. Love and Broun give us more Shakes-
peare under the moon. By-the-way, Mr. Broun,
the assistant manager of the Palace Theatre,
marries Miss Cruickshank, daughter of a
deceased naval surgeon, presently.
On Friday night, in a parting storm of cheers
and flowers, the Italian singers took leave of us—
for a little while. “La Boheme” and the mad
scene from “ Lucia ” were given to a record audi-
ence. Signor Dani had a score of button-holes
thrown to him, and something on cardboard that
gleamed as gold and flashed as a diamond was
handed over the footlights to the high C man.
Another of the male principals got a bright,
stuffed bird, balanced on a twig within a hoop.
Madame Sapio carried off several baskets of
flowers, including an armful of arum lilies.
Signor Hazon, the ever-youthful Bracy, and
proprietor Williamson, were drawn on to the
stage by the voices from on high, and the last
spoke a short, phlegmatic speech, giving credit to
anyone and everyone but himself. It is difficult
to make Father Williamson enthuse. He is deeper
than a patty-pan, and his eloquence is not of the
small-pot-soon-hot type. Last night of the opera
the audience was a combination of local society,
musicians and other-Staters eri route to the Mel-
bourne race carnival. There was nothing
startling in the way of dressing. A middle-
aged lady with an eagle nose wore a
wreath of pink baby roses. She gave the im-
pression of strong individuality, because she
clearly didn’t care what people thought. Pink
roses are muoh misused just now. To-day, in the
early forenoon, a thin, sallow girl in a holland
frock was about town wearing a wreath of full-
blown ones round her neck.
Miss Lillah M‘Carthy, as Zebuda, in the
“ Christian King,” doesn’t look the Norse maiden
that we’d conjured up from our childhood’s
memory of Viking history. Her long black hair,
black brows, carven features, her snaky grace,
and the way she stands, “like a lance at rest,” her
robes and turban, all make for a reminder of
women in Jewish and Roman history. Deborah
or Judith might have looked so and acted
thus. She’s a lovely pagan, and good old
Taffy, if he ever had to choose between a
Zebuda and an Elswitha, must have had
a terrible struggle with himself. “ Moral
qualities rule the world, but at short
distance the senses are despotic.” With a
loud, deep sigh we are forced to endorse that.
But Elswitha has her own charm. Miss Edyth
Latimer acts her very prettily as an Opheliaish
girl: geutle, obedient, who still wears her hair
down, and isn’t any more grown-up after she has
got a baby in a wooden cradle. Miss Latimer,
who was a little girl from Manly, has got on capi-
tally in her profession since W.B. took her hence
(being “ fetched ” by her reciting) three years ago.
There isn’t much millinery in the “Christian
King.” When the Danes are lving down you
think of a herd of steers at rest. I 1 hey must have
done a lot of dehorning of their own or neighbors’
cattle so that each fighting man might wear a
pair of horns above his ears.
Major “ Jack ” Antill ought to be much obliged
to the Boers—or Providence. He might have been
scattered by pom-poms on the veldt, but he
wasn’t. He brought his personable, middle-aged
self (after Pole-Carew’s example no soldier above
the rank of captain will marry before middle age)
back from the Karroo (whatever that may be) and
got married to a tall, fair-haired, dimpled girl
with a complexion and a smile. Instead of a
Boer bullet he got a billet as a bridegroom ! She
is the eldest daughter of the Wills-Allens, of
Gunnible station. Her gown was of Brussels lace,
chiffon and satin; and she wore diamond screw
studs in her unpierced ear-lobes. The reception was
held at the Australia Hotel. Bridesmaids carried
bouquets of crimson poppies with streamers
and bows of red chiffon, and by the time the cake
was cut they looked as if they had been boiled.
The poppy off its stalk declines to live
more than 20 minutes. Major-General and
Mrs. French, the latter faithful to her violet head-
gear, were present. Also, Mrs. Lassetter with
two green and crimson parrokeets transfixed
under the brim of her toque, and Mrs. Burdekin
in heliotrope, and a crowd of majors and captains
with their wives.
Another marriage: Miss C. R. Dibbs, one of
Sir George’s flock of daughters, to Captain
Waldron, of the Imperial Bushmen, on Saturday
last. Mr. W. Hickson was best man. The bride’s
gown was white duchess satin, trimmed with
Brussels lace, and the four bridesmaids wore white
Indian muslin and big white hats, and carried
white bouquets.
Marrying a scientist carries certain penalties—
for the bride. A nice girl wedded a local
fashionable doctor a few weeks ago. On the
honeymoon she learned that she must not wear
open-work stockings, for fear of microbes ; or low
dresses, for fear of bronchitis ; or kiss anyone, for
fear of contagion ; must not shake hands with un-
gloved hands for the same reason ; or speak into a
public telephone for fear of tubercle ; musn’t hire
a pair of opera-glasses when she leaves her own
at home. It was all “ Thou shalt not.” When
she came home she found her bed with its four legs
ankle-deep in jars of disinfectant. Many hus-
bands who are not scientists or medicine-men
have tried to induce their wives to curtail the tails
of their walking gowns, but got no more satis-
faction than “you don’t know what you’re talking
about!” It’s different, though, when the wife is
a bride— that clay is mostly soft to the hands of
the potter.
From Paris comes a new form of fortune-telling
“by the whole figure.” It sounds tempting, and
the new profession should be rushed by oleaginous
men with dark curls and ox-eyes. Further, the
form-reader will stand on velvet as a prophet.
He will be able to foretell that the blushing
damsel with plump arms, &c., is sure of, at least,
one husband, and that the venerable spinster with
the obtrusive shoulder-blades will find that the
gentleman whom she advertised for in Gkanny
has strictly honorable intentions—in fact, irri-
tatingly so.
Final speech of an Australian war-correspondent
after his last lecture to a London audience:
“Ladies and gentlemen,— I have travelled 49,000
miles and addressed 200,000 people—according to
my manager- ana ..ave had a real good time.
But next week it will be my privilege to sit where
you are, and say as I walk out, ‘ What a rotten
show ! 5 ”
We want a minimum-wage for teachers. But
probably it would be fixed at eighteenpenceaday.
If a board, of the class which pays its governesses
£2O a year and its cooks £6O, “had the fixing, it
wouldn’t go much beyond that figure. A junior
resident master recently went up North to a
private school. He had to teach mathematics,
Latin, French, and all English subjects at a
salary of £4O. “Board and residence in term
and during short vacations.” A highly-repu-
table Sydney agent engaged him for the billet,
and here’s an extract from that agent’s letter
evidently in reply to one of remonstrance on the
J.M.’s part : —“ A resident master (unless special
exceptional arrangements are made) has to under-
take house-duty, including Saturdays and Sun-
days. If you have two evenings to yourself you
have as much as resident masters usually have,
and those two could surely he utilised for outside
teaching” Italics mine. This young man with
his “ Latin, French, mathematics, and all English
subjects,” ought to be worth, say, the wage just
awarded as minimum to the fettlers of W.A.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent: —
“The deluge in Melbourne on Sunday week
played havoc with the new-season goods of the
blithe spring girl. A beautiful sun lured the
crowd out, and then the flood-gates of heaven
opened, and, an hour or two later, forlorn wretches
of the feathered sex were streaming home through
the suburbs, looking like fit subjects for the appli-
cation of the rules for the restoration of the
insufficiently-drowned. I camped for two hours
under one verandah where several blighted girls
were openly and noisily weeping over ruined
headgear.' While we awaited the abatement of
the storm and the draining-off of streets, an open
drag, loaded with theatricals, two-thirds of them
ladies in what were recently most expensive
frocks and top-hamper, drove past through the
raging torrent, like a band of mixed bathers
fresh from the sea. Accepting the inevitable
with the best grace, the pros, drove through
the downpour, along the street-river, singing
hilariously, but at the deep crossing the drag
bumped heavily, and a small comedian who was
doing funny business on a back seat made the hit
of his life by going plunk into the billows. The
drag had to anchor while the small comedian was
fished' out of the storm waters with a crooked
stick, amidst howls of applause from the
verandahs and balconies.
“I never before in my life met such an agree-
able aggregation of nice old men with their nice
young wives as those composing George Reid’s
Freetrade meeting at Melb. Town Hall the other
Monday evening. Every second clean, prosperous,
middle-aged Freetrade importer in this city seems
to have a wife young enough to be his niece, and
they all assisted- the veterans to admire George
Reid, shaking tiny handkerchiefs at the stout
comedian, and even throwing flowers at his in-
growing toes. If G.H.R. had been a popular
pianist ‘ with a’ayrick’ead of’air,’or a beauty-
tenor, he could not have created a greater flutter.
I got in early by strategy, when all the exits and
the entrances were barred and bolted and tyled
and guarded by a regiment of police ; and found
the place packed tight with the elite, some in
evening dress. It was like a fashionable audience
at a five-shilling concert, and not a bit like the
average political-meeting crowd.
“ At Mr. Reid’s anti-tariff meeting (Oct. 21),
when he silenced an old-age interjector with a
jeer re ‘artificial teeth on the free list,’ sensitive
hearers felt as they do when some burly larrikin
hawker savagely threatens to kick his tottering
old moke off his feet. However, if man in his wis-
dom scarifies these remarks as henfoolishness, on
the grounds that ‘all is fair in politics,’ I meekly
withdraw my yapping—till woman gets her vote.
“The young widow of the late Mr. J. A. Wal-
lace, M.L.C., is a niece of Sydney’s George Reid,
and a daughter of Mr. Hugh Reid, of Melbourne.
Mrs. Wallace, gentle and nice-looking, is an
active kirk-worker. Her step-daughter (young,
pretty Miss Wallace), like her brothers, will, pre-
sumbly, now enjoy fuller control over the wealth
inherited from her own mother, the first Mrs.
Wallace (nee Monahan). One of the Wallace
boys, by the way, married the nurse who tended
him through his illness.
“President Wrixon, of the State Parliament,
is able to do more entertaining than his shrimp-
like predecessor, for he has a wife who takes
kindly to the business, and a family of rather
festive tendencies. Mrs. Wrixon, with her
saintly brow and general Puritanic appearance, is
not a giddy elf, but she is the right kind of
mamma, and takes her turn at the helm with a
rigid sense of duty. The Wrixon boys are chips
of the"maternal block, and turn up unfailingly to
every social shivoo in Melbourne. At the semi-
official garden-partv given by the President and
his partner, at ‘ Raheen,’ Kew, last Thursday,
several Caulfield Cup stunners were aired in the
way of frocks, the hostess herself showing forth
in a cloudy blue voile built on silk and frescoed
with black cob-web lace.
“There was a bright military hop at the
Prahran Town Hall, on Thursday night, when
the officers of the Victorian Garrison Artillery
hosted joyously. The dais party would have been
entirely obscured from sight but for the diamond-
sparkling of the evergreen and omnipotent dowa-
ger of Cliveden. Outside this radius, Mrs. Jus-
tice Hood scintillated quite on her own, her gown
being daffodil faille, with finest point lace, and
mky ehoux. Her daughter, a small brunette, was
worth a second look, in sheeny silk and em-
broidered chiffon. But the most remarkably -
froc-ked woman was Mrs. Walter Clarke, youthful
and grey-haired, in a rare rig-out of black-and-
silver spangled net, with mauve encrustations,
and additions of blue furbishings. A head-dress
of blue, black, and white reared above the grey
structure.
, . Gnce more Sydney was in it—a tiny matron
hailing from t’other side obtaining extraordinary
comment at this scarlet hop. She was a weird
ethereal figure in an angel-like gauzy chiffon.’
Heaven only knows how it was glued on:
hooks and eyes being too, too coarse to do
the important part of hitching. A tangle of hair,
the shade of barley-sugar, threw a pale, golden
shade on the nape of the neck, and a pale grey
dove was glued down on the left temple.
A complexion of pale pink and cream was
the most uncommon thing in flesh-tints ever seen.
There are not too many fascinating sirens
working their incantations on the hearts and
pockets of male visitors at the World’s Fair, and
the majority of these seem to contrive an early
disappearance to the adjacent aquarium, where
couples wander into cunning corners and forget the
W orld entirely. The most of the shearing is per-
rormed by precocious juveniles, or elderly spin-
sters raffling the eternal cosy. All the same, the
crowd files into the exhibition every evening and
swells the stall takings. Cynics say that the tug-
of-war, amusing hundreds in the grounds, is no-
thing to the tug of coat-tails in the building.

“Ormond Professor Petersen, successor to
black-browed Marshall Hall, is slim and fair,
with pointed blonde beard, elfish eyes, and quaint,
inflected Scotch voioe. People speak of his odd
‘accent,’ but ‘intonation’ is the correct word.
He is very like a Danish or Swedish Varsity man,
who (judging by specimens met in Australia) are
amiable, accomplished conversationalists, with
fair beards, great fondness for music (light as
well as heavy), and a passion for discussing Con-
tinental operatic performances. In spite of
German education and a Hungarian wife, Peterson
is a canny Scot who will do good business for his
Conservatorium. Minus the musical originality
and magnetism of hymner Hall, the harmonious
pilgrim advertises his firm, so to speak, and
enlists students all over the State by
acting as judge at musical competi-
tions here, there, and everywhere. But his
second Wagner lecture (Oct. 22nd), with its mild
‘musical illustrations’ and small-sized limelight
views seemed rather milk-ancL-watery. Even
bland ‘Waxellency’ stinted his oratorical honey
when called upon to return thanks for such very
small mercies. One hears that blarneying John
wearies of being ‘nice,’ complaining that in
return man gets but ingratitude—and his salary,
of course.
“Toorak has a tasty morsel to digest. The
announcement of a marriage which took place
months and months ago only appeared last week,
and the secretly-joined pair have thrown much
dust in the eyes of their fashionable friends by
keeping up the pretence of a playful engagement.
Some laugh over the affair, thinking it clever—a
perfect comedy. Others frown, and say the whole
business, the secrecy and deceit, is awful. One of
the most jaundiced of the circle paid half-a-crown
at the Registrar’s office to see the real date of the
knot-tying. It was half-a-crown thrown away.
The public announcement was quite correct.
“ Mrs. Cox (formerly Miss Stanbridge), Dayles-
ford’s very own heiress and daughter of the soil,
plans dairying operations at her childhood’s
home, builds luxurious milking-sheds, and puts
her faith in butter. Socially, however, she wastes
no light whatever on the gloom of her native dis-
trict.
“The train from Melbourne to one ot the out-
suburbs, the other evening, brought a daintily-
apparelled lady, in a fancy dress, who sought the
station-master in a shower of tears, because the
train went no further, and she wanted to get to
the next town to a fancy-dress ball. The sympa-
thetic official got a cab for the distressed one,
when there stepped up a furious man, in pointed
moustachios and goatee, and a vindictive smile,
with ‘ Hold ! madam ! I command you to return
home ! ’ The lady shrieked, and urged the driver
to start at once, when the threatening person tore
off his disguising horsehair, and lo ! it was her
husband ! Then there were tears and recrimi-
nations, from which the bystanders gathered
that the husband had shadowed his pleasure-
loving wife all the way from Melbourne. After a
while the lady became excited and threatened to
deposit herself under the next train, leaving her
children to the mercy of the world. Then
hubby also lost his temper, and rebuked the
giddy matron by slaps ; and the local bobby, who
wasn’t used to the ways of Melbourne sassiety,
locked up the pair in separate cells, where they
passed the night without even the gratification of
abusing one another. .
“ That mad-house doctor in the Hawtrey pro-
duction is true to life, with his owl-like pretence
of medical acumen and spotting of non-existent
symptoms. I know of a deaf damsel taken to a
city aurist, t’other day, by her anxious grand-
mother. Doctor marched into his consulting
room, made straight for Granny, and shouted :
‘You’re very deaf!’ ‘How do you know?
questioned the maligned ancient. ‘By the ex-
pression of your eyes and your whole attitude!’
By this, the pompous medicine man perhaps
meant that his supposed patient had a vacant,
listless demeanor, but she soon let him know that
ears and tongue were still going strong.
“A nurse was recently attending a foreign
matron at a critical hour. The patient was so ill
that the nurse, gulping down her professional
tears, broke the news gently to the husband. He
threw his hands and his eyes up to Heaven : ‘ Ah !
you must get the deer vife bettor, for she is my
second pardner, and if she die I vill have to get
anoder —dat vill be tree,’ he exclaimed with a deep
sigh. The shocked nurse rushed from him and
worked like a Trojan to get that patient back from
the jaws of death.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.


Warner’s Safe Cure.
FEDERAL TARIFF PRICES.
The proprietors of Warner’s Safe Cure beg to
announce that, as they have a branch laboratory
in Australia, Warner’s Safe Remedies will not be
subject to the duty of 25 per cent., which will
have to be paid on all proprietary medicines
imported into Australia.
The wholesale prices of Warner’s Safe Remedies
have been slightly advanced in New South Wales
so as to make prices uniform throughout Australia,
but this advance is so small that it will not be
necessary for the public to pay more than two
pence per bottle over and above the prices paid
before the Federal tariff came into force.
Warner’s Safe Cure is the only lemely known
to science which will unfailingly cure all diseases
of the kidneys and liver. The following diseases
are cured by Warner’s Safe Cure, because they all
all arise from the retention in the system of
urinary and biliary poisons which it is the province
of the kidneys and liver to expel: Rheumatism,
indigestion, gout, neuralgia, biliousness, lumbago,
siek-headache, backache, sciatica, gravel, jaundice,
bladder troubles, blood disorders, anaemia, Bright’s
disease.*
There is one place in Sydney where the
business of money-lending is carried on in an
equitable manner and where those in need of
financial accommodation can rely on being
decently treated. The sensible man who wishes
to get rid of his monetary worries at a minimum
of cost and trouble to himself goes to the offices of
the Australian Equitable Investment Co., Ltd.
The co. has a large capital for investment at low
interest, and is always open to advance money on
risks which, the banks would decline; and acts
generally as a medium between the banks and
ordinary money-lenders. Men in business can
obtain assistance for their venture at a figure
which will leave them a fair profit for themselves.
The co. advances money to any extent on deeds of
land, bond warrants, merchandise, stock in trade,
scrip, interests under wills, furniture and all classes
of security. Press notices which have been pub-
lished from time to time express the favorable
opinion which generally exists regarding the co.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Lord Ketchener.— Bulletin copyright and
guaranteed to stick.
“Jumbo” Armstrong has taken “silk.”
A godsend to the N.S.W. junior bar !
Said that Max Hirsch was responsible for
the ground-work of G. H. Reid’s tariff speech.
The father of N.S.W. Justice Cohen was a
proprietor of the ably-conducted Sydney Aus-
tralian, whose first editors were W. C. Went-
worth and Dr. Wardell.
Candid Mr. Crick, debating “ the honor of
the House” : “ Why, the teetotallers in the House
are increasing. If it wasn’t for me the refresh-
ment-room would go bung.”
A private letter from London says that
“ Bertrand, the murderer, who spent 30 years in
Darlinghurst prison, is doing splendidly in Lon-
don, in the mining business. ”
Clement Wragge, recognising that humor
alone won’t run a show, now intersperses his
lectures on meteorology and the Stiger-Vortex gun
with sea-songs, accompanying himself on the
banjo.
A western N.S W. settler celebrated his
recent elevation to the “ Bench ” by having a
large gold scarf-pin made, bearing, in raised letters,
the legend, “M. Hooligan, J.P.”
Eden Jawge, who has suffered much from
the unkindness of newspapers in his climb to
fame, wishes N.S.W. Assembly to pass a Bill
giving itself power to deal with critical editors by
fine or imprisonment.
Ranfurley, unlike some of our Australian
Governors, desiring to prolong his term, has got
M.L. Government to request the Imperial
authorities to assent to the prolongation. That is
the old constitutional practice.
Edward Greville, who takes leave of ab-
sence from N.S.W. Leg. Council, has been for
years the Chesterfield of that slow-going body.
Ere health gave way, he was a frequent speaker,
concise, scholarly, and dignified. Till the last his
suavity of manner was conspicuous.
J. H. Carruthers is gradually getting a
party about him in N.S.W. Assembly. He is
biding his time to quietly elbow Lee out of the
way. One of his principles is to be particularly
kind to young members.
Jenkin Coles, the proud and titled S.A.
Speaker, sprained his ankle lately when being
tossed about in Spencer’s Gulf awaiting the mail-
boat. This calamity is expected to greatly
facilitate the outer harbor movement.
There seems a fervent desire in some
quarters to hurry N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley off
the bench. The sturdy veteran has several times
had to deny an announcement that he was about
to take 12 months’ holiday and retire. Whom
does the Ministry desire to appoint ?
The recent George Reid testimonial in
Sydney only panned out some £250 or so. It was
intended to raise more, to recoup Yes-No’s losses
whilst attending the tariff debate in Melbourne.
A Foretaste of the Nation’s Gratitude and a
Warning re Laying to his Book—at the Bar.
Ex-Minister Arthur Hodgson, still alive at
83, and figuring as a public man in England, took
as a bride a Woolloomooloo girl, and was the first
to settle with a white woman on the Darling
Downs (Q.). He was a member of N.S.W.
Parliament and had a portfolio in two Queensland
Ministries.
The action of the British Government in
dismissing RecTvers Buller from his command of
the Ist Army Corps is a regrettable piece of
cruelty. What future is there now for that vast
army of loyal British infants, the Buller Smiths
and the Buller Joneses who were named after him
when he was in his zenith ?
Reported that Mr. F. Bridges, Chief In-
spector of N.S.W. Schools, and temporary member
of N.S.W. Public Service Board, is to be ap-
pointed Federal Public Service Commissioner.
Mr. T. W. Turner, of Fort-st. (Sydney) Public
School, will, it is also said, be one of the six
“ inspectors ” under the Act.
State Agent Grainger (S.A.) has got
himself into trouble for poking fun at the dignified
and decorous Lords of the Admiralty in an official
communication. So far, those high and mighty
officials have been too startled by the tone of his
letter to reply, and nervous legislators fear that
the result will be a loss of trade to S. A.
What a fiery, wild Radical M‘Millan
is becoming! Even Sydney “D.T.” can’t keep
pace with him, and mildly apologises for his state-
ment that there was “ a conspiracy ” to construct
the Federal tariff for Victorians. M‘Millan seems
desirous, now that he has his knighthood safe, to
tread the John Norton path of fame.
A Sydney suburban doctor, certifying that
John Norton was not able to attend at Parliament
House, to answer the charge of libelling the Legis-
lature by stating that some of its members had
their legs tangled in hop-vines one night, particu-
larised that John was suffering from “ nervous
prostration and injuries to eye and face.” An un-
kind House laughed loudly when this was read.
Mrs. Todhunter, who recently died on her
husband’s station, near Warren, N.S.W., was a
niece of John Skinner Prout, the artist, some of
whose pictures now hang in the N.S.W. National
Gallery. The artist arrived in Sydney in 1840 and
worked over a large part of Australia. On return-
ing to England he was elected to the Royal
Academy. His “Tasmania Illustrated” is in
high repute.
Judge Meymott, whose daughter married
last week at Burwood (N.S.W.), was a pupil of
the great Chitty’s, and practised for many years
in England. As District Court Judge in N.S.W.,
he put up a record of good legal work accompanied
by striking disregard for conventions. He spent
some time as Parliamentary draftsman, and re-
signed the post with a high reputation for accuracy
and logical sequence of thought.
Minister Kingston incurred the everlast-
ing wrath of Melb. pressmen by his secretiveness
on tariff night. When they pleaded with him to
make their lot lighter by supplying necessary
figures earlier in the evening C.C.K. sternly
refused, and as it was late before he finished
telling Parliament all about it, many a suffering
pressman invented a special imprecation for his
benefit whilst trudging home sometime a.m. to a
distant suburb.
Some official people try to soften the
asperity of Gov. Le Hunte’s shortage of cash in
New Guinea by assuring him that he will obtain
an early transfer to one of the Australian State
Governorships. But M‘Gregor was twice as
big a man, and worked twice as hard and many
years longer in Papua, and his transfer was to a
fever-trap on the Gold Coast. Which, considering
Dr. M‘Gregor’s splendid record, was a mean and
even brutal act.
Tomlinson, convicted of the White
Cliffs coach robbery, is tall and slim, with re-
markably sloping shoulders, and ought to be
easily recognised; yet the passengers, coach-
driver and bike-man spent more than an hour in
company with the robber on that moonlit night,
and will not identify one with the other. They
all knew Tomlinson well, three of them familiarly.
Local opinion is that Tomlinson is certainly not
the man who committed the actual robbery.
Suggested by Geelong (Vic.) Advertiser
that something should be done by the producers of
the State to mark their appreciation of the late
James Harrison’s invention of cheap ice-making,
which enabled Victoria to become a large exporter
of perishable produce. The inventor and great
journalist (it was James Harrison who made
Melb. Age a power in the land) died poor, leaving
a widow and family. As the inscription on his
tomb reads, “Onesoweth; another reapeth.”
The Duke of York, it is cabled, irritated
French Canadians by not farewelling them in
French. If the Duke’s French is as bad as his
English, they should rather thank him for saving
the life of a language. Canada is just now on the
verge of a race problem. Those of French race
are increasing so rapidly in numbers as to threaten
to swamp the British-descended in the land.
Already the French have taken possession of nine
hitherto English-represented seats in Parliament.
“ Baldy ” Davis lately told N.S. W. Assem-
bly that he had lost £150,000 in a western grazing
property, and, after 40 years’ work, had, from a
“bloated squatter,” come down to an “ordinary
M.P.” The Bulletin knows of a man who, after
putting nearly £lOO,OOO of his own money into a
Queensland squattage, left it after long years with
nothing but a suit of clothes and a billiard-cue,
and owing the bank £250,000. But he is a battler,
and has since made a solid half-million in mining.
The King, moved thereto, it is said, by
dread of an incurable malady, has lately taken to
“ Christian Science.” There is, no doubt, a scien-
tific basis in this creed, to the extent that will-
power, or faith, is able to conquer most forms of
pain and most diseases unmarked by organic
lesion. But in Mrs. Eddy’s Christian Science
teaching the truth is mixed up with, a huge mass of
rubbish, some of it merely silly, other silly and
dirty. For instance, the daily bath is, according
to Mrs. Eddy, no more necessary than to take a
fish out of water daily, and cover it with dirt, in
order that it may thrive better 1
PAPA BUNKER.
Mr. N. Lockyer, N.S.W. Collector of Cus-
toms, had a big share in the drawing-up of the
“ purely Victorian tariff ” which the Reidites rave
about. And his departmental chiefs, with a
thoughtfulness not always found among poli-
ticians, gave him credit for his work. Said George
Turner, in his budget speech, “ The gentleman
who mainly prepared the figures was Mr. Lockyer,
of N.S. Wales. He is an able man, and I have
been astounded at the interest he has shown and
the trouble he has taken in his work.” Later,
Mr. Kingston felt it necessary to “mention Mr.
Lockyer, whose high intelligence has been chiefly
devoted to the all-important question of the
necessary calculations.”

Freetrade, is the son of an English ship-owner
who dropped dead in
his office m the Forties,
on hearing of the loss
of a valuable vessel
and cargo on the
Australian coast.

SEEDY PERSON: “I say, guv’nor, do

Admiral Sir James Brisbane, the transfer
of whose remains from Devonshire-st. to South
Head cemetery has evoked congratulations from
the present Admiral, was not Sir Thomas Brisbane,
the early Governor of N.S.W. Sir James was
Commodore of the first battle-ship that entered
Port Jackson. He died in Sydney in 1826, and
was highly eulogised in a Government Order of
the day. “His name will be handed down as a
record to posterity,” said the Order. His identity
is already almost lost.
Lieut.-General, and periodic Agent-
General, and perpetual Victorian pensioner
Andrew Clarke, claims to be the first member
elected as a working man’s candidate in Australia.
South Melbourne sent him as a Radical into the
first Vic. Assembly. He had come out as A.D.C.
to Gov. Denison, and probably Lady Denison’s
habit of laughing at the pretensions of local
snobdom influenced him to take up the cause of
the working men as against “the other class.”
The working men certainly stuck to him while he
stuck to them.
It was certainly foolish of N.S.W. Parlia-
ment to become officially aware of John Norton’s
blow at its alleged dignity. Too late, the House
will find that it would have been much better to
have let the matter severely alone. The only
John, in his most flourishing days, was hauled up
every three months for hurting somebody’s,
“dignity,” but he came out in all cases without
any damage worth mentioning. Then the ag-
grieved took the cruel course of leaving John
alone ; and his star began to set.
Statements can always be explained away,
for no statement is so definite as to be incapable
of being misunderstood. It is a politician’s Dan
O’Connorisms—the glowing ' bits of rhetoric into
which he is betrayed after a good dinner—that
haunt him in future days. Thus, during the cen-
sure debate, Joe Cook quoted Barton speaking for
G. H. Reid in the old days:—
I believe in Mr. Reid because he is a man who will
ever strive to crown the brow of New South Wales with
the rich garlands of freetrade, and will never deface her
shapely limbs with the shackles of protection.
Down Bourke-st. (Melb.) passed the other
day, with representatives of the newest Parlia-
ment in the world, a member of about the oldest
survivor of all Parliaments—Quayle, who holds a
seat in the Manx House of Keys. Quayle, an old
Eureka Stockader, is having another look at the
land where he made his first “ rise.” _ He draws
some nice comparisons between Victorian and
Manx politics. They have had woman-suffrage
for 15 years. Their land-tenure system is practi-
cally that of perpetual leasehold ; and the largest
landholder has only 600 acres. Out of a £90,000
revenue (for last year), they pay the British Govt.
£lO,OOO for military protection, the rest goes
mostly in public works. There is no borrowing
policy ; the revenue is raised solely from a few
customs duties and the land-tax—amounting
to about a farthing in the £ on unimproved value.
Quayle is proud of his House- alleged to be 200
years older than the English Commons—and his
opinion of the Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords is
unprintable.
Premier John Greeley Jenkins is now
S.A.’s principal after-dinner speaker. Originally,
“Jinks” was a book-agent, an accompanying
business advt. being a lecture on ‘ ‘ American
Humor,” which he has delivered in nearly every
town in Wheatland. Like Kingston, Jenkins has
gone grey through office cares, though he only
completed 50 years of life last month.
His Ministry is generally supposed to
be in a minority in S.A. Assembly, but
the cute Yankee, according to a Tory
opponent, has hitherto contrived to
so set his opponents by the cars that
dislodgement is impossible.
Mr. Alfred Clint, son of Mr. v
Clint, scenic-artist of Sydney and
Melbourne, is now in M.L.,
painting for Dix’s Gaiety .Co.
The history of the Clints is a
curious instance of the per-
sistence of an hereditary im-
pulse to art. The famous
George Clint (the Painter
of Players), one of Eng-
land’s best portraitists,
gave it the start. His
son, Alfred Clint, was a
well - known landscape
painter; his grandson,
Alfred Clint (now in
Melbourne), is one of the
best-known of Australian
scenic-artists; and his
great grandson, Alfred
Clint, jun., is now making
a good start in the same
profession.
To Hector Macdonald,
when he had shown unique
capacity and extraordinary
courage, Lord Roberts, in 1877,
offered a Y.O. or an officer’s com-
mission. The alternative shows
the silly system on which the
British army is managed. For his
courage, Macdonald deserved the
V.C. ; for his capacity, the com-
mission. As a matter, not of
grace but of business, he
should have got the latter
on the principle that capable
war-leaders are so few that
the utmost must be made
of the promising colts.
Sarcasm from Sydney D.T. :
Now that the Governor-General is so much in Mel-
bourne, he outshines all smaller gubernatorial lights at
great social functions and entertainments. A Lieutenant-
Governor, be he ever so elegantly arrayed, suffers from
the diminished glory, and escapes that distracting public
curiosity which, to a man of the strikingly retiring dis-
position of the present vice-regal representative of
Victoria, must be an extremely painful experience.
S.M. Herald, which gave G. B. Barton a
few casual lines to carry him to the grave, had
one-third of a leaded column about Frank Ben-
nett’s death. But whilst Barton was a writer,
Bennett had the virtue, the supreme literary gift,
of being a Newspaper Proprietor. It is of more
importance to be able .to buy brains than to have
them by nature. The late Mr. Frank Bennett
was a kindly soul and a “ real good sort,” but he
would have been equally admirable as a whole-
sale grocer, and equally happy.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —ln th* case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value,
A new variant of the story of the woodman
snapped by a log comes from Traralgon (Gipps-
land) district. The axeman had felled a tree, and
was trying to split Bft. of the butt with maul and
wedge.s. He had got a wedge well in at crib-time,
when he perched on his log for lunch. Suddenly
the wedge jumped from the green log, as wedges
sometimes will, and the splitter was snapped from
behind with a vehemence beside which the
grip of an alligator would be a tender caress.
The poor man stayed sitting on that log for about
three hours, and was then found unconscious.
Wedges were driven home to release him, and he
is now lying on his face in hospital, wondering if
he will ever dare to sit again.
• * * *
“A.G.”: Have visited a couple of Govt,
agricultural farms, and have been amused at the
way the “wonderful results” are obtained. The
modus is very simple. The “students” —they all
have to work —stack about £lO worth of manure
on about 20 sq. ft. of land, and, after working it
in carefully, put down —say, wheat. Then, taking
the return from this particular patch, which has
been kept well watered, as a basis, it is calculated
that the surrounding country will produce an
enormous number of bushels per acre. Likewise,
they raise—at an absurd expense to the State-
some thundering big pumpkins, and things. It is
all grossly misleading, and wants looking to.
Almost incredible, also, that these people are
allowed to compete, at shows, with struggling
cockies, who can’t afford big pumpkins. There
would be something on going oil the land if Govt,
would find manure and water.
• * * *
A quaint wail from a way-back woman :
Dear Bulletin. — I should like to ask, through your
columns, why are some bushmen so much fonder of dogs
than of children —even when the children are their own ?
My husband is a N.Q. police constable, and he has two
cattle-dogs which he prizes extremely, though the brutes
are quite useless, except for killing fowls and eating beef
and eggs. He will come off duty at 1 a. in. and take his
dogs for a walk until 3 a m., yet, if at any time I ask him
to mind his child for a while, he will wither me with a
look and ask me is he to work day and night? If the
children ask him to play, he is always too tired, and he
has nothing but a snarl for them, and if I speak one word
against the dogs killing my fowls and ducks he goes and
gets drunk, and I find him under the house with his dogs,
and they are full of fleas, which get on me when I tie
them up. Now, how can a wife respect a man who thinks
more of his dogs than his children ?—X.X.

Johnny Bicycle spreads the gospel: —
At North Rockhampton, a Kanaka, rejoicing in the name
of Johnny Bicycle, has, by himself, built a small wooden
church. And here, every Friday night, he holds forth to
a congregation of a couple of dozen of his own color.
Johnny’s sermons are none too bad, either. The “ sacred
edifice ” has at each end of the roof a cross cut from ki ro-
sene-tin, and the inside walls are decorated with highly-
colored pictures of saints of the sort seen in many R.O.
houses. The pulpit is draped with cream-colored art
muslin ; and altogether, as the unaided effort ' f one
“ boy,” the place is a marvel.

“C.H.G.”: (“ Jaynol,” B. 21/9/’01) con-
founds the bell-bird with the bell-minah, or honey-
eater. The bell-bird’s diet consists of insects and
seeds, whereas the bell-minah prefers honey-dew,
a sweetish inseetal secretion upon the foliage of
gums. The bell-bird’s extraordinary ventriloquial
powers account for “ the mysterious notes coming
from various quarters.” Sometimes the note is
low, as if at a distance. Then, by degrees, it in-
creases in volume until it is apparently overhead,
the bird being all the time hidden in an adjacent
gum. Color of male—crest, brownish-black ;
forehead and lower throat, white ; upper breast,
brownish-black ; lower breast and abdomen,
white.

“0.0.” : Can any reader say why the
wallaroo and kangaroo don’t inter-breed ? Though
the wallaroo more particularly affects rocky
and hilly country, the two varieties are often
intermixed. Yet, though there is no more appar-
ent difference between them than between a brown
cob and a bay thoroughbred, I have seen nothing
I could distinguish as a cross.

“Jaf" : The correct version of the yarn
about the horse shod with gold. When “Johnny”
Cameron was returned as first member for the
Ovens (Vic.) district, his friends and supporters
hired a creamy horse from a travelling circus, and
had it shod by a station blacksmith with shoes
made of gold. Cameron was then escorted by
nearly 3000 miners to the Star Hotel, Beechworth
Alleged that the shoes are now in the possession
of a Riverina lady, but more likely they went into
the ‘ ‘ pot ” long ago.

“ Pelican ” : Says the old
man to me: “Step up to the
house, me boy. Me daughter,
Cis, married Tom Biles to-day,
and it’s a dance we are havin’
to wind things up.” I went,
and had a high old time—
leaving the old man slumbering
on the floor when 1 departed.
About a week after L met
Biles and asked him how the
wife was. “ Wife be blowed,”
said, he, “ I aint married. It
was only a put-up ’tween me
and Cis. Cis’s pal, Annie
Moore, had a masher as says
‘ I’ll marry you, Annie, when
Tom marries Cis.’ So we fixes
the rig. He takes Annie to
the chapel, and I drove off
with Cis as if to the parson.
No 1 th’ ole man didn’t know,
an’, as I paid for the liquor, he
didn’t care. Anyways, Annie
got hitched all right.”

“ V.R.” : I know a China-
man who has an old grey mare
to carry oranges for sale. The
moke will stand for hours,
whilst John carries a few dozen
“ olangee” up to any wayside
selector’s ; but should any
would-be pilferer approach Old
Misery, as she apparently
dozes, he generally gets a sen-
sation ; for the old girl springs
suddenly to life, and becomes
a regular cyclone of kicking,
squealing, and biting.
“ Pros.” : During last winter I found over
30 wombats dead in the Blue Range, Yio. The
bodies were all infested with very large ticks like
those I have seen on stock along the coast about
Mallacoota Inlet. The ’bats varied very much in
color—black, brown, grey, reddish, sandy ; one
was almost pure white.

Jack was working at the windlass, and
was in the act of pulling his mate up the shaft after
firing a shot. When the mate was half-way up
Jack’s dog and a strange tyke started to fight,
whereupon Jack pegged the windlass and went to
see fair play. Meanwhile the shot went off, bang-
ing lumps of rock against the bucket, in which
the mate remained suspended and swearing.
When the mate was at last hauled up, the dog-
fight was supplemented by a man-fight.
“ Lar.” : When I was in charge of Kanakas
on a sugar-plantation up North, and had to make;
’em rise in the morning and start yakker, one of
the women used to refuse to proceed to the cane-
brake without a saucy green silk parasol, which,
barring a shell-necklace, constituted her whole
rig-out.

Letter from a shearer at Winton, North-
west Queensland :
I’m writing this at my bedroom window at the pub.,
facing the barren, brown downs, broken by a dried-up
billabong lined with stunted, parched coolabahs, with a
few hungry hills on the heat-glimmering horizon for
background to the despairing scenery. The downs are
spotted with snow-white bleached skeletons of horses and
cattle—the victims of the last severe drought—and a few
still-hairy, half-decayed carcases are also scattered about.
No need to bury or burn cadavers up here. Two days’
sun almost turns them into mummies ; the crows and flies
do the rest. These fresh victims were probably con-
valescing from the recent severe drought, and were too
weak to battle against this renewed attack of the wither-
ing fiend. Across the way, a living skeleton of a cow is
nibbling the dry hark off a coolabah rail fence, while in
the street a feW goats are devouring some fresh horse-
droppings. These goats will follow a stable-fed horse for
miles, in anticipation of a feed. It’s a ghastly sight! Not
a vestige of greenness anywhere. If this continues, many
stations out here won’t have a single sheep to shear
next season, and travelling on horseback will be abso-
lutely impossible. As for footing it, with creeks and
tanks all empty—well, a fellow might as well suicide at
once. Almost every day news comes in of men being lost
and found dead from thirst. Do you remember old
“Paddy the Snob,” who was wool-rolling at Oondooroo
the year we were there ? He was found dead between
Bouiia and M’Kinley, a while ago. Took the usual
“short-cut” that so often leads to destruction in this
hellish country, and died of thirst. He was found undc-r
a tree, and on his billy was scratched with a knife : “ Pat
Daley. Perished.” “Perished,” you see, instead of
“ perishing.” He knew! . . . We cut-out of Aiding
ham a few days ago after only a fortnight’s shearing,
and down below, in the bar and on the verandah,
most of our fellows are fighting, cursing, blaspheming
and drinking, drinking, drinking !—notwithstanding the
fact that this may be their last shearing cheque for a year
or two to come. When their cheques are melted they’ll
jump their horses over the bar, and then, maddened and
weakened, will lave to foot it away across those endless
downs to God knows where, perhaps to meet the fate of
poor old Paddy. But the men must drink to break
their awfully hard and monotonous existence. Th?y
don’t read, and most of them have lost all ambition, and
are resigned to end their lives out here, fighting droughts,
heat, flies, crows, banks, and absentee station-owners.
Without a spree occasionally most of them would go
stark mad. I wish to God I could get out of this living-
hell to-morrow. I’m longing to see real, live green
grass once more, and to get soaking wet in a good shower
of rain. The cursed flies are getting into my eyes, nose,
and mouth. They, too, are dying for something wet.
The ink-pot is already chock-full of them, so I must close.
* * * *
“Yarrum”: At a N.S.W. country pub,
t’other clay, I struck a dish I was much taken
with. Didn’t know what it was, as it didn’t ap-
pear on the menu. That night, interviewed the
cook—a superior person —and she told me, in con-
fidence, that it was “curried concawah.” She
didn’t know the correct name of the bird, which
was a*grey thing, frequenting honeysuckles ; but
it was locally called “concawah,” on account of
the resemblance of its prolonged squeak to that
word. Anyhow, it was excellent.
* * * *
“ Y.” : Lived once in small N.S.W. town,
where, for a long time, bandicoot constituted the
leading dietary article. It started with the
lawyer, who bought up all he could get; and,
when telling us of the “ delightful flavor,” would
smack his chops and pass a flabby hand over his
capacious stomach. So, as I thought that what a
fat lawyer relished wouldn’t be a bad thing for
me—a lean catechist—l tackled bandicoot. Then
recommended it to the local banker, who took it
on, and ’coot was soon in such demand that the
price rose from 6d. a-head to 4s. 6d., and hard to
get at that.
* • » *
This year Wimmera (Vic.) crops are being
smothered with “ red poppy.” Its appearance
dates from a couple of years back. Prior to that,
cockspur, star thistle, Bathurst burr, and the
“ white weed,” or dwarf species of the everlasting
daisy, held sway each in turn. Very often a flower
plague is the result of the mischievous pining o
home-sick exile for some remembrance of his nativ
land. Scotchmen are the worst offendersm *
respect, and to their nostalgia we are indebted io
the thistle. The English daisy and the briar rose
are other agricultural pests imported for senti-
ment’s sake, and now most heartily cursed.
* * * *
Maoriland “ fancies ” itself more and more
every year. It is now asked by Wellington Pos r
to believe that it was the Garden of Eden, on the
ground mainly that —
At Rotorua, and many other places Pacific,
Nature had cooking-places all readv prepared, thus the
peopling of the world may have begun from Polynesia,
for we know that all the conditions are there with all tne
possibilities for development ; and that a better supplied
and arranged establishment could not be designed for
scattering a primitive people over the world.
In short, if the Deity didn't start the human race
in M.L. it made a big mistake.
* * * *
The pilchards which have for some time
swarmed in Port Phillip afford good sport to the
blue barracouta, a sober, methodical tish, which
refrains from rushing open-mouthed at the shoal
and swallowing all he gets, but systematically
spots a fat victim and chases him until near
enough to bite off his tail. Left thus helpless, the
pilchard is leisurely assimilated. If the ’couta be
caught and opened atter a meal, his victims will
be found methodically stowed like sardines in a
tin—the heads all one way, the tails mutilated or
missing.
“Thurla”: Be bell-bird (B. 12/9/01).
“Jaynol” is correct. “Scutum” is evidently
alluding to the crested wood-pecker which has
a green back and yellow breast. It is the male
bell-bird that utters the bell-notes; the female
makes only a harsh, grating noise. The male is
slightly larger than the ordinary woodpecker, with
light-grey plumage, crest, and black throat and
breast, but the hen is a plain drab.

“ Yarrum” : What a lot of things that could
be grown profitably in Australia are neglected !
now that there is a stiffish duty on hops farmers
ought to grow all we need. The hop certain’y
thrives well on N.S.W. south coast. At Nelligen
(Clyde River) some years ago I knew a Kent man
to have a splendid crop. There was then “ only
one thing,” he said, “ that stopped him from going
in for it largely.” There is nothing now. [But
Tasmania grows hops in plenty already. Ti e
trouble is that brewers use so little of them.]

“ Edson ” : Be a recent, discussion on
“ Au-sterile,” and the fate of the human race in
inhospitable lands, note Prof. Baldwin Spencer s
description of the country the Spencer-G i Hen expe-
dition lately passed through, from Barrow to
Tennant’s Creek : “Simply abominable—a waste
of mulga scrub and white-ant hills, varied now
and again by miles of porcupine grass.” Speaking
of the local aborigines, however, Prof. Spencer
declares he finds them physically “ the finest
Australian natives he has ever seen. Winch
bears out my theory: while there’s life there s hope.
* * * *
“ Old Cove ” : Things are altering ! The
Australian still dearly loves a horse—to save his
legs—but he likes to sit behind it—on wheels. In
these parts, owing to the previousness of events,
marriages are often made in haste, but every self-
respecting couple (or trio) must commence matri-
monial life with a horse and trap, though they
“doss” on a sheet of bark in a hay-shed, and
camp the kid in a candle-box. Also, a swagman
—a real, genuine swaggie—passed last week carry-
ing an umbrella, and the last traveller that called
here insisted—positively insisted—on earning his
dole at the wood-heap.

“ Frawth ” : Re “ Fraulein ” and her
snake-bitten dog (B. 8/6/’01). A few years back I
was watching a mob of sheep being driven through
a gate at Ponto. The dog was rounding them up
when I was attracted by his furious barking.
Rode over and found that the object of his atten-
tion was a death-adder some 10ft. away. Suddenly
the reptile doubled itself and sprang—striking the
dog and rebounding to its former position, I lie
dog howled pitifully for a few minutes, then
showed signs of being sick, and in 12 minutes was
dead. Mr. Kennedy of Ponto (who, only the
previous week, had refused £25 for the do") can
corroborate. [Mr. K. could doubtless testify to
the loss of the dog, and the offer of £25, but there
doesn’t appear to be any evidence but “ Frawth’s”
own about this marvellous 10-foot spring of the
adder. —Ed. B.]
Since the conquest of Constantinople by
the Turks there have been 38 Sultans, of whom
only four have died natural deaths. What a
record!
In an interview with a Herald (Melb.)
reporter (3/10/’01) a just-returned Fifth Con-
tingenter stated that when Steele and two others
tried to get their comrades to'agree not to go out
with General Beat son again, about 300 decided
to follow their advice, but afterwards backed out.
The portrait herewith is that of Miss
Lilian Wheeler, a comely and capable Australian
actress (boni in Castlemaine, Vic.), who is going
to seek her fortune in London. Australia sends
all its best young artistes to London, and gets
back consumptives, shoddy and Governors. The
photo, represents Miss Wheeler as Mrs. Ford in
‘‘The Merry Wives of Windsor.” Though still
very young, she has played a multitude of good
parts and played them admirably—as Desde
niona, Miladi in “ Three Musketeers,” and Dolores
in that beautiful but unappreciated play, “The
Flesh and the Devil,” she was even more than
admirable. The writer will always remember “ the
Flesh and the Devil ” by reason of Henry Diver’s
picturesque Duke, of Alva and Miss Wheeler’s
rendering of the lady who called on him and laid
the information against a certain short, thick-set
Dutch patriot who shall be nameless. Every year
“stars” without a quarter of Miss Wheeler’s
capacity are imported from England, at high
salaries, but the local artiste is mostly crushed by
the uprising of somebody who says, briefly : “ She
can’t act; why, my sister used' to go to school with
her.” The Bulletin trusts that Miss Wheeler
fully realises her folly,in selecting this country as
a place to be born in, and won’t do it again.
According to Rev. Dr. Corlette,
there are in Sydney plenty of single-
roomed dwellings, the rental of which
—about 3s. a week—is on a par with
the low-grade rents in the Olerkenwell
slums of London. “ Advance Aus-
tralia !”
The two smiling, curly-headed
lads who tramped from Invercargill to
Dunedin (M.L.) to see the Duke, and
who were widely paragraphed, treated
like lordlings, and publicly presented
to the royal couple, have bioken out in
a fresh place. Thirsting for more glory,
they valiantly secured a boat and some
fog-signals and started manoeuvring up
the coast. Their adventures terminated
rudely in the police-court, where they
were ordered to an industrial school.
“Lillipilli” : I have seen N.S.W.
inquests at which, by a farcical theory,
the whole aim of 14 or 15 people, mostly
officials, has been to sift matters till
a cause of death could be laid bare ;
and I have seen such presided over
by drunken coroners. I have seen, also,
drunken doctors trying to stagger
through evidence, with a possible ver-
dict of “murder” at the end. Does
the Department know ?
The advertisment of the Aus-
tralian Mutual Provident Society, ap-
pearing in another column, forcibly
reminds one that the first year of the
twentieth century is fast drawing to a
close, and also directs attention to the
fact that the next Annual Distribution
of the profits of the Society will be
made at 31st. December next. To
those of our readers who are already
members of the Society this announce-
ment will not occasion any surprise,
but to any who may be uninsured
they should seize the first favourable
opportunity of securing a policy in
this well known institution, and
thereby reaping the benefit of the
bonus to be declared for this year.
Those who already carry assurances
on their lives, if they consider the
amount so carried insufficient for the purpose,
should lose no time in effecting further assurance.
The amount distributed in cash-bonuses for the
year 1900 was £537,895, a sum larger than has
ever been divided in a corresponding period by
any other mutual life office in the British
Empire. It will be well, therefore, for those
contemplating assuring their lives to do so with-
out any further delay.*

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC) CONTRIBUTORS.
Tusker : Again— Tub Bulletin doesn’t claim to be an
absolutely original paper ; but it tries to be as original
'as possible. It never consciously uses another paper’s
matter without acknowledgment, but it hasn’t time to
read and remember the contents of all the countless
thousands of papers printed. Therefore, it is occasionally
imposed upon by people who trade chestnuts off
upon it for money. The Bulletin always tries to be
honest, both as regards literary matter and drawings.
It never steals pictures—it is not on the same plane with
Robber-Shots and Prig-Shots and Thief-Bits— and that
is one reason why it has lived and prospered where other
papers have died... .Walter T.C. : One, or both, will do..
Father John : Joke about St. Peter, the Devil, and
the Freetrade soul passed on to George Reid, for use
when he has become a Protectionist....
Amongst the Coal Pits.
When a special commissioner was visiting the
Bulli district he had the pleasure of meeting Mrs.
Henry Marsh, wife of a well-known and experi-
enced miner, then engaged at the Woonoona pit,
a mile or so outside Bulli. Some of the experi-
ences Mrs. Marsh told him of proved most
interesting.
“I have lived in this district for twelve years,
said she, “ eight of which we have been in the
house we now occupy. I well remember that just
about eleven years ago, after the birth of twins,
T became very ill. An operation was performed
but I was left very weak, and a victim to nervous
debility and exhaustion. I experienced sharp
pains between the shoulders, a wheezing in my
throat, and sometimes I would have to loosen my
clothes to enable me to breathe. I have been
laid up for three weeks at a time, and was often so
bad that I could not walk straight, and had to
hold on to the furniture to help myself along.
Frequently I lay awake till three in the morn-
ing."
“ No doubt this further injured your health ?
“Indeed, it did, for when I got up I felt as
though I had not been to bed at all. Dreadful
neuralgic pains shot from under my eyes into my
head, causing almost unendurable agony. Take
what I would, I could not find a cure. Then I
read about Dr. Williams’ pink pills in the Sydney
Daily Telegraph and decided to try them.
After using them for a weak I really felt greatly
improved. I was slightly stronger, and the pains
in my head and back grew less. The action of the
pills also brought away some congealed blood,
the color of black ink, from beneath my breast,
where I had been operated upon. I continued
the pills carefully according to directions, and
was before long restored to perfect health. I
never want to lie down now, I feel bright and
fresh, and since my recovery I have planted
a nice little garden, which I take a delight in
tending. I confidently recommend Dr. Williams’
pink pills to every woman.”
Ladies afflicted with ill health will find Mrs.
Marsh’s advice invaluable. Dr. Williams’ pink
pills enrich the blood, tone up the nerves,
strengthen the spine, and build up the constitu-
tion in such a way as to remove every symptom
of disease. They cure chronic dyspepsia,
neuralgia, sciatica, rheumatism, anaemia, debility,
consumption, bronchitis, disfiguring skin diseases,
effects of influenza, pneumonia, measles, etc.
Bold by chemists and storekeepers and by the
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co., Queen’s Place,
Sydney, one box two and nine, six boxes fifteen
and three, post free. Write a description of
your case and receive valuable information.

Notwithstanding the tariff on “Primus"
kerosene gas-stoves Brandt Bros., 236 Pitt-St.,
charge old prices. They cook for a large family
at Is. per week. If used with their oven, from.

taneously.*
THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian,
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par-
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance,
is solicited.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Victoria’s Pecksniff Ministry only carried
the reduction of the maximum Old Age Pension
from 10s. to 7s. by one vote —35 for 10s. a wee k ;
30 for 7s.
Brisbane Street now spells it “ Kanka.”
But not, be it noted, on purpose, for the Street
is rabid Kanaka. In which connection “ Street ”
is an excellent name.
The Labor parties in all the Australian
States and in the Federal Houses contain, propor-
tionately to size, the greatest number of lay-
preachers as well as the greatest number of total
abstainers.
Though Queensland Parliament is sitting,
only half-a-dozen members were on the platform
at the recent Brisbane pro-Kanaka meeting. The
elections are too close and the Commonwealth
voting was too decided for that.
South Australia, in Parliament and in the
Press, is protesting vigorously against the Federal
desire to pay £3OOO a year to the Military Com-
mandant. The general idea is that as Canada can
get a good man for half the money, Australia
should be able to do ditto.
A newly-elected member of N. S. W. Assem-
bly received instructions from a small centre in
his electorate to purchase a dozen second-hand
overcoats, moderate in price, and as much alike as
possible, to be used as uniform great-coats for the
town band. It is with problems like this that the
State politician now wrestles.
THAT GOVERNMENT SERVICE.
CISSIE : “ Isn't he a nice johnnie?”
GIRLLE : “ Yes. I wonder what he’s doing for a
living. He's too well-dressed to be of inde-
pendent means, and doesn’t seem to have
enough brains for a profession.”
CISSIE : “ Oh, I suppose he is in the Civil
Service.”
“Eko”: Looking at the collection of
Federal flag designs, shown in Melbourne lately,
I came across one that woke up a dormant brain-
cell—five silver stars on a blue ground. To my
knowledge I had never seen it before, but some
hereditary influence made me raise my hat to it.
Then I recognised it as the flag under which
dad had fought and received a wound nearly 50
years ago, when the Republic of Victoria began
the career that ended at the Eureka Stockade on
l)ec. 3rd, ’55.
The threatened insurrection up North for
the retention of Kanakas, would, if it were
not absurdly impossible, be a brand - new
example of the hoisting petard. When Fede-
ration was in the air, soon after the Q.
shearers’ strike, the most alluring point in the
programme was Parkes’ statement that Federal
troops would be available to repress turbulence in
every part of Australia. The petard would work
if the first duty of the Federal troops was to
“ deal it out ” to Kanaka bosses.
The great curse levelled at the tariff by
the Sydney importing crowd recalls the emphasis
with which they attacked the Dibbs advalorem
duties in the early 90’s. Some of those who
shrieked loudest were all the time in mortal fear
lest the impost on their particular items should be
removed, and Dibbs was occupied all his time with
trembling Freetraders, who crawled into his office
under cover of strict secrecy, and pointed out how
terribly they would suffer if the duties were taken
off after they had so carefully prepared themselves
for the awful tariff.
The natural dishonesty of municipal
institutions is strikingly evidenced in Victoria.
The Vic. Government recently wrote off as bad
£1,642,711 of the money it had lent to water trusts
and other local bodies. The irrigation trusts
alone were relieved of £1,057,571 of the debt they
had incurred to the State, and left owing only
£316,600. And now the State finds it as hard to
collect any interest as ever, and unless it passes a
law giving the Treasurer power to himself levy
special rates on the irrigated land it might as well
write off the balance and drop the whole business.
Cabled that Horace Tozer, speaking in
London, has expressed “ Australia’s gratitude for
the splendid work of the Duke of York in con-
solidating the Empire.” A tozer, a palpable
tozer; and the same crawlsome falsehood is to be
repeated by the Australian Agents-General,
wriggling like a tin of sand-worms before the
little Duke, hailing him as the concrete expression
of the Imperial idea, the impressive and over-
awing type of British Majesty. The Bulletin
begins to imagine the airs put on by the West-
minster Abbey worms when they meet relatives
from plebeian graveyards.
“ It might be better,” writes a little hurdy-
gurdy organ run by the naval and military clique
of Australia, “ this year, for the Com-
monwealth Parliament to present the
Admiralty with £1,000,000 (1) as a
contribution to the maintenance of
the Australian fleet, and (2) for
the consideration of four men-of-
war suitable as training ships;
or (3) for the presentation of
the Katoomba cruisers to the
Commonwealth, if they are
replaced by more modern
ships, as in-
dicated in
H.E. Admiral
Sir L. Beau-
mont’s des-
p ate h to
H. E. the
Grovemor-
Genera 1 ;
but all the
naval force
to be under the
Admiral of the
station.” The cool-
ness of J. Bull and
his parasites ! Aus-
tralia is asked to spend
£1,000,000 on scrap-
iron, sold at inflated
rates, and then to
still leave the
scrap-iron in its
original custody.
More than likely a
big factor in the ensuing
Q. elections will be see-
tarianism. The Ministry
is taking—or allowing a
mob of parsons to take—
a mongrel plebiscite of
parents of children at-
tending several big
State schools on the
question of State Bible-
thumping. The question
is about the only one
likely to cause a real
split in the Labor
Opposition ranks.
Already in one
electorate Orange
and Green are
sparring over it. If
the Laborites let them-
selves be sooled at one
another’s throats on the
subject more fools they! The Ministry, of
course, has no convictions on the subject and only
twists the pups’ tails for its own ends.
T’other day Softgoods Sargood stated in
all three Melbourne dailies “that these high
duties are not needed is proved by the fact that
my M.L. firm commenced to manufacture boots
long before any duty was thought of, and now
employs some 300 hands, although the duty is only
per cent., as compared with the late Victorian
of from 25 to 150 per cent.” Next day, some
officious person fished up the sworn evidence,
before the Arbitration Court lately, of Sargood’s
Maoriland manager, stating that the 22f per
cent- ad valorem duty was “ not sufficient to pre-
vent importations increasing at the expense of
manufactures.” A freetrader requires a good
memory.
THE SOUTHERN CROSS.
S.M. Herald wants the tariff to be
decided—not by “a counting of noses” in the
Federal Parliament, but by an appeal to the
people—the unholy Referendum, in fact. That
tax on printing-paper has radicalised the Herald.
In the light of cable accounts that the
Boers are able to import over sea cannon and
ammunition, Ketchener’s proclamation that they
have no regular army in the field, and must be
treated as brigands shows up as the coolest of
bluff.
Sydney D.T. can’t find a single “ Billite ”
in Sydney and suburbs now. But, then, it
couldn’t find any trace of a supporter of the See
Govt, before the last State election. The King-
street daily should remember that election—it
was only about three months aero.
Since the Tied Houses Bill was rejected in
N.S.W. Council the lobbies have been relieved of
the presence of gorgeous beer-canvassers, and
the brewers, who were quite willing that the
measure should be passed, are doing their best to
bear up bravely under a Great Sorrow.
Chief Justice Griffith denounces as a crime
against Australia any attempt to revive the ques-
tion of religious instruction in State schools. It
is well to find Sam Griffith staunch to an early
principle. He helped Lilley in the old days to
popularise the free, secular, and compulsory doc-
trine.
One thing is pretty sure—while they have
to pay 4d. per lb. duty the importers won’t bring
twopenny tea to Australia. While tea was free in
N.S. Wales a lot of them did import such rubbish
—and divided the huge profits they made on it
between their own pockets and newspaper adver-
tising.
The Pecksniff Ministry of Victoria has
apparently reached a hopeless state of drift. The
Old Age Pensions (Permanent) Bill is still before
the Assembly. Two-thirds of the session has
gone, and practically not one measure passed.
The Convention Bill, for the reform of the State
Constitution, is hung up—in the hope that it will
die of suffocation. Educational reform, infant-
life protection, public service reorganisation,
grants to shires, conciliation and arbitration, re-
classification of railway employees, inspection of
boilers and of scaffolding, and prevention of
adulteration in foods are also neglected promises.
The Exported Products Bill was promptly thrown
out by the House of Fat, and so the season has not
a single useful measure to credit.
Tasmania’s Leg. Council has a vast amount
more horse-sense than its dreadful Victorian
brother. The Vic. House of 48 Rich Landlords
threw out the Exported Products Bill, intended
to prevent the export of bad produce, which, by
damaging the repute of all Victorian goods,
brings down values all round, and makes the
State lose many thousands annually. Tasmanian
Council also had an Exported Products Bill sent
up to it, and, instead of rejecting the same, it
seized it with both hands and added this clause to
make dead sure that all was right:—
The inspector may forbid the export of any product
which he may consider unfit for human food, or in
such condition as likely to injure other products ex-
ported at the same time, or of such inferior quality or
condition from any cause as to be unfit for the purpose
to which it is usually put.
Premier Jenkins, of S.A., is making some
progress with his measure for cutting down the
two Houses of the State Parliament, but with the
party which wants no reduction at all, and the
party which favors a reduction of each House,
and the party which wants to preserve the Assem-
bly as it stands and abolish the Council, and the
party which wants to refer the whole business to
an elected Convention, Jenkins is steering in
troubled waters. About 30 legislators would be
quite enough to run S.A.’s State concerns, and
then they could be paid ,£4OO or £5OO apiece, at
which figure good men should be procurable.
But 30 legislators, divided into an Assembly of 18
(six of them Ministers) and a Council of 12, would
be about as ridiculous as an army of three men
divided into seven regiments, a general staff and
a brass band.
“F.” : The political labor movement has
so greatly helped the industrial agitation for
an eight-hours’ work day, that a common
rule of 48 hours a week for male workers seems
likely to be soon attained. It is time,
therefore, that some more especial attention
should be given to the sweating of women.
Mean employers, who dare not sweat 11-stone
men, banded in to 11,000-stone unions, take
general advantage of her helplessness to sweat
8-stone women. The male workers should
stretch out both hands now to lift their
sisters out of the ruck. One most valuable
bit of assistance would be to make a little
easier the hours and conditions of work of
the woman who takes the job of being the
working-man’s wife, Reform in that direc-
tion would relieve a good deal of the sweating
by big employers. Too often the female
factory hand sees in marriage only the nro-
spect of longer hours and harder labor.
In view of the loud assurances ot the
Queensland black labor gang that, if the
Kanaka Bill is passed, financial institutions
will never again advance a penny to any
sugar-grower—did these institutions cease
making advances in 1891, when the supply
of Kanaka labor was positively stopped ? If
they did, what happened ? If anything very
bad happened, how was it that the sugar
industry expanded so remarkably during that,
period ? And if there is such a terrible down-
fall impending, and the sugar industry is.
only half as lost as it is alleged to be, how is it
that the local capitalist continues to give such &
high price for the shares of the Colonial Sugar
Co. ? A very large amount of this co.’s property
is in Queensland, and if that property is prac-
tically destroyed, then Colonial Sugar Co.’s scrip ■
should be getting down fast towards waste-paper-
rates. But it isn’t —and the market talks every
time.

make the Government wince. The 7s. maximum was
carried by only one vote, and even then Ministers had
to rely upon such arch-Conservatives as Mr. Gillies and:
Mr. Madden for their main support.” — AGE.
N.S.W. Minister for Labor, in inviting
“anybody who knows that the Factories Act is-
being evaded to let the department know,” forgets,
the fate of various individuals who, because they
did their duty by the State against the interests of
a private employer or a departmental boss, have-
been sacrificed "without a word of Government
protest.
