The National Aspoct of Production.
Some remarks by a correspondent- whose
is illegible :—•
Dear Bulletin,—How is it that both sides in the fisc,
battle ignore the national aspect of the matter, and ba~
all their arguments on the economic side of the question ';
As far as economics are concerned, I am personally an
absolute Freetrader—a believer in real Freetrade, not th
bastard systems of England and, worse still, N.S.W. But,
however strongly I am convinced of the economic sound-
ness of this principle, it clashes so materially with my
national aspirations as an Australian that I am forced ti
cast my economic considerations aside, and take on a
plainer system which can cook and wash and look afte r
the national household. Australia, in the first instance
extends almost from the equator to far into the temperat
zone. Everything desired by the heart of man is capahl
of being produced within her limits. If the Millenniur,
could be declared by proclamation in the Commonwealt:
Gazette, I would certainly say, let us have Freetrade
but there seems to be no immediate likelihood of :
universal peace, so I go strongly for a protection which
would amount to absolute prohibition. My object it
this : Under prohibition Australia would be forced to
grow everything she consumes, and that is the natural
and proper position for a country which may at any time
be cut off from the rest of the world. Not only should
those industries already worked in a dilettante fashion be
extended until they supply all local requirements, but
enterprises which would mean an absolute, or rather,
apparent money loss, such as iron-mining and tea-grow-
ing, should be established. Nobody can say that a month
hence Australia will not be embroiled in a European war,
and the navy of Great Britain may be too busy
otherwise to protect Australia’s commerce. Where will
our tea and our iron and all the rest of the things we
consume come from then? Thanks to the measure of
Protection which has been given to some of the States in
the past, the people will not be left with nothing but
meat, wheat and ’possum skins; nevertheless, many
articles are not manufactured in Australia at all, and
when these wear out there will be none to take their
place. The picture is not overdrawn. Britain is the first
consideration in a war-emergency and properly so. She
is utterly dependent on foreign nations for her
very food, and even if every cargo boat has to be
escorted by a battle-ship, she must live and, if anyone is
to suffer, it must be those parts of the Empire which are
better able to feed themselves. In England the economic
overshadows the national. The situation of the
United Kingdom is such that only a small proportion of
the articles consumed are produced there, and Freetrade,
with its concomitant, a great navv, sits like a
nightmare on the chest of the nation. This is not the case
in Australia. We can grow everything, make everything.
Having provided the means of doing so, we want a navy,
not for the huge purpose of protecting thousands of ships,
but simply to keep an enemy out of our ports. Is it
better to grow your vegetables in your own yard, or to
get them from a distant Chinaman and pay an expensive
police force to keep him from being robbed on the way to
your gate ?
Summing-up by the late Irish Judge
Morris in an abduction case: —
“ I am compelled to direct you to find a verdict of
guilty in this ease, but you will aisily see that I think
it a trifling thing, quite unfit to occupy my time. It is
more valuable than yours. At any rate, it is much better
paid for. Find, therefore, the prisoner guilty of abduc- !
tion, which rests, mind ye, on four points—the father was
not averse, the mother was not opposed, the gurrl was
willing, and the hhov was convaynient.” Verdict —guilty :
sentence—the rising.”
A “nut" from Edmund Burke for the
cheap Australian Jingo :
I cannot conceive any existence under heaven—which
in the depths of its wisdom tolerates all sorts of things
tha't is more truly odious and disgusting than the im-
potent, helpless creature, without civil wisdom or military
skill, without a consciousness or any other qualification
for power hut his servility, bloated with pride and
arrogance, calling for battles which he is not to fight, and
contending for a violent dominion which he can never
exercise.
Said on the authority of Henri Vangeois,
a leader of the French “ Nationalist ” party, that
the British bullet which found Villebois-Mareuil
did valuable service to the French Republic, as
Mareuil intended the S’African campaign as a
'point (Uappui for a revolution.
Before Melbourne Practice Court a
a novel point was unsuccessfully taken against
the conviction of John Rose, lately fined
for “profanity.” The appeal relied on the fact
that the magistrates who signed the information
had omitted to put full stops between the letters
J.P. and P.M. following their signatures 1
They have a poor opinion of Ms.P. in Lis-
more, N.S. W. A man applied for an hotel license,
and produced testimonials from M’sL.A. The
P.M. said “he would sooner take a testimonial
from any other man than an M.P. The last place
in the world that one of these should be produced,”
said his Worship, “ was in a court, and to attempt
to introduce them showed bad taste.” To such a
depth of degradation have honorable members (!)
sunk in their desire to pitchfork “poor relations”
and bedraggled friends into billets !
Compulsory arbitration need have no
terror for the employer. Grumbling at its opera-
tions is more likely to come from the other side.
The first trade dispute arbitrated on in N.S. W.
under Barton’s Act (since expired) was that of the
Helensburgh colliers and their employers. The
men did not find the award at all to their liking,
and were at first much incensed against the labor
member of the Court (P. J. Brennan). After a
while, however, matters settled down. Arbitration
Courts will certainly tend to industrial peace, but
they will not, if properly administered, favor any
side.
“Yang Anyut ” : Re multiplicity of offices.
A friend of mine in British North Borneo was at
one time deputy-commandant of constabulary,
supt. of fire-brigade, supt. of gaols, postmaster-
general, harbor-master, assistant private sec. to
the Governor, inspector of slaughter-houses, in-
spector of markets, inspector of gambling-farm,
inspector of brothels, inspector of weights and
measures, inspector of opium farm, inspector of
turtle egg-farm, assistant immigration officer,
chief boarding-officer, &c. In his spare time, he
assisted in the Govt. Treasury, managed property
for absentee landlords, sub edited the Govern-
ment Gazette, took photographs professionally,
and dealt in native arms and curios. He was,
besides, a good botanist, entomologist, and
taxidermist, and acted as interpreter to the
Supreme Court. And all this at 18 years of age !
A Maoriland man has issued the plans
and specifications of a new religion. The central
ideas appear to be that people are only to marry
in leap-year, and that they are only to have
children in leap-year. Three years out of the
four they are to swear off the lusts of the flesh,
and the fourth year the twins are to bellow in
the land, and perambulators are to rush over the
face of the earth like the cataract that goes down
at Lodore. The Bulletin hasn’t exactly got the
hang of the advantages that are to arise from
this arrangement—but there are certainad vantages.
The trouble is that the infant hasn’t been con-
sulted as to whether it wants to be born only in
leap-year or not. And the infant is like the wind
and bloweth where it listeth—both infants do,
if they are twins ; and it arrives casually, like the
locust, at no fixed date, and furiously drives its
go-cart over the tail of the domestic cat, and
there is no arguing with it. In this country the
infant is born when it darned well pleases, and it
will probably keep on like that.


Only a Few Days More.
NOTICE OF WITHDRAWAL OF THE OFFER.
The time has now come when the offer of the “Library of Famous Literature” on the exceptionally generous terms set forth, in these announcements must
be finally and irrevocably withdrawn. The response on the part of the public of New South Wales has been so prompt that the copies available for this State will
be exhausted earlier than was expected. When the last copy has been applied for no more can be secured and no orders for this work can be accepted on any terms.
In older to place residents of every part of the State on an equal footing, an effort will be made to accept all orders posted not later than the date of withdrawal.
This date will be widely announced in the daily press of New South Wales, but readers of these announcements are warned against further delay if they intend to
take advantage of this opportunity.
If the Order form printed below is posted at once one of the few remaining copies can be secured. To-day there is still time to act. Next week will be too late.
If there are any who are inclined to think that they may at some future date secure the “Library of Famous Literature” on some other terms than those
now offered, and if they delay accepting the present offer in that expectation, they will be doomed to disappointment. When this offer has been withdrawn, no more
copies of the ‘ Library can be secured on any terms. Those who delay, and thereby miss this chance of a lifetime, will only have themselves to blame.
CUbai your neighbours Chink.

In Sydney.
The announcements concerning the “ Library of Famous Literature” which have recently been
appearing in the press of New South Wales have aroused unusual interest on the part of the public,
and thousands have applied for Specimen Pages and full particulars. In response to the thousands of
inquiries which have been received, and which continue to pour in from every part of the
State, thousands of handsomely illustrated Prospectuses, together with an Order Form giving
in detail the terms and prices, have been sent to all parts of New South Wales. For the
benefit, therefore, of those whose desire for information concerning the work itself may have
been satisfied by the announcements appearing in the press, and who now desire only information
concerning the terms and prices, a complete Order Form is printed below. Those who still desire to
secure Specimen Pages before giving their orders will be accommodated as long as the sunnlv of
Specimen Pages last. J
For the moment prompt delivery of orders can be made from the supply of books now in Svdnev
But this offer will be withdrawn before another issue of The Bulletin appears. Readers of The
Bulletin are hereby warned that this is the last opporunity to secure this great work There is
longer time for hesitation ; there is barely time for acceptance. The Order Form printed below should
be posted to-day.
This is the Last Issue of “ The Bulletin ” in which this announcement will appear.

A Critical Case.
A Senior-Inspector of Police Submits his
Evidence.
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills add one more
name to the long list of their wonderful cures.
It is for those once placed on the verge of the
grave, and who have been given a longer term
of life by the timely use of this wonderful
remedy, to give the Pills their due of praise. All
who use them give them a good name, but it
is only those who, after a long and dangerous
sickness, feel through their use the rich blood of
perfect health in their veins, that can bring
warmth and conviction and the clear ring of
manly and vigorous truth to their words. Such
a man is Senior-Inspector W. T. Langworthy, of
Yass, New South Wales, who writes as fol-
lows : —“ Dear Sirs, —For many years I have been
a sufferer from rheumatism, constipation, giddi-
ness, violent pains in the head, and a ‘liver,’
colds, chills, and worse than all, lumbago; of
course, I got temporary relief from doctors and
chemists. At last I read your pamphlet on Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills, and 1 considered the
vegetable ingredients would not hurt, and took
four—then three, and no pain. The change was
wonderful. Instead of leaning against the wall to
put on my trousers, as usual, through giddiness, I
was able to stand upright, and I felt a desire to
turn a somersault upon the bed as a boy would.
You will laugh. Well, sirs, it is a fact. I have
used them now occasionally for three years, and
carry the little bottle with me on the road when
travelling. I recommend them to everybody,
with good results. Whenever I look at the
little bottle, I feel that I owe one debt to you,
and now I will repay it by expressing my
gratitude.” (I have no objection to my
testimonial being used relative to the benefit
I derived from Dr. Morse’s Indian Root
Pills.—W. T. Lang worthy.) Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills are a perfect blood purifier
and a positive cure for biliousness, indigestion,
constipation, headaches, sallow complexion,
liver and kidney troubles, piles, pimples, boils,
blotches, and female ailments. Sold by chemists
and storekeepers, Is l£d per bottle, or six bottles
6s. 6d. Sole proprietors, The W. H. Comstock
Co., Ltd. (Australasian Depot), 58 Pitt-street,
Sydney. Packed in amber bottles, and the full
name blown thereon.*

Cup, but it is still better to select that which

in life. D.C.L. whisky does this by keeping all
the human functions at their highest point of

Crudely defined, a planet is “a celestial
body moving about the sun,” and Planet is the
name of a new mechanical body moving about the
lawn. It takes Divine power to set the celestial
body in motion, but a child can move the mechani-
cal body with ease. It is beautifully built in
three sizes, with regulating screw (we refer to the
mechanical body), and for 155., 17s. 6d., or 205.,
according to the size of it, we can give you the
pleasure of moving the Planet Lawn-Mower about
your lawn, leaving law, order and neatness in
your track. M'Lean Bros, and Rigg, Ltd., 107
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne.*
The Bulletin acknowleges receipt of the Ade
laide Steamship Co.’s Guide for 1901-2. The work is
well got-up, tastefully illustrated, and, besides all pos-
sible information as to the fares and freights between the
various ports and the condition of the service carried on
by the co.’s fleet of 25 steamers (to say nothing of its eight
steam tenders and lighters and tugs), contains a lot of
really interesting and valuable information about the
Commonwealth generally. Anyone desiring to deceive
his friends with the idea that he is a travelled individual,
who has been all over the continent, should procure the
Guide, after which he can lie fluently and reliably about
the particulars of climate, the names of the streets, the
post-offices and chief buildings of every town and district
worth lying about.

Presuming that the handicapper “ brought them

between them should be close, but one naturally prefers
the chance of the flying mare.
* • • •
Hymettus, on- the Caulfield running (in Stakes
and Cup), has nothing the better of Wakeful, for although
an extra 31b. may give no extra trouble to the big-
hearted giant, 41b. off the back of the comparatively
little mare should count for a full 640 z. in her favor.
Again, Wakeful has already finished a good third in a
Sydney Oup, whereas Hymettus, whose legs are un-
doubtedly near the end of their racing career, has never
yet gone two miles in public. The writer takes Wakeful
to beat all the well-known performers in the coming Cup
• * • •
Nitre is a possible winner on her best form, her
appearance, and her private reputation ; and George
Frederick is a real good animal—for all that he has been a
frequent disappointment. Flagship’s chance is a question
of staying power. The four-year-old was a respectable
third in the Caulfield Oup, and with 6.9 up he ought to
finish at the front end of the field next Tuesday.

as a certainty for that race, which would have brought her

Revenue at fours, Wakeful lengthened out to tens with
big parcels on offer.
* * *' 9
To fancy Revenue for the Cup is to believe that
the stable has made no mistake in judging the private
form of the Trenton-Water Lily horse. Punters can
take it for granted that Revenue has been tried with
Wakeful, and the mare, being a generous, headstrong
lady, she is not likely to have deceived her trainer in
private out of sheer cussedness. Besides, the watch is a
check on such possibilities. The public have seen Revenue
do nothing- estimable since March ’92, when he scored his
third and last win as a two-year-old. He ran wretchedly
in the Cup last year, hut the Revenue of 12
months ago was the sickest-looking nag you
ever saw. As h= stood in his stall in Fleming'tori
bird-cage, the flies settled on his woe-begone counten-
ance and his eyes were full of tears. How he ran last year
i- a matter of no importance. Revenue has been a deli-
cate horse, but it was good enough for Manager Leslie
Macdonald to buy him at the sale of the late W. R.
Wilson s stud. The stable always had a great opinion of
Revenue, fit and well. The 5-year-old was fit and well a
iveek ago, and will probably remain so until Cup day.

* • * .
Something has got to win the Derby next
Saturday ere the Melbourne Cup comes to pass. Haut-
villers, Ibex, Grasspan and Haymaker are likely to
provide the finish for the blue ribbon race, and of these
the two first-named seem to be the only youngsters of
possibly brilliant) merit, the possibility, even "in their
cases, being very slight. Ibex was not entered for the
Cup, so if he wins on Saturday the Cup chance of Haut-
villers, with 7.12 to carry, will look poor indeed, whereas
Grasspan (7.6) and Haymaker (6.13) may be still worth
considering. A few pounds more or less go for a good
deal with a young 3-year-old in a two-mile ding-dong
struggle. Nevertheless it is reasonable to reckon that
this year’s Derby colts can be counted out of the Cup
In competition with older horses all of them, save
Ibex, have cut second-rate figures. Hautvillers, after
winning the A.J.C. Derby in good time, ran moderately
in the Metrop. two days later, when the pace was faster.
Grasspan and Haymaker were nowhere in the Eclipse
Stakes at Caulfield, when Hymettus nearly succeeded in
giving 311bs. to Bonnie Chiel, the winner. In short, the
8-year-olds thus far have been seeminglv outclassed.
Present scribe guesses that Grasspan is the sturdiest of
the lot, and will win the Derby from Hautvillers, Ibex,
and Haymaker. Also Grasspan should finish among the
first half-dozen or so in the Cup, but some of the others
will probably be lengths ahead of Grafton’s respectable
offspring.
• * * *
Rich Maribyrnong Plate, at Flemington to-day
(Saturday), would look a fair thing for the Bill of Port-
land—Fishwife filly Fishery', if she could be relied upon
to get well away, which the little flier rarely does. She
failed to do so in the Debutant Stakes, and also in the Gwyn
Nursery, and in the latter, after wiping off the big lead,
came a cropper at the turn. Flemington touts declare
that the first time she moves with the others a swallow
won’t catch her. Another two-year-old in the same race
was Donald, by Wallace from Ilortense, who cost a
thousand guineas as a yearling. Dirk Hammerhand’s full
brother, Troubridge, winner of recent Gwvri Nursery, will
be among the first, and is unquestionably the best colt
Adelaide has produced for a long time. Brakpan is
regarded by many as very good goods for the race on the
strength of his Mona Nursery triumph, and will probably
start favorite. But his brother, Grasspan, also won Mona
Nursery last year, and, although reckoned a “cert.”
before the plate, failed to secure a “ drum.”
* ¥ * *
Monday’s seratchings for the Cup were plenti-
ful. Juggler went out as the result of a broken leg, sus-
tained while doing- a trial spin that morning. Writing
before final forfeits, and making every allowance for
sweep influences, it is probable that not more than 25 will
line up. Taken by and large, the quality of this Cup field
is about the poorest in the history of the race.
* » '* *
“ Tout ” : The halt and the lame are at time of
writing as much in evidence as ever in this Saturday’s
Derby and Tuesday’s Melb. Cup. Turf scribes write of
Ibex as “ under a cloud,” Haymaker is, or was, “ slightly
lame,” Hautvillers looks “ a trifle light,” Bonnie Chiel
(never of robust appearance at best) “ hasn’t got over his
Cauifield Cup gruelling,” and Rock Gun is “badly
affected in the pins.” These are five of the seven left,
which makes it appear as if Grasspan and Manlock will
have the fun to themselves. Past experience of such
things teaches that more than one of the quintette of
cripples will be found very much alive in the race. Ibex
has done little work since winning the Guineas, but that’s
what he seems to thrive on most, while Grasspan is served
best when dealt with the opposite way ; he fairly revels
Jj 1 rd F aft ’ failure to fill a place in Eclipse Stakes
t other day was due to being shut in without a chance
of getting out till too late. Caulfield Cup saw him
finish seventh, but as he was nowhere in the first mile he
must have travelled the rest of the journey at a scorching
pace to get as far up as he did. Manlock is much of a
mystery. Toorak Handicap distance proved too big a
task for him, but he triumphed in the Windsor, which makes
it seem that sprinting is his forte. Given Hautvillers up
to what he was in the Craven Plate at Raridwick, this
writer would like his chance best of all, but if he is below
that standard (as the betting indicates) the winner
should come from Grasspan or Ibex.
* * * *
Wild followers of coincidences in racing are
bound to back George Frederick for the Cup. The son of
Carbine didn’t get his name from the Duke of York-he
got it, in fact, from his former owner, George Frederick
Gray but he’s the namesake of an English Derby winner
that wets named after the child who has since grown into
the little Duke. In the event of G.F. pulling off the big
event, we shall have an awful assortment of Australian
press-comment in various shades of “ loyal ” drivel.
* * ♦ *
Looks as though most of the “double” boobs
will get struck this year, Hymettus having been a
fairly popular first choice. Punters who have achieved
“ one leg in ” with an Ikey of no importance—and very

person who had nothing to lose by the transaction when

.The Betting Ring ”in Australia is a very un-
financial crowd of Ikeys just now, therefore, if the
favorites are fairly successful on the first three days of
V.R.C. meeting, the public will have to whistle for'any-
thing like reasonable odds on the last day. This dearth
of capital among the raucous “ metallicians ” means that
owners of good horses can’t afford to fool about in the
hope of “ bringing off a coup” next time. When there is
a plump stake available for the winner they must go for
it. The size of the stake has never been a more important
consideration than now. “Patience” and “cleverness”
being bad policies under the circumstances, punters had
better close their ears, as a rule, to the whisper of the
“knowing” person who affirms that something or other
didn’t win because “ the money wasn’t right.” Of course
there are exceptions to the rule. One stable scratched
its- horse for Caulfield Cup because the money wasn’t
right. The so-called “ leviathans ” of the ring couldn’t
® e ® fc . heir wa y clear to “ pooling ” a wager of £BOOO to
£IOOO against the ’orse in question, and the “clever
division ” scratched it some days before the race.

One rash tipster advertised before the Caulfield
Cup that his selected horse would be able to “ fall down
during the race and then win.” Others of the humorous
fraternity invited the public to pay a fee of 10s. or ss. for
the name of the animal which was going to “ walk in ”
at a “ long price.” And the race, when it came to pass,
was practically reduced to a match between the first and
second favorites ’ one of them beating the other by half a
“ Tatt.’s ” No. 1 sweep (55.) on Melbourne Cup
shows several possibilities of wonderful luck for syndicate
investments. For instance, 38,918 drew Flagship, 38,957
Hymettus, and 38,977 Silk Lace —a chance of two favorites
and a non-starter for a parcel of 60 consecutive tickets.

“ Lucre ” : Looks as if the winner of Melb. Cup
will come from placed horses (Hymettus, Wakeful, and
Flagship) in Caulfield Cup. Weight of money on first
favorite, Revenue, should cause him to be respected,
especially as he and Wakeful are similarly owned. Reve-
nue hasn’t faced the barrier for a single race since
figuring in last Melb. Cup, when coin went on him from
the same quarter as now and just as freely, but he didn’t
get near scoring then ; why he should do better this
time, in the total absence of public form, is a puzzle.
Australian Colors cau hardly be thought much of since
Saturday’s private go with Khaki, when that nag beat
him pointless. Probably Australian Colors wasn’t in
working mood (he’s often that way lately), but he’d need
to brace up a heap to have a chance on that form. Grass-
pan’s licking in Caulfield Cup wasn’t so bad as it appears.
A photo, of the field turning out of the straight reveals
Grasspan with only five of the 23 behind him, and
those in front well strung out, even at that period.
Grasspan did remarkably well to finish eighth under the
circumstances. Melbourne Cup might bring him better
luck.

Eleven starters for Moonee Valley Cup (11 fur.),
and three fell at the turn into the straight. They didn’t
all fall over one another as the result of one and the same
jostle. Cluster and Gosport fell the first time the field
turned into the straight, after which there was no acci-
dent until the other nine horses arrived at the home turn
for the second time. Then Peeress fell. And yet the
newspapers persist in speaking of the Moonee Valley
circus ring as a racecourse.
* * * •
Obit. : Once forefront jock, Tommy Hales, aged
54. He had won almost every notable Australian event
bar Caulfield Cup, beginning at 13 years of age. From
1872 to 1894, when he rode last, he had ridden 1678 races,
and won 496, finished second in. 332, and third in 195.
Altogether, he had scored 1023 places in 1678 mounts.
Total stakes won by the dead champion were £168,340.
This is an Australian record never even approached by
any other rider. After leaving the saddle, Hales ran a
stud-farm and station in the Riverina, but was not a big
success at breeding. He died (worth much money and
property) of bronchitis and acute congestion of the liver,
on Saturday afternoon, after many years of suffering from
asthma.
• • * *
Jockey Ernest Cubitt, a worthy young’ fellow of
his type, seems to have been pitched into the Yarra and
drowned the other Saturday night, but the inevitable
“suspicions” of the Melbourne police are not likely to
result in the arrest of the murderers. Cubitt won some
money at Caulfield races, and foolishly allowed it to be
known that he was carrying the winnings about with
him when he came up to town in the evening. At mid-
night he left Her Majesty’s Hotel, sober enough, and
started to walk home to Caulfield, taking the Yarra bank
for his shortest cut. This was his usual way when he had
missed the last train. Numerous spielers, touts, &c.,
must have been aware of Cubitt’s risky practice, and
some of the stone-broke fraternity probably went ahead
and waited for the poor devil. Anyhow, Cubitt’s body
was found in the river two days later—his watch had
gone and his pockets were moneyless. He had indeed
taken a short cut home.

At Moorefield, Saturday, TJ.S.A. jockey Ames
further maintained his reputation by landing the “shop ”
and course double (exactly the same as that at Rosehill
two weeks before), and making a good second on Daimore
in the Welter. Ames’ first mount, Portland Light, in the
Flying Handicap, lined-up at a long price in a field of
five, with a steadier of 9.5. The gum-chewer kept his nag
nicely behind till into the straight, when he came like a
flash and pipped favorite (Plain Tom) by a neck, just as
that prad’s backers were starting to collect the money.
Similar navigation won the big race for Glenore even more
easily. Ames has now scored four wins in eight mounts
without the slightest help from handicaps or running.
* * * •
Shooting at N.S.W. National Rifle Association,
last week, was phenomenal and consistent. Possibles
were more plentiful than ever, and records were conse-
quently frequent. A. Carter (Victoria) won “ The Vic-
torian ” (10 shots at 600yds.) with the “ possible ” 50. R.
Wallace was second—one less. First N.S.W. “King’s”
match went to J. T. Lake (5.A.)—228 of a possible 215.
Vagaries of light and wind hampered shooting somewhat,
but this total bears favorable comparison with (if it does
not actually excel) the best ever previously done
in these parts or at ’Ome. South Australian champ.,
Carter, was second (221), and Victorian G. Waugh third
(220) The “New South Wales” (7 shots at 700 and 800
yards) fell to A. C. Wiseheart with 1 less than the possible
70—a record. In “ the Westralian ” (10 shots at 400yds.),
16 possibles were registered. The shoot-off produced
something brilliant. The absolute winner (B. Voight, of
Queensland) notched 31 consecutive bull’s-eyes before
landing the prize ; his target was a curiosity. J. T.
Lake (S.A.) and A. Carter (Vic.) tied for the premiership,
but in the shoot-off (at 900yds.) Carter was successful
with 2 points to the good.
* • * *
The progress of Sydney senior cricket: On
Saturday, Sydney notched a brilliant win against North
Sydney, which closed its innings with eight wickets for
322. The gall of the thing is that Quist—Sydney’s biggest
scorer, with 105—was, for a long time, North Sydney’s
most consistent player. Deery contributed 65 (including
five s’s and seven 4’s), and the necessary runs were com-
piled with five wickets to spare. Sydney’s batting was a
revelation. Previously, it had gone down before Leich-
hardt-Balmain and Paddington, without the slightest
glimmer of Saturday’s top-notch form. Crack wicket-
keeper Kelly topped individual scores with 115 for Pad-
dington against Burwood, and C. Gregory hit up 100 for
Waverley against Glebe. O.G. has now made two centu-
ries, his totals being 57, 150, and 109—the highest aggre-
gate in the grade.
* * * *
Bruiser Denny Moloney collided with Bill
Davis, of Bananaland, at Sydney Golden Gate A.O. on
Saturday night. Davis was wary and wily and showed
a bag of tricks that mystified Denny greatly. However,
Denny never missed a chance to score, and rushed and
floundered around and flung himself at his fleeing
opponent. In a wild rally after a succession of squalls
Davis caught Denny a jolt on the point which spread-
eagled him, but the Irishman came up instanter, shiver-
ing from the bash, and they swirled, and grunted and
slogged round the ring while the audience howled
joyously. At the end of round 16 the Davis crowd
“skied the wipe” and Moloney went round
to collect the cash. Moloney isn’t a boxer,
merely a man-eater who was suckled on bash and
brought up on stoush, and a confirmed bruiser who takes
a smashing like whisky and is right on top of his man all
the time he isn’t on the floor, lie drifts round town with
half-a-yard of bright green handkerchief streaming like a
pennant from his pocket, and his ties are wonderful
creations in every color but orange. He declares he’ll
fight anyone for anything worth carting away, irrespec-
tive of weight, class, rank, hue or odour.
* * * •
The contest between “ Cocker ” Tweedie and
coon Williams for the light-weight supremacy of South
Australia, drew a big house at Adelaide. Only Boz.
difference in the weights, and both men eager, things
hummed from the start. Tweedie was a will-o’-the-wisp
and dodged the negro’s disaster-laden smites at exactly
the psychological moment. Occasionally they started to
mix things, hut Tweedie was always missing when any
danger appeared. In the ninth round Tweedie won, and
told the crowd that he would tarry with them a little
while and “ tap the claret ” of anything of his own weight
available. And the crowd yelled, “ Amen.”
Botha’s troops, the oable states, have again
escaped “in a fog.” Same old fog.
There seems to be a slump in South African
hangings, or else the cable no longer keeps tally
of them. Commandant Seheepers (“ the worst
villain of them all ”) whom the daily papers had
agreed must be hanged, has been sentenced to
banishment only.
Sydney sculptor, James White, had re-
cently on view at his studio the clay model of the
prize group intended for a monument at Perth to
the memory of the Westralian warriors who fell
in S’Africa. Two soldiers are figured ; one stand-
ing grimly with his rifle at the “ ready,” while
from this man’s water-bottle drinks the other,
wounded and half lying on the ground. The
composition is very simple and forceful, and the
modelling as clever as anything Mr. White has
yet done. The group should be a striking one in
the bronze.
A contingenter, returned per Afric, sup-
plies this item to Sydney Sunday Times :
“ The hard-working fighters for the Empire had to go
without food when there was a scarcity, but Boer
prisoners were not allowed to go short.” “ And what was
the result?” “ Well, knowing the silly sentimentality that
prevailed on the subject, the soldiers would rather shoot
the Boers than take them prisoners, because it paid them
better to do so. I remember a party of 50 men being sent
away with 19 Boer prisoners t.o a place some 10 or 12
miles distant, where food was scarce.” “ Did they arrive
all right with the prisoners?” “ Not very much.” “ Why,
what happened ?” “ The Boers were shot on the journey,”
was the grim reply, “and they did not eat any more of
the short supplies.”

Queensland’s own Yes-Noer is the doughty
MacPaterson, who represents Brisbane in the
Federal Parliament. In State politics he was noted
for making fierce speeches in the House against the
Ministry, and clearing out before a division. The
Melb. scribe who classed him as doubtful on the
tariff question knew what he was writing. Pater-
on is doubtful on every question until the cat
jumps.
M‘Lean Bros, and Rigg, the Melbourne iron-
mongery firm, have received an enthusiastic testimonial
t° tj ie efficacy of their “ Registered Key-Chain ” system.
j?" s< fuattah wrote in wonderment to thank them for
se iding along the bunch of keys which he lost in London,
siv months ago, and never hoped to set eyes on again.
Ine chain register is simple enough in theory, but the
practical working of it, as a system of lost-key recovery
bmeans of a 5s. reward, was not brought to its present
perfection at one fell swoop. Time was when M‘ Lean
and Co. merely sold the chains, without recording the
purchaser’s name and address. This led to painful results.
Here and there a dishonest purchaser would fix up a
worthless bunch of keys and claim os. reward for finding
them.
Among all the masses of loose verbiage
and vague unfixableness which Reid and importer
MMVlillan poured out at the Sydney Town Hall
meeting last Monday, the one item that is
definite enough to be identified is that Schweppes,
the soda-water people who represent a cer-
tain amount of absentee capital in Sydney, will
be compelled by the tariff to discharge 200 or 300
hands. And as they don’t employ 200 or 300
hands, or anything like that number, in this
country, this is just the usual Dry-Dog-isin.
Sydney Telegraph, in its issue of 6th
Sept;, published its idea of a tariff—a Freetrade
tariff in accord with its principles. And it
proposed to raise £7,414,000 by customs against
the Barton Government’s £7,388,000 ; and
£8,972,000 by customs and excise against the
Barton Government’s £8,942,000. It also pro-
posed a free list only half the size of the Barton
Government’s free list. But, while taking more
money from the people, that tariff had one advan-
tage tha.t, in the Freetrader’s view, amounted to
everything. It was guaranteed not to aid any
local industry whose products might damage the
importer’s business, or help anybody to obtain
wages or employment. That was the great, un-
speakable beauty which made it precious in the
eyes of the tribe of Calico James. The sinfulness
of providing anybody with work at a local in-
dustry makes Calico dames shudder every time.
Reason why Freetrade papers fight the
Barton tariff so savagely is largely a pocket one.
Sydney D.T., on its “audited circulation,” will
pay £0000 to £8000 a year on printing-paper duty.
S.M. Herald probably as much, or more; the
News a good deal less, but still Something very
substantial. The rage for big sheets has lately cut
into the profits of the Sydney dailies, ' and
with the threat over their heads of having also
to pay postage, no wonder they express a frenzy
of sorrow for the burdens of the poor under a
tariff which will force their proprietaries to
economise a little in cigars and champagne. If
they can’t kill the paper-duty—and the chances
are they can’t—the Sydney dailies will have to cut
down the size of their blankets or reduce dividends
considerably. Melbourne dailies will not pro
portionately be so much affected as they do
not give the public nearly so much waste paper
for a penny.
“ J.D.” : Heard George Reid’s great Free-
trade screech from Sydney Town Hall steps on
Monday night. Quote : “People ask what they
shall do with their boys. Well, my advice is to
treat your boy as you treat a pup : if he won’t go
into the water then churk him in! (Cheers.) And
you’ll find that, as in i!;e case of the pup, after
you’ve chucked him in two or three times,
you will hardly be able to get him out of
the water.” (More cheers.) Why doesn’t someone
correct George Reid’s unnatural history ? Surely
it needs correcting. Writer has coaxed dozens of
pups to go in the water and learn to swim, but
never yet “chucked ” one in without making the
animal afraid of water ever after—even as the
Great Dry Dog himself fears that fluid. The only
time writer remembers the “chucking-in” process
proving even a partial success was when, as a very
small boy, he tried to cast a half-grown Newfound-
land into the Murray and ‘chucked’ himself in
instead. The dog magnanimously waded in and
dragged the biped to shore by the seat of his
little wet pants. N.B.—Mr. Reid is cordially
advised not to emulate this exploit ; his dog isn’t
big enough yet.
Mentioning Barton’s name at the Sydney
Freetrade meeting on Monday, G. H. Reid
always paused for the hoots; and they always
came; it was part of the arrangements.
How the Carey jumps : Sydney “D.T.,”
reporting the big Freetrade demonstration, gives
Solemn Lee four inches in the third person, chippy
Carruthers nearly a column in the first person.
For Solemn Lee'this is the Writins' on the Wall.
On Tuesday evening last, at Sydney Ex-
change, Commander Worcester, of the P. and O.
S.S. India, read an interesting dissertation on the
possibilities of water-conservation in Australia
and of artificial rain-precipitation by concussion
combined with sudden lowering of temperature.
To this end Captain W. advocates the use of car-
bonic anhydride—liquid carbonic acid —in copper
shells containing 501b. of the acid with a bursting-
charge of “tonite,” fired by a Bickford time-fuse.
For the discharge of these shells the use of the
Zalinski pneumatic-gun is suggested ; and, in
view of the extreme Australian importance of the
matter, The Bulletin hopes this theory will soon
be put to practical test.
As to Protection increasing prices. Prior
to 8th October last, mowers, hay-rakes, and ploughs
paid 15 per cent, duty in Victoria, and windmills
25 per cent., whereas in N.S.W. all these were
duty-free. Yet compare Melbourne importers’
catalogues with those of Sydney houses. The
prices for identical implements are in many cases
exactly the same. Apparently, the Melbourne
importer and Yankee exporter pay the duty out
of their own pockets. They can’t pass it on to
the farmer because of the competition of similar
implements made in Melbourne and Ballarat
manufactories. Protection doesn’t increase prices
after the local article gets on the market, but Free-
trade increases middle-men’s profits all the time.
There was no doubt as to the success
of George Reid’s Freetrade demonstration
on Monday at Sydney Town Hall. The Hall
was packed in every sense of the word, and com-
plete arrangements had been made to secure an
altogether harmonious gathering. Freetraders
were admitted free ; others couldn’t get in at any
price, even on the production of a useful inter-
jection. The great crowd had come just to
hear G. H. Reid’s quips and jokes, and made
no pretence of concealing the fact. So soon
as the Freetrade leader had concluded, and
Solemn Lee arose to support a motion in
denunciation of the tariff, the people rushed from
the hall and left Lee to patter, and Carruthers to
chatter to empty benches. Their troubles about
the tariff : they wished to hear more jokes outside.
At the overflow meeting Reid was more successful
than inside. The big hall was too much for his
voice, and, in the effort to be heard, his humor
lost its bubble, and fell somewhat flat. In the open
air he recovered some of his true form. Whilst
Reid was fairly funny, he was quite eclipsed by
John Haynes, who delivered a slashing wigs-on-
the-green speech. Altogether the demonstration
afforded irrefutable proof that the people of Syd-
ney are determined to patronise any humorous
entertainment offered to them free.


Releasing the Upper Richmond.
THE ESTATE OF KYOGLE: A PERFECT
DAIRYING COUNTRY.
As soon as it became evident that the squatty
Kanaka and spindle-legged Hindoo could not
spread the soles of their dirty feet over the rich
lands of the Northern rivers of New South Wales,
many sugar cane growers drifted, perforcely, into
dairy-farming. The move proved to be a paying
one; others followed suit, not out of sympathy
with the White Australia enthusiasm so much as
a desire to chip into an industry where it can be
pursued to profit. Every mother’s son of us is
anxious to follow a calling that promises a mini-
mum of risk with a maximum return for energy
expended and capital invested. Dairying on the
Clarence, Richmond, and Tweed not only pro-
mised this, but fulfilled it right out. Besides
that, it is a little man’s calling and a ready-money
business ; every member of the family, except the
baby, can do a bit—he mostly sucks.
* * * *
A more thriving population doesn’t exist in the
State of New South Wales than in the far
northern rivers districts. Every second man you
meet seems to have a mission to invite you to
drink, if not whisky, then milk—never water or
cold tea. • This generous disposition wells up
from the hearts of the residents because the
farming community, upon whom the trades-
people depend, are pursuing an easy-going branch
o. the agricultural industry. These dairy farmers
THE JERSEY BULL, MAGNET’S PROGRESS III., IN THE HOMESTEAD PADDOCK.
do not gamble on a three years growth of sugar-
cane, or put all their eggs in one wheat or maize
basket, and, perhaps, see the crops go smasli
by a frost or flood ; they do what is infinitely
more reliable and sensible, namely, graze and
otherwise feed pure-bred or grade dairy cows, that
yield milk rich in butter-fat (wice a day.
« • * *
In the old cane-growing, timber-getting days,
butter was made and sent to Sydney for local
consumption. It was packed in tubs and resembled
rusty, streaky bacon, with a flavor and an aroma
that left everything to be desired. Naturally
under those conditions dairy farming did not
expand ; but as soon as the refrigeration system of
butter-making was intro-
duced, and monthly cheques
for the percentage of butter-
fat in the milk paid, then
dairying for the world mar-
ket became an accomplished
fact. The principal district
in the north to make dairy
farming one of the staple
industries of the State was
the Richmond River. First
came the land along the
river banks, next the Big
Scrub, and now the Upper
Richmond—perhaps the best
of it all. Before coming to
the greater Upper Rich-
mond country it will be as
well to note the strides in
dairying on the river and Big
Scrub during the past few
years Tn 1894 the dairy
cdws in the Rous and Rich-
mond district totalled 19,8(54
head, increasing in 1901 to
rather more than 50.000. For
-the year ending 31st March,
1894, only 1,213,705 lbs. of
butter were made, while for
this year, ending 31st March,
the output of butter totalled
<5,50(5,(515 lbs.
* * *
That development has been
made practically without
touching the magnificent
dairying country between
Casino and the Macpherson
Range. The reason simply
being because the land has
been used as big squattages. Noted brands
of horses and beef cattle have roamed the
black soil plains and chocolate ridges ; picturesque
homesteads have dotted the landscapes, if you
' view it through a high-range telescope ; and a few
aboriginals have been able to survive, through the
general conversion of things. This is all very
well from the lordly pastoral standpoint, but it i 3
not putting the best country to the best possible
use; it is not advancing Australia, except to the
crack of a stock-whip. However, the scene is
changing; one of the huge properties, hitherto
noted for its Devons, Durhams, and blood horses,
is now about to be partly transformed into homes
for white men.
* * • •
The estate that we allude to is that of Kyogle,
so named because the plains turkey (aboriginal
name for the bird) abounded on the run This
property comprises upwards of 42,000 acres, is of
basaltic origin, and carries a soil fit to grow any-
thing without manure from now to the end of the
century. It is no exaggeration to say that the
soil is equal to that of the Darling Downs,
Queensland, or the Big Scrub, while it has a better
rainfall and milder winter than the one, and no
clearing to engage in before getting a return, as in
the other. This estate starts about 14 miles from
Casino, at the north-western end of Runnymede,
and it is here that the sub division is to be made.
The Richmond River runs through it; there
are treeless, black-soil plains and open forest
ridges, lightly timbered; and the surveys have
been so made that every farm site has a river
frontage, and a portion of both plain and ridge.
The winters on Kyogle are short and mild; the
rainfall is good—averaging 44in. to 48in. per year,
spread over 200 days—and there is a general
absence of hot northerly winds in the summer.
When the pioneers went into the Upper Rich-
mond country in the early days they knew what
they were about. So, too, did Mr. C. C. Lance,
when he spied out this land for dairying. It
would be fit for Moses to lead the people of
Israel to, and they are not inclined to go any-
where that does not promise to flow with milk and
honey.
* * * *
Kyogle extends fully 14 miles along the course
of the river. As you enter the estate at the
Runnymede end you pass over Brummy’s Plain —
a pasture void of trees and weeds and thick of
grass ; the only stones that are to he found are on
the road, and they had to be carted from ——
knows where. Off the road and you are on to the
garden—the garden of the State. There is a good-
sized creek and a fine lagoon—besides the river, on
BRIDGE OVER FAWCETT’S CREEK, WITH CARTS RETURNING FROM KYOGLE CREAMERY.
Brummy’s Plain; the plain is broken here and
there by gentle ridges, lightly timbered, but no
brush. Six miles on Kyogle from Runny-
mede is situated the creamery, on the banks
of Fawcett’s Creek; overlooking the creamery
is the homestead, nestling under Fern-
mount; the hill that gave the name to the
property before it was called Kyogle.
When you are there you have a panorama of rich
country, both for fertility and natural beauty
equal to anything anywhere this side of Paradise.
Behind you is Brummy’s Plain and the river, with
its creeks and lagoon ; ahead of you is seven miles
of Kyogle Plain, with the river, creeks, and gras3 ;
while to the left are the farms and cultivation-
bespeaking the richness of the soil and the mellow-
ness of the climate. Why, in July and August,
stiff winter months in most parts of the Common-
wealth, oats for green feed were three to four feet
high ! No wonder, therefore, that Mr. R. -T.
Cullen averaged 14s. 6d. per cow of 70 head during
July, and £2 each for six-months’ pigs—sold on
his farm to Queensland buyers. Mr. Cullen was
manager of Kyogle Creamery, but he looked at
the soil, his rising family, and the advantage of
milking cows for himself in preference to separat-
ing milk for his employers, so he took on a herd of
Kyogle station-bred cows and heifers from Mr.
Smith, the former proprietor, and has been in a
good frame of mind since.
Kyogle sub-division, comprising about 8000
acres, is to be offered in lots of from 120 to 200
acres at the School of Arts, Casino, on the 28th
November next. The auctioneer is Mr. J. B.
M'Dougall, in conjunction with Messrs. Hind-
marsh and Dunn, Lismore. Mr. Dow, ex-Minister
for Agriculture, Victoria, who recently paid a
visit to the Clarence and Richmond Rivers,
writing on the estate in the Leader,
said that if a person blindfolded were
to take a map of the sub-division and stick
a pin anywhere on it, not a prick could be made
on a farm site that was not as good as
any land he had seen, and each site
equal to the other. The property, for the
time being, is worked partly for beef-cattie
grazing and dairying. As the dairy herd grows,
so, doubtless, the- pint-pot brand of Devons and
Durhams will diminish. The owners have already
imported nearly 1000 head of dairy stock, from
the Bega and other parts of the south coast
districts. The bulls are to be available for the
farmers on the estate, 24 of which are Jerseys and
two milking strain short-
horns, specially selected
by Mr. A. L. Manning,
Bega.
• * •
There is a plentiful
supply of good timber on
Kyogle for fencing and
other purposes ; a sawmill
is to be erected, so settle-
ment should not prove
very costly. Blood-wood,
tallow - wood, red - gum,
spotted-gum, and similar
woods abound; also, a
sprinkling of cedar and pine. The roads are good,
and the State Government is so appreciative of
the district for permanent settlement that a sur-
vey for the extension of the Lismore-Casino
line from Casino towards the tablelands is
ordered. At present there is one creamery at
Kyogle, but as it is at Fawcett’s Creek, between
three and six miles of the subdivision, a new one
is to be erected ; there are already three dairy
farms in full swing, and four others in course of
equipment. Two of the dairymen there are from
the South Coast, and of the four others about to
establish themselves two are from South Aus-
tralia. The great advantage of settlement is, of
course, the fact that the stock have a wave of kan-
garoo and couch grass to feed on the moment, they
arrive; no waiting to clear, burn-off, and the
grass and fodder crops to be sown and grown
before stock can be put on and got into profit.
Good plains, good ridges, good rainfall, good
river, good creeks, good timber, good roads only
need good men and good stock to make the Upper
Richmond advance Australia at electric motor-car
pace.
* * * *
The sub division provides for between 50 to 60
farms, absorbing from 7000 to 8000 acres, and is
open to inspection at any time, by anyone,
whether intending purchasers or not. Those who
wish to view the farm sites are invited to do so, as
a man is specially told-off to facilitate inspection
in every way. Really, the country is worth a
visit without a view to settlement, for it is cer-
tainly one of the most beautiful parts, not only of
New South Wales, but Australia. Those pro-
ceeding by boat should disembark at Coraki,
and proceed from thence to Casino, where
arrangements for specially taking visitors to
Kyogle have been made. A coach runs daily from
Coraki to Casino; also a daily coach plys be-
tween Lismore and Casino. The new railway from
Lismore to Casino will speedily alter that mode of
locomotion. Coaches also run daily from Tenter-
field to Casino. The sub-division is 14 miles from
Casino, and the road out gives an excellent idea
of the country. Any information concerning the
auction to be held on Thursday, November 28,
will be gladly furnished by Mr. H. W. Chambers
(secretary), the Estate of Kyogle Office, Royal
Exchange, Bridge-street, Sydney; Mr. M'Dougal,
Casino ; Messrs. Hindmarsh and Dunn, Lismore ;
or Mr. Alex. Campbell (manager), Kyogle, Upper
Richmond River district.
CATTLE GRAZING ON KYOGLE PLAIN, MACPUERSON RANGE IN THE DISTANCE, AS VIEWED FROM KYOGLE HOMESTEAD.

What THE PRESS Says.
Sydney Morning Herald.
“We have no hesitation in
saying that the production
is not only a credit to Mr.
Rowlandson, the publisher,
or to the writers whose aid
he has enlisted, but that it
is an achievement in illus-
trated periodical literature
which would do
credit in every way
to a much older and
larger community
than ours. In fine,
The Commonwealth
Annual is heartily
to be commended,
not only because it
is Australian, but
because it shows
good work all
through.”
Sydney Daily Tele-
graph.
“ The Common-
wealth is the best
shilling’s worth of
its kind that has yet
been offered to the
Australian public.”
Bulletin.
The Common-
wealth is a creditable attempt to win popular favor, many
well-known Australian writers and draughtsmen are
represented; the process engraving is noticeably good,
and, taken all round, The Commonwealth is a splendid
shillingsworth.”
Sydney Mail.
“ The best shilling’s worth in the way of magazine or
annual yet produced in Australia, comparing favorably
with the efforts of American and English publishers. The
result is in every sense a credit to all concerned, and the
enterprising promoter will have general good wishes as to
the success he has boldly bidden for, and in seeking which
he has evidently spared neither labor nor expense.”
Town and Country Journal.
“ Our people want educating up to their magazine, and
Mr. Rowlandson has wisely determined to make a reputa-
tion with his wonderful shilling’s worth of stories and
pictures before venturing into the deep waters that have
hitherto beset the monthly. It has been shown in The
Commonwealth Annual how possible it is to produce a
local article that, while equal to the imported one in every
respect of literary work, of letterpress and illustrations,
is at the same time double the size. It is hoped that, in
this case, The Commonwealth will be bought to read and
keep as well as to be posted abroad.”
Freeman’s Journal.
“ We regard The Commonwealth as far and away the
finest illustrated magazine every presented to the Aus-
tralian public. In every way it is racy of the soil —as in
art, literature and mechanical get-up it is worthy.”
Evening News.
“ Far and away the best publication of its kind as yet
been published in Australia. Essentially and truly Aus-
tralian, and is a thoroughly creditable Australian pro-
duction in every respect.”
The Age (Melbourne).
“ The idea which has promoted the issue of this hand-
somely got-up, excellently printed, and literary meri-
torious publication by the N.S.W. Bookstall Co., is a
praiseworthy one. The selection of sketches, stories,
verses, and essays, is sufficiently varied to attract all
classes of readers.”
The Argus (Melbourne).
“ The N S.W. Bookstall Co. now publish an illustrated
annual, The Commonwealth, which makes a bold bid for
support. The verses of Roderic Quinn and Arthur H.
Adams are all of superior merit. Only praise can be
accorded to the many reproductions of the fine pictures
of the Sydney Art Gallery.”
And Hundreds of Others, all Highly Praising.

DRUNKENNESS.
The Eucrasy Company, of Sydney, extracts from whose
hook on the disease of drunkenness have been published
by this paper from time to time, have lately issued the
third edition of their pamphlet, explaining the action of
Eucrasy, and pointing out their method of curing those
addicted to the liquor habit.
The Eucrasy cure is one of the oldest, having been
before the public since 1882, at which time sanatoriums
were in successful operation in many cities in America,
and the succees of their remedy is shown by hundreds of
grateful patients all over the world.
A well-known merchant, residing at Newcastle, says
“ I have much pleasure in testifying to the excellent
qualities of Eucrasy. I felt even after the first dose a
different person, and may say now that lam cured. The
craving has entirely gone, and I can with full confidence
recommend it to anyone, no matter what hold the liquor
habit may have of them.
“Thanking you sincerely for your kindness, I am,
yours faithfully,
Any reader of The Bulletin can obtain this book free
by addressing THE EUCRASY CO., 62 Hunter Street,
Sydney.

Concerning an old familiar figure of
George-street, Sydney:—
“ Mother Fivebob ”is dead. She used to sit near the
Royal Hotel selling matches, and her cry in a thin, high-
pitched voice: “Two boxes a penny ’ere—a penny a
box!” was well known. A cheery old party, with an
affable “ Good day, me son 1” to anyone addressing her,
she had violent prejudices on some points, and any
derogatory remarks as to Mother Church were resented
fiercely. St. Patrick’s Day was her great festival, when,
doffing the familiar hood and apron, she came out
resplendent in green silk from top to toe. She left a
neat little sum behind, which goes to the church of which
she was such a fervid adherent
Editor Bulletin.—London Daily Mail
quotes you as saying that General Elliott, who
defended Gibraltar against the French and
Spanish, “ was, by a queer chance, the best de-
fensive engineer in Europe.” But, considering
that Elliot was originally an artillery officer,
educated at the R.M.A., Woolwich, there was no
chance about the matter. Generals Lawrence and
Wilson, who respectively defended Lucknow and
Delhi, had also been artillery officers —as well as
Lord Roberts —to say nothing of one Napoleon
Buonaparte. True, in the British Army an
artillery officer rarely gets the chance of an im-
portant command, but, when he does, he generally
comes out on top. —Palman, Q.M.F.

WHY WE ADVERTISE!
Because Advertising is the legitimate means vouchsafed to enlighten people,
to acquaint each other with their avocations and wants. If a man has a
Speciality, and has devoted years of his life and expended large sums of money in studying that Speciality and in
purchasing the necessary appliances, etc., for treating certain diseases, it would be absurd for him not to announce
the fact, and disseminate the knowledge universally, We have no apology to offer for informing the world through
this advertisement and otherwise of the means of relief we can afford to sufferers who need our help. Therefore,
however opposed to your own preconceived ideas and to those of your medical advisers, our method of treatment
may be, the fact still remains that it is successful, and this is verified by the thousands of testimonials received from
grateful patients. We make no secret of our mode of practice. We only claim to have utilised in a common-sense
way the result of scientific research and long experience. If you listen to the counsel of your sceptical friends and
jealous physician you may omit consulting us, but remember if you allow the disease to run riot in your system, you
are guilty of culpable neglect, and are alone responsible for your future pitiful condition.
Nine Diplomas and Certificates of Qualification and Registration by Government
Medical Boards of New South Wales, Victoria, South Australia, Great Britain
and America Open to Inspection by Dr. Wallace, our Physician-in-Chief.
Late Consulting: Staff, Homcepathic Hospital, Melbourne.
Late Medical Staff, British Army.
HOME TREATMENT,
-If you live out of town and cannot visit us, this need not deprive
you of the benefits of our wonderful treatment, because in the new
system of combining medicine and electricity a complete Electro-Medical home treatment is provided for. Scarcely a
day passes that the news does not reach us of some wonderful home cure of a person living so far away that a per-
sonal visit could not be made. Write a plain statement of your condition, explaining your feelings and symptoms as
clearly as possible, and from it we will be able to accurately diagnose your case. It only costs you the trouble of
writing, and twopence postage to get the opinion and advice of Australia’s Leading Specialists.
DO NOT BE DRUGGED TO DEATH BY QUACKS
When there are so many quacks and impudent pretenders in the field of Medicine, who arrogate to themselves
the monstrosity of giving counsel and advice in matters of which they are profoundly ignorant, and who have
nothing to recommend them but their audacity, sufferers cannot be too careful whom they consult. Although
liberty of choice belongs to all, discrimination is necessary. In consulting THE FREEMAN AND WALLACE
ELECTRO-MEDICAL AND SURGICAL INSTITUTE patients may feel assured that they shall receive the advice
of those fitted by superior skill and education, the result of special study. Our reputation and unparalleled
success as Specialists entitle us to your confidence.

Forty Years in Queensland.
'
Everybody Knows, and Everybody will Believe
MR. JACOB PEAREN’S NARRATIVE.
(A reporter’s special visit.)
Hushed in silence so delightfully soothing
to the city business man, the charming sur-
roundings of Humpybong a sea side
pleasure resort near Brisbane seems to
possess every fascination to beguile the
senses of the visitor. It was at VN oody
Point that a reporter halted at the gate
which led to a house called Bay View, and
on looking into the garden he espied- a
robust old gentleman, with sunburnt face
and brown muscular arms, busily engaged
in digging.
“Are you Mr. Pearen?” asked the
scribe, with an inward feeling that he could
not possibly be the man whom he sought.
“ Yes; Jacob Pearen is my name. I
thought everybody around Brisbane knew
me, for I have been living in this btate
close on forty years. In Gyrnpie, too, I am
well known, having gone there with the
great gold rush in ’67- lam doing a bit of
gardening, you see, and I am very thankful I
am able to do it. About this time three
years ago I was lying in a helpless condition
with a plaster on my back.”
“ So I believe, and I hear that your suffer-
ings extended over a long period.”
You are quite right. I was working in
the garden one day, apparently in good
hea tli, when I was suddenly seized with a
terrible pain in the small of my back, which
almost paralysed me. When I had partly
recovered from the first shock I took the
garden rake and put it under my arm to
help myself indoors. It took me all my
time to reach the house, and when I did get
there I did not stir out agaiu for several
days I had medicines and lotions from a
chemist, but the pain did not ease off at all.
I became so ill that I had to go to Brisbane,
where I was laid up for two months, receiv-
ing medical attention all the time. When
the medicine from one doctor did not do
any good my friends said : ‘ So-and-so is a
good man ; try him.’ And I went on in
that way till four doctors had handled me,
and I did not feel any better for their treat-
ment, so I was brought home again. I
started taking chlorodyne till I could take
sixty or seventy drops, but all the comfort
I got only lasted a quarter of an hour, and
then the agony came on again with greater
violence. Every physic I tried was worse
than useless, for between them my internal
organs were frightfully deranged. Twelve
mouths last June I went over to my
daughter’s place. It was a cold, drizzly
morning, and the doctor said I had no right
to go out. His words proved only too true,
as in a few days afterwards I got terrible
pains in the hips, and my medical adviser
said it was sciatica, which I had contracted
through catching/jold. The most excruciat-
ing agonies used to flit from one hip to the
other day after day, and then the pains ex-
tended right down both legs to my feet. I
was a perfect cripple, being quite unable to
stand or move my limbs. A rope was sus-
pended over my bed so that I could l’aise
myself up, but when I wanted to turn I had
to get somebody to help me. Some days I
was as helpless as a baby, and had to be fed
with a spoon whilst lying in bed.”
“ Were you able to digest your food, Mr.
Pearen 1 ”
“ I could net digest even the lightest of
diet. My che3t was so heavily compressed
that I could not breath properly, and closely
following the taking of food came acute
pains in the stomach. Then I used to vomit
the food, and I felt a little easier, but this
meant losing strength. I lost nearly three
stone in weight, and my face was quite
pinched and haggard. My mouth was
clogged up with a nasty stuff which made
my breath very disagreeable, and there
were such horrible dizzy sensations in my
head that the ceiling seemed to be spinning
round. I had very little sleep at any time,
but at one period I did not sleep a wink for
three weeks, and at the end of that stretch
my nerves were completely shattered.
There is no doubt that I was at death s
door, and nobody thought I could possibly
recover. Friends used to say : ‘ Poor old
Pearen is dying ; the doctors say he cannot
last much longer ; ’ and so it seemed that 1
was on my last journey. One of my
physicians said I must go into a hospital;
but I said I will go and die in my own
house ;so I came home. I was yelling out
with pain one day when a lady came rushing
into my room, saying she could hear me in
the opposite paddock. ‘ I cannot help it,
I said, ‘I am in such awful agony.’ ‘ Hid
you ever try Clements I onic i ’ she asked.
‘ I don’t know anything about it,’ I replied ;
‘ but I suppose it is the same as quack
medicines ? ’ Not at all,’ she said.
‘Clements Tonic cured a fiiend of mine
when she was nearly dead,’ and then the
lady gave me particulars of her friend s
cure. That day 1 sent to Brisbane for a
bottle of Clements Tonic, which was for-
warded by next mail coach. After drinking
three-parts of the bottle I fancied a smoke
a luxury I had toabandon when I was taken ill
—and from that ve y hour I made such st eady
progress that I have been smoking my pipe
ever since. I sent for more Clements lonic,
which carried me further on the road to
health every day. The lumbago and sciatic
pains were less tormenting, and all the
aches and agonies in my body subsided.
My nerves were completely steady when 1
had taken a few bottles of Clements lonic,
which gave me calm sleep every night, and
an appetite for my food which I had not
known for many a year. My mouth and
breath became clean and sweet, constipa-
tion troubles were overcome, and my food
was never again vomited. Clements lonic
made iny digestive organs healthy, for the
distressing symptoms of that complaint
were entirely removed, and the people
round here said they could see me putting
on flesh every week. I was so far gone that
it required a fair amount of perseverance
with Clements Tonic to bring me back to
life, but I stuck to it till my diseases were
thoroughly cured and my health and
strength iestoied to the degree of perfection
which enables me to do this heavy work with-
out feeling any inconvenience. My friends all
expected me to die, and I'm sure that if I
had gone to the hospital I would have been
dead long ago, because I would never have
heard of Clements Tonic there, but which
actually saved my life. You are at liberty
to publish these facts in every paper, and
pamphlet in the universe as you plea-c.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Jacob Pbarkn, of “Bay View," Woody Point,
Brisbane, in the State of Queensland, do solemnly
md sincere y declare as follows : —That I have
jarefully read the annexed document, consist-
ng of three folios and consecutively numbered from
one to three, and that it contains and is a true ana
aithful account of my illness and cure by Clements
Conic and also contains my full permission to publish
n any manner my statements which I give volun-
larily, and without receiving any paj ment; and I make
;his solemn declaration conscientiously believing the
same to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of
die Oaths Act 1867.

Dee Bubleteen : Whaffoh shuttem out
Chinaman by poll tax ? White man no glo
vegetlable; only Chinaman glo ’em. Shuttem
out Chinee labourla, gleat vegetlable industlee

Daily paper item :
Chew Moy, storekeeper, of West Maitland, stated that
he never had a naturalisation certificate. He never paid
poll-tax, as he landed at Brisbane and came into N.S.W
over the border. Tin Yow, the bankrupt, said that he
always had two names ; Chong- Beong was his birth name,
given him by his mother ; Tin Yow was the name his
father gave him. He denied having bought his certificate
of naturalisation. Another Chinese obtained it for him.
David Oyoung, partner in Hop War and Co., said that his
Chinese name was Oyoung King. The English called him
David Oyoung. He was married to a Chinese woman.
He had not a certificate of naturalisation, but a friend of
his gave him one a long time ago. He had used Ah
Cheong’s since then. He had used Ah Bum’s certificate
of naturalisation. Ah Bum arranged to bring his wife
from China, and sent his certificate of naturalisa-
tion. He understood that Ah turn’s wife was
too ill to travel, and the agent sent witness’s wife out on
Ah Bum’s certificate. His wife came to this State as Mrs.
Ah Bum. After his wife arrived, he gave the certificate
hack to Ah Bum. The real Mrs. Ah Bum came to Sydney
about three years ago. She got in on Chew Moy’s cer-
tificate. Chew Moy came down from Maitland and landed
Mrs. Ah Bum as his wife. Chew Moy was married, and
his wife was in China. Chew Moy was going to get his
wife out, but his mother would not let her come.
And it is this evasive kind of person, who has
two names of his own naturally, and is commonly
called by a third one, and often trades under a
fourth, and doesn’t mind if he is called by ten
more, and who looks exactly like 100,000,000 of his
countrymen, all with two names each, and most
of the names Hop or Fat or Wow—this party who
is as undefinable as one prawn in an ocean—con-
cerning whom Vic. Chief Secretary Trenwith pro-
poses to take strong measures to prevent persona-
tion. Trenwith is going to stand on the shore, in
a manner of speaking, and not allow any one
prawn to pass itself off dishonestly as another
