The Derelict.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At 3 o’oloek on Sunday mosninf Casey, ®
Flinders-laue doorway, besought the tud of the
constable who had found him sheltering from the
rain, to tell him when the first issue of the
morning papers would be out. §
“You better get home, old man, began the
constable.
“ That’s wot I want ter do,” gulped Casey.
“ Well, run along then. Where d’ yer live? ’ ,
Casey shook his head, dolefully. “Dont
j cnow _that’s w’y I wanted ter advertise. W’ere s
th’paper place?” „
“ Oh, rot! —you’re not so bad as all that.
“Ye’sh, I am,” protested Casey; “I’ve bin
tailin’ coppers ’bout losin’ me home an’ fam’ly all
ni’—an’ none of ’em ’ll believe me.”
“You’ve lost yer mem’ry,” grinned the con-
stable
“No—me home. All gone like smoke!
wailed Casey, miserably, as they both backed to
the wall to escape another shower.
“ When did yer lose ’em ? ”
“ I didn’t lose ’em—they just went.”
The policeman laughed, and another bulky form
tramped up from somewhere and, after getting a
short explanation, joined-in. Casey shrank
further into the shadow and listened until they
both began to question him.
“How did it all start, Casey?” inquired the
sergeant, after a highly-diverting cross-examina-
tion ; “tell us th’ whole yarn, an’ p’raps we can
help yer. ”
“I left ’ome in Richmond—Sat’d’y mornin’,”
commenced Casey, wearily. “ Got back’bout one
this mornin’ an’ th’ ’ouse was empty—family all
fone, an’ nothin’ but a dead cat in th’ front garden.
knocked-up th’ man next door an’ asked ’im
w’ere me wife was, an’ he tole me t’ go t’ blazes
an’ slammed down th’ winder. Then I threw
stones at th’ front door o’ th’ man on th’ other
side, an’ w’en ’e poked ’is ’ed out I asked ’im fer
th’ loan of ’is spade t’ bury th’ cat, an’ did ’e know
w’ere me wife was ? ’E tole me ter go some place
I didn’t ’ear, an’ w’en I asked ’im ’nother time ’e
tole me t’ go ’way or I’d be killed. Then I started
lookin’ fer a paper place, an’ I bin talkin’ t’ silly
coppers all ni’. Wot’s th’ day now ? ” he sighed,
drowsily.
“Sunday.”
Casey moaned. “Got t’ wait f* th’ Monday
papers ! —too long. Wot’ll I do ?”
“ You see if yer can get a cab to take ’m out,”
the sergeant instructed the constable, and resumed
his beat.
“Come on, Casey,” commanded the constable,
catching him by the arm through force of habit.
- Casey woke with a smothered yell. “ Oh-h,
don’ run me in, copper—ole boy !” he entreated.
“ Shut-up—l’m only lookin’ fer a cab t’ take yer
home.”
“ Got none !” sighed Casey, hopelessly.
“Yes, y’ave—somewhere.” The policeman
linked his arm through that of his prize and
trotted him along.
“ I don’ know where it is.”
“Never mind,” murmured the hopeful con-
stable, towing strongly through the rain-mist.
Just then a cab trotted slowly up from the
direction of St. Kilda, and was immediately hailed
by Casey’s appointed guardian. After a short
parley and the jingling of silver, which Casey un-
earthed from the depths of his trouser pockets, he
was assisted into the cab, which immediately
drove off towards Richmond.
The constable stood on the kerb in the dawn
and laughed.
• 4i * *
About daybreak the cabby pulled up in a dingy
back-street of Richmond, and, after waking up his
sleepy fare, helped him to the kerb, and drove off,
leaving the silent street to Casey and the dissi-
pated cats, scurrying home from their midnight
revels.
Casey, the bereaved, at last arose and wandered
up the street to his aforetime happy home. Then
he clung on to his own front fence and cried aloud
at the utter desolation of it, oalling the heavens to
witness that he had been a kind husband and
father. Then he broke down altogether, weeping
unrestrainedly on the threshold, for it was as yet
an unearthly hour, and the street was like a dead
street in a dead city.
When he had recovered somewhat, he wandered
aimlessly round the empty house, like an idle
ghost with plenty of spare time on its impalpable
hands. Finally, he crawled into the fowl-house,
and there, under the empty roost, sank to his
unhonored slumbers.
4i 9 * *
He was awakened by the sound of a shrill, angry
voice. After listening sleepily for a while, he
peered through a crack, and beheld his lost wife
declaiming to the sympathetic woman next door.
“ Yes —I ’spose ’e’s in th’ lock-up. ’Corse —as
you know—Casey an’ me ’ad fixed up ter move
next Monday. Well, th’ vanman come t’ me on
Sat’dy—after Casey ’ad gone t’ work, yer under-
stand—an’ ’e said, if we didn’t move then, we
couldn’t be moved fer ’nother week —so I moved.
I waited ’ere fer Casey all yesterd’y, upter 11 larst
night, but ’e never come. You just wait till I
catch ’im / ” she snapped, viciously, and the
woman next door nodded approval, and chimed-in.
And Casey, prostrate in the darkest corner of
the fowl-house, remembered things, and listened
quietly, until he screwed up courage to wriggle
out through a hole into the lane, and fled.
• • • •
A friend of his met Casey, the other day, in
town, looking unkempt and miserable. Casey
produced a newspaper, and pointed out a para-
graph, in which he was described as “Missing.”
Asked for an explanation, Casey, after exacting
an oath of secrecy, told his story.
“ W’y don’t yer go ’ome now ? ”
“Don’t know where it is, an’ wouldn’t go if I
knew.”
“ But th’ p’lice ’ll grab yer any minit now,
CftSGy
Casey’s face lit up with a look of dawning peace.
“I’m just goin’ V give meself up,” he smiled.
“ I won’t go near my wife without a big copper
with me. Comeanavadrink ! ”
He was last seen trying to surrender himself to
a busy constable, who was wearing a cold gleam
of suspicion in his official eye, and didn’t appear
to be paying much attention,
v.
KODAK.
Lewis Litton addresses to Sydney City
Council a valuable memorial on road-paving. He
contends that the essentials of “ metal ” paving
are a strong foundation, a fresh (not decomposed)
stone, and a hard and clean superstructure, using
both large and small metal, and (in Sydney) with-
out clay binding. As a practical man of 34 years
experience, his views are well worth attention.
A Tale of Two Churches.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They had both oome away from the rich man’s
table feeling unsatisfied. He was a clerk in the
rich man’s office, and she was the rich man’s
favorite daughter. The moon stared down
whitely at herself in the water, and the wavelets
lapped lazily shorewards.
“ Before next month’s moon shines in that water
you will be his wife,” said the clerk, bitterly.
She sighed a sigh of soft, pleasant sorrow.
“If I had only dreamt that I should have
known you,” she said, “ I should never have been
engaged to him. Now, honor demands that I
shall marry ham. He has been true to me for a
whole voyage to England and back.”
“ But he is not back yet,” said the clerk.
“ But he will be—this week.”
The clerk leaned forward and whispered some-
thing. ,
“John ! ” murmured the maiden shrinking.
He whispered again—a little longer this time.
“No, no, no /” she replied.
He only smiled. He was sure of his victory.
Voices came down from the portico—the rich
man’s voice and others. The clerk hurried fhe
last part of his whisper: “St. Michael’s churoh,
and go straight in ! ” he said.
“ Straight in ! —oh, John ! ”
“Darling!—Half-past two, Wednesday after-
noon, and—St. Michael’s church. Darling ! ”
• • • a
Wednesday afternoon came, sulky and blustery.
Kitty donned a “ travelling costume ” of grey and
a large feather. Also, she could not resist bring-
ing two girl friends, who wore fawn and carried
handkerchiefs. They started out ten minutes
late, eager for the bride to observe girlhood’s
privilege to the last.
“It seems bold,” they said, “for the bride to be
quite to time.” Then, being women, they hurried
all the way and arrived to the minute. It vexed
Kitty. “ I should so like to have been a weeny
bit late,” she said.
“ It does look nice for the man to be waiting,
silent and glum and almost desperate,” they
agreed. So they waited in the porch for five
minutes.
Then Kitty took the door-handle into her hands,
and immediately began to tremble. Up the aisle
they went, the bride’s eyes drooping ground-
wards. Near the altar she looked up. There was
an atmosphere of dust, an old woman, and a
broom. The pale, impatient bridegroom was not
visible. Kitty gasped. “Well, I neverl” said
her friends.
Kitty gasped again. “It was three he said,”
she told them quickly, wishing she had not
brought her friends.
“So, after all, we shall be first,” they said.
They all sat down in the second front pew and
waited. Kitty’s face went from white to red and
back again many times, and not one change was
lost on her friends.
“If he is not here at three sharp,” the
bride said, grudging the half-hour’s grace she was
granting, “we will not wait. We all have shopping
to do.”
• * * *
The clerk went straight to St. Michael’s.
Going, he cast a glance over to St. Stephen’s.
The doors were open.
“Another wedding, I’ll be bound,” he re-
marked.
He took the church aisle grimly. It wanted
five minutes to half-past two. There was a small
christening party in front of him. Two or three
godmothers, a father and mother, and a long
sweep of white robe. He had to stand while a
speck of cambric and lace was arranged over a
small red face.
“It’s a wedding,” said a godmother, looking at
the clerk.
The words passed on. They all looked at him,
and they all whispered : “ It’s a wedding.” Then
some one said, “ Let’s wait.” And they all sat
down in the front pew.
The clerk stood at the altar, and put his hands
behind him. At 20 minutes to three he folded them
across his chest. At a quarter to three he dropped
them to his sides. His face was quite white, and
a Byronic corsair smile ran across his lips once or
twice.
The clergyman, white-robed and waiting, sat in
a high-backed chair with his eyes on some lost
sheep on a window. At last even the baby grew
impatient. He screamed, and the sound brought
the cold perspiration to the clerk’s brow. It was
ten minutes to three. He counted up to a hun-
dred, said the Lord’s Prayer through, and turned
his thoughts into the matrimonial service.
The clergyman rose and retired to the vestry.
John’s eyes went wildly to the lost sheep. He
folded his hands behind him and wished he might
whistle —or say something. When three struck,
the christening-party gathered themselves to-
gether. “Poor man ! ” they said, examining the
clerk’s back.
At ten minutes past three he wished he might
smoke, at a quarter past he turned sideways on
the christening-party, and at twenty past he
strode out of the church.
“Poor fellow! ’’they said. In the church porch he
stood still and lit a cigar. Then he went into town.
Half-an-hour later he met her. She. held two tiny
tissue-paper parcels in one hand and her lips were
evidently saying gay things to two giddy little girls
in fawn.
How was he to know they were bridesmaids
mulct of a bride? He blew out some smoke and
looked his bitterest. Then he strode onward down
the street.
It was not till two months later that Kitty
learned which was St. Stephen’s and which St.
Michael’s edifice. She was a bride then, and her
bridegroom, whose faith had been tried for the
term of a voyage to England and back, had chosen
St. Stephens church for the ceremony. It was
too late then for her to do anything, but forget.
N.S.W.
LILLIAN TURNER.
Trouble has arisen in Maoriland over the
question of the general-store employees’ wages.
The Grocers’ and Drapers’ Union has each a
minimum wage, and the boot-shop assistant
has another minimum figure. In the country the
general-storekeeper’s assistant sells everything,
and the employers want to put him on thq, lowest
scale, while the employees demand to be placed
on the highest. The remedy is a special classifica-
tion for the general-storekeeper’s assistant.
Ernest Favenc, writing in Sydney Daily
Telegraph about matters geographical, points
out that in Gordon’s “Grammar of Geography,”
published about 1750, the map of North America
gives the name New South Wales to the region
south of Hudson Bay, as distinguished from
“ New North Wales ” —the territory north of the
bay. Thus Australia is not even original in the
f absurd designation of its own Mother State.

Night-Waves.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Waves of the Dawn sweep creaming
Thrill’d through with a golden song ;
The Waves of the Noon lie dreaming
The shimmering coasts along ;
And swift in the black Pelorus
The tides thro’ the rock-race flow :
But wild is the wailing chorus
That rings where the Night- Waves go.
The Waves of the Dawn are, lovers
A-kiss as they swing and roll;
But Waves of the Night are rovers
That come to our ports for toll—
Off-shore where the channel blackens
They tramp and I watch them go,
And never their quickstep slackens,
And never a wave swings slow :
To Nor’ard their army reaches
And under the Western stars
It growls on the silver beaches,
And staggers across the bars.
The bays that were darkly sleeping
Churn white when the Night-Waves pass
A-roar, with their vanguard leaping
And Death in their close-packed mass.
The tread of the burdened bearers—
The grief of the March in Saul-
Black robes and the pallid wearers—
A coffin—a dead-black pall—
I see and hear ; and I fear them—
These songs that the Ocean’s soul
Wails out, that the men who hear them
May know that the Night-Waves roll.
They march to the North and Westward,
And shuddering to their blast
The fisherman’s wife clasps breastward
Her child, till their song is passed.
The crash of their music deadens
The cry of a mangled foe,
And ever a blood-trail reddens
The path where the Night-Waves go
To pillage and loot and ravage,
And scream at a harbour-light—
For cruel are they and savage,
Yet beautiful in their might.
I saw a light to the Nor’ward—
Full white like the Star of Day,
But Waves of the Night leaped forward
And threatened the star away.
I heard a song to the South’ard—
But, lifting their grey manes old,
The Wolves of the Darkness smothered
Its tune as their ranks patrolled
•The seas in their search for plunder—
A murderous, ruthless crew ;
They march with their shields a-thunder
And howl as the night-wolves do.
The moon swings high in her season,
To shine on a sea of pearls
That croons, with a summer breeze on
Its bosom, the songs of girls—
Songs dear to the hearts that cherish
A passion that thralls and numbs—
Night goes, and the moon-songs perish,
Yet never a Night-Wave comes.
But when in the deep Pelorus,
The tides thro’ the darkness surge,
They march to a moaning chorus—
A song that is half a dirge.
Red-lipped are the waves foretelling
The march of the ranks of Day ;
And warm are the Noon Waves swelling
To burst on the reefs in spray ;
But fierce, where the channel blackens,
The hosts of the Night- Waves go,
And never their quickstep slackens,
And never a wave swings slow.
M.L.
QUILP N.
Hardy old Ca.pt. William Jackson Barry
(now 82) has induced M.L. Govt, to undertake
the publication of his comprehensive book dealing
with the history of the Australian colonies. The
captain arrived in Sydney in 1829, and is still
battling for a crust.
Baden-Powell never had a more ardent
adorer than Melb. Argus. There was a time
when that paper constantly contained evidence to
support this writer’s belief that the staff prostrated
itself twice a day before a pottery bust of the
Hero of Mafeking which adorned Editor Wil-
loughby’s table. Yet this is how the Akgus was
forced to talk of its fallen god at a late date :
Another South African celebrity who has attracted un-
commonly little attention is General Baden-Powell ....
the official world ignores him. The fact is that “ 8.P.” is
rather out of favor with the authorities. If my infor-
mation is correct, “ 8.P.” is not likely to obtain any high
command in the immediate future, or if he does it will
not be in South Africa.
Poor jumped-up “8.P.” is like the battered top-
hat that reminds a man of the night he made a
braying ass of himself.
The following books worth reading about
the South African War will be posted direct
from London to any Australasian address
(with delivery in about 11 weeks), on re-
ceipt by The Bulletin of order with
stamps or postal note for prices annexed. The
African cables about the war are notoriously un-
trustworthy, and the information which reaches
this country is poisoned at the source in the
interests of the syndicates and politicians who
made the war. Every Australian should strive to
understand the real facts of the Boer struggle
for liberty. The books recommended are:—
J. A. Hobson’s “Psychology of Jingoism,” 3s. (a
statement of what lies beneath the surface
of the Imperialistic idea and its manifestations);
J. A. Hobson’s “The War in South Africa,
its Causes and Effects,” 3s. (a clear state-
ment, of the disgraceful manner in which the
British Government and nation were deluded);
Methuen’s ‘ ‘ Peace or War in South Africa,” Is. 6d.
(a handbook of parties and events in S’Africa,
showing the true facts of the present situation);
H. J. Ogden’s “The War against the Dutch
Republics,” Is. 6d. (a sort of unofficial Blue-book
of the history of the war and its controversies).
The four books will be sent for Bs. 6d. to any
Australasian address, direct from London.
An Arbitration Case.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little settlement of Wenlock Wells—out nor’-
west, beyond civilisation—-was represented, in the
white, by Michael O’Shannassy, deserter. The
other inhabitants were a tribe of aborigines, who
had wandered there from the interior.
“ Take these things,” said Michael to the king,
“ and appear in state, as you ought to ; ” and he
handed him a pair of seamen’s india-rubber knee-
boots, an oilskin sou’-wester, and a telescope with-
out lenses ; “and this,” he added, throwing in a
map of the city of Dublin; “it is the chart of
magic ! In return, keep me in food, and I will
take Yaba, your daughter, to wife.”
“It is agreed ! ” said the king.
“With these,” said Michael to Yaba, pointing
to a barrel of rum and an armful of furnishings,
stowed in the humpy, “ we shall do.”
In course of time, Yaba bore him a son, whom
they called Moola. A handful of white people,
too, found their way to the settlement; among
them, Bridget O’Mahony and Father Kildane,
and Michael became friendly with Bridget. “A
man should keep to his own color,” he solilo-
quised.
“The place,” muttered the priest, “would not
yield me a glass of potheen, let alone a church ; I
will return.” But, before doing so, he married
Michael and Bridget.
“Return now to your father, the king” said
Michael to Yaba, “and take the boy witn you;
otherwise, if Bridget be the woman I take her
for, she will make it hot for you.”
That night, at the council, the king appeared
in state, and issued his decree.
“ This affront on my family,” he cried, “ cannot
be forgiven ! Let Michael, the white man, take
back Yaba, or be clubbed ! Take him when he
carries not his weapon of fire.”
After that Michael carried his gun. But six
months made him forgetful, and when he was
chased one day by the tribe, the rum asserted
itself at an unlucky moment, and he fell dead
beneath a tree. . . . And there he was buried.
“ It was a trick to cheat us,” the king declared.
“His spirit but waits to be born again! Entice
the woman, Bridget, to pass by the tree, so that
his spirit may enter into her ! ”
. . . “Lie aisy thin, if ye can,” sobbed Bridget,
as she concluded her lamentations over the spot
the next day, “ for ye wanted the praist badly !”
“ It is well,” said the king ; “ look-out—we will
have him yet!”
It was with Bridget in the manner of women,
and in a few months she bore a son.
‘ ‘ What did I say ?” yelled the king. “ ’Tis he !
he is back again, disguised as an infant! The
hair of flame, and the one eye that seeks the other!
Justice shall yet be done ! Sorry shall he be that
he kept not Yaba when she was young and
lovely !”
The course of 20 years had brought a few more
white stragglers to the settlement, and a publican’s
license for Mrs. O’Shannassy.
The king scoured the country with his telescope,
and consulted the chart. “Time is up!” he de-
clared. “ Yaba shall be kept in suspense no
longer, and Moola, who is troublesome with the
young gins, requires a father’s club. Let Michael
now make his choice !”
The place was in an uproar! What would
young Michael choose ?
TheO’Shannassys tried compromise—sustenance
for Yaba, her person to remain sacred. Yaba
herself refused it —indignantly, and in tears.
“There’s only one thing,” shouted Harry the
Bull-Whacker (once in a eattle-stealing case) —
“ Harbitration —and I’ll be Judge ! ”
When the idea was understood, it was agreed -
to, and the place swelled with importance.
“ A pint of rum,” said Mrs. O’Shannassy, aside ;
“ two if we win, Judge darlin’! ”
“Right!” said Harry.
“A roast wallaby—killed but a week” —
suggested the king.'
“ No ! ” said Harry.
“Any gin in the tribe, bar Yaba?”
“ No ! ” shouted Harry.
“The right to club old Nulla, my wife’s
mother,” pleaded the king.
“No! ye murderin’ devil!” roared Harry.
“ What about rum ? ” he whispered.
“Baal rum,” said the king, sadly.
“Be off with ye!” yelled Harry; “would ye
dare to bribe a Judge ? ”
The Court and the settlement disposed itself for
the hearing upon fallen logs in a neighboring
hollow.
“Bosun Bill” —the one-time pearler—rolled to
his seat, with a half-emptied bottle in his pocket.
“ For the defence ! ” he shouted.
Feeling ran high. As points were made, on
one side or the other, they were greeted with par-
tisan cheers.
At an impressive moment a young gin shrieked.
“Remove the youth, Moola ! ” commanded the
Judge.
“If there ain’t order,” he thundered (rising),
“while I gives the verdict, I’ll . . .” (And Tie
mentioned it. It secured silence.) “I’ve give
great consideration to what was put forward by
the king,” he roared, “and the oncommon like-
ness between the two men is pretty nigh good
enough to settle young Michael! On t’other hand,
Bosun Bill is pretty nigh as strong. What’s his
argyment ? * Twins they are, ’he says, ‘by differ-
ent mothers ! ’ Mind ye, there’s much in that.
‘Likewise,’ says he, ‘if Michael had meant to
come back agin, he warn’t the man to fall into
the trap the king laid fr’im—he’d a chose a differ-
ent woman for to throw ’em off the scent! ’ And
that’s weighty, too, that’s what it is—weighty!
But I remimbers one thing what proves to me
that this young Michael ain’t the man what was
buried. ’Tis this : t’other Michael had his squint
in the right eye, while this ’ere young un’s in the
left! The verdict is for the O’Shannassys ! ”
Thus the feud was settled; and Harry the
Bull-Whacker reigned long and respected in
Wenlock Wells.
G. M. TRAIN.
The notion that Queen Victoria’s eldest-
born was a really brainy woman is well sustained
by the observations attributed to her by the
German authoress, Madame Von Preuschen :
“ What is the good of talent to me, or genius, or
ardent longings, endeavors, and strivings ? Have I even
the time, have I strength enough, with all.these thousand'
empty, hateful duties of etiquette, which kill the human
soul and nourish the propriety puppet in us ? And even
if I did accomplish anything great in art, would anyone
believe in it ? Would it not be always thought—as with
all art work done by crowned heads, from Nero down-
wards—that it was not done by mine own hands ? Oh,
what a curse rests on us Royalties—what a'curse for
those who would like to be human ! ”
“ Who would like to be human!” The pathetic
aspiration of a woman who was evidently very
human !
